First Argument: Angelic Messengers Are More Effective
Author defends angelic messengers as more effective.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first argument for why prophets must be angels rather than humans, including the distinction they bear and the refutation of common objections.

Core Idea
Angels can appear human yet be distinguished; God's wisdom chooses the more effective way.

This passage presents and defends the first argument for angelic messengers. The author anticipates that people might object that angels are unseen or harsh, but he shows that they can appear in human form without needing physical needs, and they obey God's command for gentleness. He concludes that sending an angel is more conducive to the divine purpose.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the source Arabic once, then its literal translation. Notice the author's style of presenting an argument and responding to objections.
The passage includes three objections marked by 'As for his second/third statement'.
2
Identify the main claim and distinguishing feature
The author argues that an angel can appear as a human. The key distinction is that the angel does not need to eat, drink, clothe, or marry. Why is this distinction important?
It provides a visible difference without causing fear.
3
Analyze the three counterarguments and responses
The author addresses three objections: angels are harsh, humans prefer their own kind, and the angle of fear. For each, note how he refutes it using divine command or divine causation.
The response to the 'species inclination' relies on Quran 8:63.
4
Understand the final wisdom principle
The author concludes with a general principle: if two ways exist, the wiser chooses the more effective one. This implies that God, being wise, must choose the angelic messenger.
This is a key rational argument supporting the choice of angels.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم نقول : لم لا يجوز أن يقال : إن الناس يشاهدون ذلك الملك في صورة البشر (۱) ؟ ويكون الفرق بينه وبين سائر البشر : أن لا يحتاج إلى الأكل والشرب والمليوس والمنكوح ، وهذا القدر من التفاوت لا يوجب الخوف الشديد ، ويحصل الامتياز بينه وبين سائر الناس . وأما قوله ثانيا الملائكة لهم قهـر شـديـد فهم لا يسامحون البشر . قلنا : الملائكة لا يعصون الله فيما يأمرهم ، فإذا أمرهم بالرفق لم يفعلوا شيئاً من التشديد . وأما قوله ثالثاً : الجنس إلى الجنس أميل . فنقول : حصول الميل في القلب ، ليس إلا من الله تعالى، كما قال تعالى : ( ولكن الله ألف بينهم فيثبت بما ذكرنا : أن إرسال الرسول من زمرة الملائكة ، أفضى إلى المقصود ونقول : لو كانت الرسالة جائزة ، لوجب أن يكون ذلك الرسول من الملائكة [ لأن الحكيم (۳) ] إذا أراد تحصيل مطلوب ، وكان له إليه طريقان ، وكان أحد الطريقين أفضى إلى حصول ذلك المطلوب من الطريق الثاني ، فإنه يجب عليه بمقتضى حكمته ، أن يرجح الطريق الأفضل الأكمل
Then we say: Why is it not permissible to say that people see that angel in the form of a human? And the difference between him and other humans is that he does not need eating, drinking, clothing, or marriage. This degree of difference does not necessitate intense fear, and distinction between him and other people is achieved. As for his second statement: 'Angels have severe domination; they do not show leniency to humans,' we say: Angels do not disobey God in what He commands them; if He commands them to be gentle, they do nothing of severity. As for his third statement: 'The species is more inclined to the species,' we say: The occurrence of inclination in the heart is only from God Almighty, as He said: 'But God united them.' Thus it is established by what we have mentioned that sending a messenger from the group of angels is more conducive to the purpose. And we say: If messengerhood were permissible, that messenger would have to be from the angels, because the Wise One, when He wants to achieve a desired end and has two ways to it, and one of the two ways is more conducive to achieving that desired end than the other, then He must, by virtue of His wisdom, prefer the better, more complete way.
Source pages p. 84

Commentary
This passage presents and defends the first argument for why messengers must be angels rather than humans. The author begins by raising a plausible objection: why can't people simply see the angel in human form? The answer is that the angel would be distinguished from ordinary humans by not needing food, drink, clothing, or marriage—a difference sufficient to establish distinction without causing excessive fear. Then he addresses three counterarguments. First, the claim that angels are harsh and unforgiving: the author replies that angels only do what God commands; if God commands gentleness, they will be gentle. Second, the claim that humans are more inclined toward their own kind: the author replies that inclination in the heart is created by God, citing Quran 8:63. He concludes that sending an angelic messenger is more conducive to the purpose. Finally, he adds a general principle: if both an angelic and a human messenger were possible, the wiser choice would be the more effective one, and since the angelic messenger is more effective, it is obligatory by God's wisdom to choose the angel.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that angels are invisible and thus less effective as messengers because people cannot see them.
Correction: The author argues that angels can appear in human form, and they are distinguished by not needing physical necessities, making them recognizable without causing fear.

Checkpoint
According to the first argument, what distinguishes an angelic messenger from ordinary humans?
A) The angel has wings and a halo.
B) The angel does not need to eat, drink, clothe himself, or marry.
C) The angel can fly.
D) The angel never sleeps.

Key Takeaway
Angels are more effective messengers because they can appear as humans yet are clearly distinguished, and they perfectly obey God's command, making them the wiser choice.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a choice between two paths, do you naturally assume God's way is the most effective? Or do you secretly trust your own planning more? What would it take for you to fully surrender a specific decision to His wisdom today?
?
The angels obey God's command to be gentle without hesitation. In which relationship or responsibility do you resist God's gentle command because you think severity will be more effective? How can you let go of that resistance right now?
?
If you truly believed that God chooses the better way for you even when it seems harder, what would you change about your attitude toward a difficult situation you are currently facing? Write down one concrete action you will take this week to trust His choice over yours.

Prophethood

Why the Perfect Teacher Must Be an Angel
Author argues that humans are imperfect and need a perfect teacher, who must be an angel free from desire and anger.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the author argues that the perfect teacher for humanity must be an angel, free from desire and anger, rather than a human.

Core Idea
Human imperfection requires a perfect teacher; only angels are free from desire and anger.

This lesson examines the second argument for prophethood: since humans are deficient in knowing and doing good, they need a perfect teacher. The author argues that this teacher cannot be human because humans are inherently subject to desire and anger, which are sources of deficiency. Instead, angels, who are free from these, are the most suitable teachers.

Guided Path
1
Read the argument carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Pay attention to the definition of human perfection and the observation of deficiency.
The passage is self-contained; it begins with defining perfection and ends with the conclusion about angels.
2
Identify the key components
Look for: (1) definition of human perfection, (2) observation that humans are deficient, (3) need for a perfect teacher, (4) condition that teacher must be free from deficiency, (5) infinite regress argument, (6) human's inevitable desire and anger, (7) angelic sanctity from these, (8) conclusion.
3
Understand the infinite regress objection
If the teacher were also deficient, he would need another teacher, and so on without end. This shows the teacher must be perfect in the relevant sense.
This is a common argument in kalam to establish a first cause or perfect being.
4
Contrast human and angelic qualities
The author says humans cannot be free from desire and anger, while angels are sanctified and absorbed in divine knowledge. This contrast supports the conclusion.
Notice that the author mentions imagination and illusion are absent in angels, implying they are free from error.
5
Reflect on the conclusion
The argument concludes that sending angels as teachers is 'more appropriate' (أولى). Think about why this argument is used to support prophethood.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه الثاني في تقرير هذه الشبهة : إن كمال الإنسان في أن يعرف الحق لذاته ، والخير لأجل العمل به . ونرى البشر ناقصين في هذين الوصفين فاحتاجوا إلى شخص يكون كاملاً فيهما ، حتى يصير ذلك الكامل مكملا للناقصين ، وذلك المكمل يجب أن يكون مبرا عن النقصان في هذين الوصفين ، وإلا لافتقر إلى مكمل آخر ، ولزم التسلسل ، وكل من كان بشراً فإنه لا ينفك عن الشهوة والغضب ، وبسبب حصول هذين الوصفين (٤) يكون النقصان أما الملائكة فهم مقدسون عن الشهوة والغضب ، والخيال حاصلا فيهم والوهم ، مستغرقون في المعارف الإلهية مواظبون على الطاعات والعبادات فكانت بعثتهم لأجل تكميل الناقصين أولى .
The second aspect in presenting this doubt: The perfection of man consists in knowing truth for its own sake and good for the sake of acting upon it. And we see humans deficient in these two descriptions, so they need a person who is perfect in them, so that this perfect one may perfect the deficient. That perfecter must be free from deficiency in these two descriptions; otherwise he would need another perfecter, leading to infinite regress. And whoever is human is not free from desire and anger; due to the presence of these two descriptions, deficiency occurs. As for angels, they are sanctified from desire and anger; imagination and illusion are not present in them; they are absorbed in divine knowledge, persistent in acts of obedience and worship. So their being sent to perfect the deficient is more appropriate.
Source pages p. 84

Commentary
This passage presents the second argument. It begins by defining human perfection as knowing truth for its own sake and doing good for the sake of acting upon it. Humans are seen as deficient in both, so they require a perfect teacher to complete them. That teacher must be free from the very deficiencies he is meant to remedy; otherwise he would need another teacher, leading to an infinite regress. The author then argues that every human is inevitably subject to desire and anger, which are sources of deficiency. Angels, by contrast, are sanctified from desire and anger; they are free from imagination and illusion (which cause error), absorbed in divine knowledge, and constant in worship. Therefore, sending angels to perfect deficient humans is more appropriate.

Key Terms
كمال
نقصان
شهوة
غضب
تسلسل
مكمل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that human prophets are perfect enough to teach others.
Correction: The author argues that humans inevitably have desire and anger, which are imperfections, so a human teacher would himself be deficient and require another teacher, leading to infinite regress.

Checkpoint
Why does the author argue that the perfecter cannot be human?
A) Humans are too weak.
B) Humans have desire and anger, which are deficiencies.
C) Humans cannot learn divine knowledge.
D) Humans are not trustworthy.

Key Takeaway
The perfect teacher must be free from human limitations, and angels uniquely possess that freedom.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The lesson says humans are deficient in knowing truth and doing good. Where do you see your own deficiency most clearly—in your understanding of truth or in your actions? What specific step will you take today to seek guidance from the Perfect Teacher rather than relying on your own desire and anger?
?
You claim to follow a prophet, but the argument here says even the best human teacher is not free from desire and anger. How does that truth challenge your trust in human leaders? Are you willing to submit your desires and anger to the teaching of the angelic revelation, even when it contradicts your personal inclinations?
?
The infinite regress argument shows that only a being free from deficiency can perfect you. Have you ever tried to perfect yourself through human teachers alone, and how did that fail? What would change in your life if you truly accepted that only the angelic message can complete you?

Third Argument: Angels as Direct Messengers
Author argues that since prophets receive from angels, sending an angel directly is superior.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third argument that angels are superior to human prophets because prophets themselves receive from angels, and how this is supported by Quranic verses.

Core Idea
Prophets learn from angels, so angels are higher.

In this passage, the author presents the third argument from those who hold that angels are superior to human prophets. He begins with the premise that prophets themselves acknowledge that they derive their perfections from angels, and then he supports this with multiple Quranic verses. The conclusion is that sending angels directly to creation is more perfect than sending human prophets.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the literal translation. Let the author's flow come through.
2
Identify the main premise
The passage opens by stating that prophets acknowledge they receive their perfections from angels. This is the key premise.
The premise is stated in the first sentence.
3
Examine the Quranic citations
Six Quranic verses are cited. Read each one and see how they support the premise that prophets receive from angels.
Notice the variety: some refer to the Quran's revelation, others to support with angels.
4
Understand the conclusion
After the evidence, the author concludes that sending an angel is 'more fitting and more perfect' than sending a human prophet.
5
Reflect on the misconception
Consider the common misunderstanding that human prophets are superior, and see how this argument challenges that view.
The passage uses the prophets' own admission to argue for angelic superiority.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه الثالث : إن الأنبياء - عليهم السلام - مقرون بأنهم إنما يستفيدون [ ما لهم (۱) ] من الكمالات من الملائكة . قال تعالى : و نزل به الروح الأمين . على قلبك (٢) ، وقال [ في صفة القرآن (۳) ] ( إنه لقول رسول كريم (٤) ، وقال : علمه شديد القوى (٥) ، وقال : ( وأيدناه بروح القدس (۱) وقال : « يمددكم ربكم بخمسة آلاف من الملائكة مسومين (۷) ، وقال : « فتمثل لها بشراً سويا (۸) ، فلما ثبت باتفاق الأنبياء - عليهم السلام - أنهم إنما يستفيدون ما لهم الكمالات ، من الملائكة السماوية ، والأرواح الفلكية كان إرسال الملك إلى الخلق : أولى وأكمل . فهذا تقرير كلام هذه الطائفة [ والله أعلم ) ) .
The third aspect: The prophets—peace be upon them—acknowledge that they only derive their perfections from the angels. God said: 'The Trustworthy Spirit descended with it upon your heart' (26:193-194); and He said describing the Quran: 'It is the word of a noble messenger' (81:19); and He said: 'Taught by one of severe powers' (53:5); and He said: 'And We supported him with the Holy Spirit' (2:87); and He said: 'Your Lord will reinforce you with five thousand angels marked' (3:125); and He said: 'Then she took a veil apart from them, and We sent to her Our spirit, and it appeared to her as a perfect human' (19:17). So when it is established by the consensus of the prophets—peace be upon them—that they derive their perfections only from the celestial angels and the animating spirits, then sending an angel to creation is more fitting and more perfect. This is the presentation of the words of this group—and God knows best.
Source pages p. 85

Commentary
This passage presents the third argument. It begins by stating a fact: the prophets themselves acknowledge that they derive their perfections from angels. Then the author cites multiple Quranic verses to support this: 26:193-194 (the Trustworthy Spirit descended with the Quran on the Prophet's heart), 81:19 (the Quran is the word of a noble messenger, i.e., Gabriel), 53:5 (taught by one of severe powers), 2:87 (supported by the Holy Spirit), 3:125 (God reinforces believers with angels), and 19:17 (the spirit appeared as a human to Mary). The argument is that if prophets, who are the best of humans, receive their knowledge and perfection from angels, then it is more fitting and more perfect to send the angels directly to the people rather than sending human prophets who are themselves dependent on angels. The passage concludes that this is the position of the group being argued for, and God knows best.

Key Terms
أنبياء
ملائكة
الروح الأمين
رسول كريم
الكمالات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Human prophets are inherently superior to angels.
Correction: The argument uses the prophets' own admission of receiving from angels to contend that angels are actually higher in rank.

Checkpoint
What is the main premise of the third argument?
A) Angels are stronger than humans.
B) Prophets themselves derive their perfections from angels.
C) Humans cannot see angels.
D) Angels have wings.

Key Takeaway
This argument uses the prophets' own dependence on angels to establish the superiority of angelic messengers.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If the prophets themselves admitted they receive their perfections from angels, what does that reveal about your own need for guidance? Do you seek knowledge from those God has appointed as intermediaries, or do you rely on your own intellect?
?
The prophets acknowledged their dependence on angels. In what areas of your life are you trying to be self-sufficient instead of relying on God's appointed means of guidance?
?
Do you view angels as merely distant beings, or do you realize they are actively involved in transmitting divine knowledge? How does that change your attitude towards seeking sacred knowledge and those who convey it?

The Third Aspect: Prophets Receive Perfections from Angels
Razi presents the argument that prophets rely on angels for their perfections.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's argument that prophets rely on angels for their perfections and how this supports the necessity of angelic mediation.

Core Idea
Prophets themselves confirm that their perfections come from angels.

This lesson examines Razi's third proof for angelic mediation. Drawing on a consensus among prophets and numerous Quranic verses, Razi argues that prophets receive their perfections from angels. Understanding this argument is key to grasping the role of angels as intermediaries in divine communication.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Begin by reading the Arabic passage and its literal translation. Let the words of Razi and the Quranic verses settle in your mind before analyzing.
Note that the passage cites six verses; count them as you read.
2
Identify the core premise
Razi starts with the premise that prophets themselves agree they derive their perfections from angels. Why is this a strong starting point?
This premise comes from the consensus of prophets (peace be upon them).
3
Examine the Quranic evidence
List the six verses Razi quotes. For each, note how it shows a prophet or the Prophet receiving something from an angel. Consider the variety of contexts: revelation, support, reinforcement.
The verses cover different prophets and different types of angelic assistance.
4
Understand the conclusion
After establishing the premise and supporting it with verses, Razi draws his conclusion: sending an angel to creation is more appropriate (awlā) and more perfect (akmal). What does this imply about the necessity of angels?
This conclusion directly supports the main argument for angelic mediation.
5
Reflect on the significance
Consider why Razi needed this argument. How does it fit into the broader defense of angelic mediation? Reflect on the importance of prophetic consensus as a source of theological proof.
This argument shows that even the highest human beings need angels.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه الثالث : إن الأنبياء - عليهم السلام - مقرون بأنهم إنما يستفيدون ما لهم من الكمالات من الملائكة . قال تعالى : و نزل به الروح الأمين . على قلبك ، وقال في صفة القرآن : إنه لقول رسول كريم ، وقال : علمه شديد القوى ، وقال : وأيدناه بروح القدس ، وقال : يمددكم ربكم بخمسة آلاف من الملائكة مسومين ، وقال : فتمثل لها بشراً سويا . فلما ثبت باتفاق الأنبياء - عليهم السلام - أنهم إنما يستفيدون ما لهم الكمالات من الملائكة السماوية والأرواح الفلكية كان إرسال الملك إلى الخلق أولى وأكمل . فهذا تقرير كلام هذه الطائفة والله أعلم.
The third aspect: The prophets, peace be upon them, are agreed that they only derive the perfections they have from the angels. He, the Exalted, said: 'The Trustworthy Spirit has brought it down upon your heart,' and He said in describing the Qur'an: 'Indeed, it is the word of a noble messenger,' and He said: 'Taught by one severe in strength,' and He said: 'And We supported him with the Holy Spirit,' and He said: 'Your Lord will reinforce you with five thousand angels bearing marks,' and He said: 'Then she screened herself from them, and We sent to her Our Spirit, and he appeared to her as a well-proportioned man.' So when it is established by the agreement of the prophets, peace be upon them, that they only derive the perfections they have from the celestial angels and the celestial spirits, then sending the angel to the creation is more appropriate and more perfect. This is the statement of this group, and God knows best.
Source pages p. 85

Commentary
This passage presents a proof (the third aspect) in favor of angelic mediation. It begins by stating a consensus: the prophets agree that they obtain their perfections from angels. Razi then cites six Quranic verses to support this. Verses: (1) 'The Trustworthy Spirit' (Jibril) brought down the revelation. (2) The Qur'an is described as the word of a noble messenger (the angel). (3) The Prophet was taught by one severe in strength (the angel). (4) Jesus was supported by the Holy Spirit (Jibril). (5) The believers are reinforced with angels. (6) The angel appeared to Mary as a man. From this consensus, Razi concludes that sending an angel to humankind is better (awlā) and more perfect (akmal) than sending revelation directly without an intermediary. This conclusion supports the general argument that angels are necessary as intermediaries between God and prophets.

Key Terms
الأنبياء
الملائكة
الكمالات
إرسال الملك
الأرواح الفلكية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the main conclusion drawn from the fact that prophets agree they receive perfections from angels?
A) Prophets do not need angels for their knowledge.
B) Sending an angel to creation is more appropriate and perfect.
C) Angels are superior to prophets.
D) The Qur'an was revealed directly without angels.

Key Takeaway
Prophets derive their perfections from angels, making angelic mediation a necessary and superior channel for divine communication.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly believe that you cannot attain any spiritual perfection without divine means? The prophets themselves admitted their dependence on angels. Where is your pride in seeking knowledge or worship without acknowledging Allah's appointed intermediaries? Commit today to thanking Allah for the means He provides.
?
When you recite the Qur'an, do you remember that it came through the Trustworthy Spirit? How would your recitation change if you constantly felt the presence of the angel who brought it? Will you pause before reading to reflect on this chain of transmission?
?
The prophets were supported by angels in their struggles. You too can seek angelic support by obeying Allah and following the Sunnah. What is one specific action you will take this week to invite the angels' company, based on what you have learned?

How the Prophet Knows He Is a Prophet: The Critics' Objection
Razi presents the critics' objection that the prophet cannot know his own prophethood.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the epistemological challenge raised by critics against the prophet's own knowledge of his prophethood, and the reasoning behind their objection.

Core Idea
The prophet faces the same problem as the community: he needs a miracle to verify the angel, but he doesn't know what is normal for angels.

In this passage, Fakhr al-Din al-Razi presents a sophisticated objection from critics of miracles. They acknowledge that the community can recognize a prophet through miracles, but argue that the prophet himself cannot be certain that the angel who brings revelation is truly from God, not a devil. This objection forces us to consider the epistemological basis of prophethood itself.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own argumentative flow come through before interpreting.
Notice the rhetorical structure: the critics present a dilemma.
2
Identify the two possibilities for the prophet's knowledge
The passage presents two ways the prophet might know his prophethood: direct divine address or angelic mediation. Find where these are stated.
3
Understand why direct address is dismissed
The critics argue that direct address is unlikely and that most prophets agree they received revelation through angels.
This leaves only the angelic mediation possibility.
4
See the parallel between community and prophet
Just as the community needs miracles to distinguish a true prophet from a false one, the prophet needs a miracle to distinguish a true angel from a devil.
This is a key move in the objection.
5
Grasp the core problem: unknown angelic customs
The prophet does not know what is customary in the angelic world, so any miracle the angel performs might be considered normal. Thus, no miracle can prove the angel's authenticity.
The passage ends before the response to this objection.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل الثالث البحث عن الطريق الذي يعرف الرسول كونه رسولا من عند الله عز وجل. قال الطاعنون في المعجزات : هب أن الأمة يعرفون بواسطة المعجزات كون الرسول إنساناً أميناً صادقاً في دعواه . أما الرسول فكيف يعرف كونه رسولا ؟ وذلك لأن ثبوت رسالته إما أن يكون لأجل أن الله تعالى يرفع الواسطة من البين ويقول أيها العبد أنت رسولي إلى الخلق وإما أن يكون لأجل أن الله تعالى يرسل إليه ملكاً ويقول له ذلك الملك أنت رسول الله تعالى إلى الخلق. أما القسم الأول فبعيد . وأكثر الأنبياء مطبقون على أنهم إنما جاءتهم الرسالة من عند الله بواسطة الملك . فيبقى القسم الثاني فنقول كما أن الأمة مفتقرون في التمييز بين المدعي المحق وبين المدعي المبطل إلى الحجة فكذلك الرسول لا يمكنه التمييز بين الملك المعصوم وبين الشيطان المرجوم إلا بالمعجز . لكن لا سبيل إلى هذا المعجز . وذلك لأن الرسول البشري لا يعرف ما يوافق العادة في عالم الملائكة وما يخالف العادة هناك . فكل معجز يأتي به الملك في تقرير أنه محق فإن الرسول البشري يجوز أن يكون ذلك أمراً موافقاً للعادة في عالم الملائكة . وبهذا التقدير فإن ذلك الملك لا يقدر على تقرير الحجة على كونه ملكاً معصوماً . فإن قالوا إنه إذا أتى الملك بفعل خاص ثبت بالدليل أنه لا يقدر على إحداثه أحد إلا الله سبحانه وتعالى فحينئذ يعرف
Chapter Three: Investigation concerning the way by which the messenger knows that he is a messenger from God Almighty. The critics of miracles said: Suppose that the community knows, by means of miracles, that the messenger is a trustworthy, truthful human being in his claim. As for the messenger, how does he know that he is a messenger? That is because the establishment of his messengership is either that God Most High removes the intermediary and says: 'O servant, you are My messenger to the creation,' or that God Most High sends an angel to him and that angel says: 'You are the messenger of God Most High to the creation.' As for the first division, it is unlikely. The majority of prophets are agreed that the message came to them from God only through the angel. So the second division remains. Then we say: Just as the community needs evidence in distinguishing between the truthful claimant and the false claimant, so too the messenger cannot distinguish between the protected angel and the accursed devil except by a miracle. But there is no way to this miracle. This is because the human messenger does not know what is customary in the world of angels and what is contrary to custom there. So every miracle that the angel brings in establishing that he is truthful, the human messenger may consider that as something customary in the world of angels. And on this assumption, that angel cannot establish evidence that he is a protected angel. If they say: If the angel brings a particular action, and it has been established by proof that no one can produce it except God Most High, then he would know...
Source pages p. 86

Commentary
This passage is a presentation of an objection (from 'the critics of miracles') regarding how a prophet knows he is truly a prophet. The objection proceeds in steps: (1) The community recognizes the prophet through miracles, but the prophet himself faces a difficulty. (2) The prophet's knowledge of his prophethood could come either directly from God without an intermediary, or via an angel. (3) The first option (direct address) is dismissed as unlikely, and most prophets agree they received revelation through angels. (4) So the only remaining option is the angelic mediation. (5) But then the prophet faces the same problem as the community: he needs a miracle to distinguish a true angel from a devil. (6) However, this miracle itself is problematic because the prophet does not know what is customary in the angelic realm, so any act the angel performs might be considered normal for angels. (7) The passage ends mid-sentence as the objectors anticipate a possible response: if the angel performs an act that only God can do, then the prophet would know. But the full refutation of this response is not provided in the available text.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Prophets have immediate, infallible knowledge of their own prophethood without any need for verification.
Correction: The critics argue that even prophets face an epistemological challenge: they must distinguish a true angel from a devil, and without knowing angelic customs, no miracle can provide certainty.

Checkpoint
According to the critics, why can't the prophet rely on a miracle from the angel to confirm his prophethood?
A) Angels cannot perform miracles.
B) The prophet already has direct knowledge from God.
C) The prophet does not know what is customary in the angelic world, so any act might be normal.
D) The devil cannot perform any acts.

Key Takeaway
The critics argue that the prophet cannot be certain of his own prophethood because he lacks knowledge of angelic customs, making any miracle from an angel potentially ordinary.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If a prophet himself could struggle to be certain of his revelation because he doesn't know what is normal for angels, how certain are you that the spiritual experiences or feelings you attribute to God are truly from Him? What would it take for you to be as certain as a prophet must be?
?
The critics argue that verification requires evidence beyond the experience itself. In your own life, what external signs has God provided to confirm your faith in prophethood? Are you actively looking for those signs, or have you assumed certainty without warrant?
?
This passage shows that even the most direct encounter with a supernatural being can be epistemologically ambiguous. Do you treat your beliefs about Islam as resting on solid evidence, or have you relied on comfort or tradition? What would it mean for you to seek genuine certainty, even if it requires hard work?

How Does a Prophet Know He Is a Prophet?
Razi's dialectical argument on prophetic self-knowledge and the limits of miracles.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that a prophet cannot know his own prophethood through miracles and the theological resolution that God grants necessary self-evident knowledge.

Core Idea
A prophet cannot rely on miracles alone to know he is a prophet; God must grant him direct, necessary knowledge.

This passage tackles a crucial epistemological question: how does a prophet himself know that he is truly a prophet? While miracles can convince the public, the prophet faces a unique problem—distinguishing a genuine angel from a deceptive devil. The author presents and refutes several objections, ultimately arguing that God must create necessary knowledge in the prophet.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the full translation. Let the argument's flow register before you dissect it.
Notice the dialectical structure: objection, response, counter-response.
2
Identify the objection and its two possibilities
The objectors grant that miracles work for the nation but ask how the prophet himself knows. They outline two paths: direct divine announcement or angelic intermediary. Observe how the first is dismissed.
The author notes that most prophets agree on the angelic intermediary.
3
Understand the core problem: angel vs. devil
Without a miracle, the prophet cannot distinguish an infallible angel from an accursed devil. But even a miracle is problematic because the prophet does not know what is customary in the angelic realm.
This is the central epistemological gap.
4
Examine the counter-argument and its refutation
The objectors suggest that an act only God can produce would prove the angel's truthfulness. The response: it could be a customary answer to prayer in the angelic world, so it is not decisive.
The response hinges on the possibility of divine custom in angelic affairs.
5
Grasp the conclusion: necessary knowledge from God
The only solution is that God creates necessary, self-evident knowledge (ʿilm ḍarūrī) in the prophet, making him certain of the angel's truthfulness.
This concludes that prophetic certainty is a divine gift, not a rational deduction.

الفصل الثالث
البحث عن الطريق الذي يعرف الرسول كونه رسولا من عند الله عز وجل

قال الطاعنون في المعجزات : هب أن الأمة يعرفون بواسطة المعجزات كون الرسول إنساناً أميناً صادقاً في دعواه . أما الرسول فكيف يعرف كونه رسولاً ؟ وذلك لأن ثبوت رسالته ، إما أن يكون لأجل أن الله تعالى يرفع الواسطة من البين . ويقول: أيها العبد أنت رسولي إلى الخلق ، وإما أن يكون لأجل أن الله تعالى يرسل إليه مَلَكاً ، ويقول له ذلك الملك : أنت رسول الله تعالى إلى الخلق .

أما القسم الأول : فبعيد . وأكثر الأنبياء مطبقون على أنهم إنما جاءتهم الرسالة من عند الله بواسطة الملك . فيبقى القسم الثاني ، فنقول : كما أن الأمة مفتقرون في التمييز بين المدعي المحق ، وبين المدعي المبطل إلى الحجة ، فكذلك الرسول لا يمكنه التمييز بين الملك المعصوم ، وبين الشيطان المرجوم. إلا بالمعجز . لكن لا سبيل إلى هذا المعجز . وذلك لأن الرسول البشري لا يعرف ما يوافق العادة، في عالم الملائكة ، وما يخالف العادة هناك . فكل معجز يأتي به الملك في تقرير أنه محق ، فإن الرسول البشري يجوز أن يكون ذلك أمراً موافقاً للعادة في عالم الملائكة . وبهذا التقدير فإن ذلك الملك لا يقدر على تقرير الحجة على كونه ملكاً معصوماً . فإن قالوا : إنه إذا أتى الملك بفعل خاص ، ثبت بالدليل أنه لا يقدر على إحداثه أحد إلا الله سبحانه وتعالى ـ فحينئذ يعرف النبي كونه ملكاً معصوماً لا شيطاناً مرجوماً . فنقول : هذا أيضاً لا يفيد وذلك لأن ذلك الفعل ، وإن كان فعلا لا يقدر أحد على إيجاده إلا الله تعالى ، إلا أنه لا يمتنع في العقل أنه - تعالى - أجرى العادة في عالم الملائكة بأن كل من دعا الله ، وتضرع إليه في طلب ذلك الفعل ، فإنه تعالى يفعله إجابة لدعاء ذلك الداعي، وإذا كان هذا المعنى محتملاً ، لم يكن ظهوره على يد هذا الملك : دليلاً على كونه صادق اللهجة في ادعاء الرسالة فيثبت أن الملك لا يمكنه تعريف الرسول البشري كونه صادق القول في ادعاء كونه رسولاً من عند الله إلى ذلك الإنسان . بقي أن يقال : إنه تعالى يخلق في ذلك الإنسان علماً بديهياً ضرورياً ، بأن ذلك الذي وصل إليه ملك صادق ، لا شيطان كاذب فإنه إن لم يحصل هذا المعنى ، امتنع كون الرسول البشري ، عالماً بأن ذلك الواصل ، ملك من عند الله تعالى وإذا وقع الشك في الأصل ، فوقوعه في الفرع أولى والله أعلم بالصواب
Chapter Three: Investigation of the way by which the messenger knows that he is a messenger from God, Mighty and Majestic.

The objectors concerning miracles said: Grant that the nation knows by means of miracles that the messenger is a trustworthy, truthful human in his claim. But as for the messenger, how does he know that he is a messenger? That is because the establishment of his message is either because God Most High removes the intermediary and says: 'O servant, you are My messenger to the creation,' or because God Most High sends an angel to him and that angel says to him: 'You are the messenger of God Most High to the creation.'

As for the first division: it is improbable. Most of the prophets are agreed that the message came to them from God through the intermediary of an angel. So the second division remains. We say: Just as the nation is in need of a proof to distinguish between the truthful claimant and the false claimant, so also the messenger cannot distinguish between the infallible angel and the accursed devil except by a miracle. But there is no way to this miracle. That is because the human messenger does not know what is customary in the world of angels and what is contrary to custom there. So every miracle that the angel brings to establish that he is truthful, it is possible for the human messenger that that matter is something customary in the world of angels. On this supposition, that angel is not able to establish a proof that he is an infallible angel. If they say: When the angel brings a specific act, established by proof that no one except God the Glorious and Most High is able to produce it, then the prophet knows that he is an infallible angel, not an accursed devil. We say: This also does not help, because that act, even if it is an act that no one except God Most High is able to bring into existence, it is not impossible in reason that He Most High has a custom in the world of angels that everyone who supplicates to God and implores Him requesting that act, then He Most High does it in answer to the supplication of that supplicant. When this meaning is possible, its appearance at the hand of this angel is not evidence of his being truthful of speech in claiming the message. It is established that the angel cannot define for the human messenger that he is truthful of speech in claiming to be a messenger from God to that human. It remains to be said: He Most High creates in that human necessary self-evident knowledge that the one who has reached him is a truthful angel, not a lying devil. For if this meaning is not obtained, it is impossible for the human messenger to know that the one reaching him is an angel from God Most High. And when doubt occurs in the foundation, its occurrence in the branch is more fitting. And God knows best the correct.
Source pages p. 86

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical argument from a kalam work (likely al-Razi's al-Matalib al-Aliya) addressing the epistemological problem of how a prophet himself can know that he is a prophet, particularly in the face of skepticism about miracles. The structure is as follows: First, it presents an objection from critics of miracles: they concede that the community (the nation) can know, through miracles, that the prophet is trustworthy and truthful in his claim, but they question how the prophet himself knows his own prophethood. The objectors then outline two possibilities: either God directly announces it to him without an intermediary, or an angel delivers the message. The first is dismissed as unlikely, and it is noted that most prophets agree that they received the message through an angel. So the discussion focuses on the second possibility. The core problem is then stated: just as the nation needs a proof to distinguish a truthful claimant from a false one, the prophet himself cannot distinguish between an infallible angel and an accursed devil except by a miracle. But this very miracle is problematic because the prophet does not know what is normal or miraculous in the angelic realm. Therefore, any miracle performed by the angel to prove his truthfulness could be merely a customary event in the world of angels. This makes it impossible for the angel to establish a conclusive proof of his infallibility. The objectors then consider a counter-argument: if the angel performs an act that only God can produce, the prophet would know it is a true angel. The response to this is that even such an act does not guarantee truthfulness because it is rationally possible that God has a habit in the angelic world of answering the prayers of any supplicant for such an act. Hence, the act is not decisive evidence. The argument concludes by suggesting that the only solution is that God creates in the prophet a necessary and self-evident knowledge (ʿilm badīhī ḍarūrī) that the being reaching him is a truthful angel, not a lying devil. Without this, the prophet could never be certain. The final line warns that if doubt occurs at the fundamental level (prophet's own knowledge), it is even more likely in derived matters.

شيطان مرجوم
علم بديهي ضروري

Misconception: A miracle is sufficient for the prophet to know his own prophethood.
Correction: The passage shows that the prophet cannot rely on a miracle alone because he cannot distinguish whether the miracle is a customary event in the angelic realm or a genuine sign.

Checkpoint
According to the objection, why does a miracle performed by an angel fail to prove to the prophet that the angel is truly an angel and not a devil?
A) Because the prophet may be hallucinating.
B) Because the prophet cannot know whether the miracle is a customary event in the angelic realm.
C) Because the angel might be lying.
D) Because the miracle is not performed by God directly.

Key Takeaway
The prophet's certainty about his prophethood cannot be based on miracles alone; it must be grounded in necessary, self-evident knowledge created directly by God.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
How do you know that the message you believe in is truly from God? Can you point to a miracle or sign, or is your certainty grounded in something deeper that God has placed in your heart? If your certainty today were challenged, what would it rest on?
?
The passage argues that even a prophet cannot rely on miracles alone for certainty. Reflect on your own relationship with Allah: Are you relying on external signs to keep your faith strong, or have you sought the direct, necessary knowledge that only He can grant? What would it take for you to seek that kind of certainty?

Consider the possibility that the devil might present convincing signs to lead you astray. How confident are you that you could distinguish a true message from a deceptive one? The passage says God creates necessary knowledge in the prophet. Have you ever experienced a moment where Allah placed such clarity in your heart? If not, are you actively seeking it?

Why Miracles Alone Cannot Prove an Angel's Truthfulness
Al-Razi argues that miracles alone do not prove an angel's truthfulness without necessary knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the occurrence of a miracle at the hand of an angel does not necessarily prove the angel's truthfulness, and why Allah must create necessary knowledge in the prophet to certify the angel's authenticity.

Core Idea
A miracle alone is not decisive; necessary knowledge from Allah is required for certainty.

This passage from Al-Razi addresses a critical question: how can a prophet be sure that the being delivering revelation is a truthful angel and not a deceitful devil? The common assumption is that miracles are sufficient proof, but Al-Razi challenges that. He argues that even an extraordinary act could be granted by Allah in response to an angel's prayer, making it inconclusive. The real guarantee, he explains, is Allah's creation of necessary, self-evident knowledge in the prophet's heart.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read both the Arabic and English carefully. Notice how Al-Razi structures his argument: first stating the problem, then offering a potential counterexample, then proposing the solution involving necessary knowledge.
The text uses brackets to indicate manuscript variations; focus on the main flow.
2
Identify the core objection and response
What is the objection Al-Razi is answering? Why does he say that a miracle alone does not prove the angel is truthful? Identify the scenario he proposes (angel's prayer being answered).
This is the key logical move in the passage.
3
Understand the role of necessary knowledge
Al-Razi concludes that Allah creates 'necessary, self-evident knowledge' (علم بديهي ضروري) in the prophet. How does this solve the problem? Why is this knowledge labeled 'necessary'?
Necessary knowledge is intuitive and not derived from reasoning.
4
Reflect on the implication
Consider the final sentence: 'If doubt occurs in the principle, its occurrence in the branch is more likely.' What does this mean for the reliability of prophetic knowledge?
Al-Razi emphasizes that without this necessary knowledge, even the foundation of prophethood would be uncertain.

النبي كونه ملكاً معصوماً لا شيطاناً مرجوماً . فنقول : هذا أيضاً [ لا يفيد وذلك لأن ذلك الفعل ، وإن كان فعلا ] لا يقدر أحد على إيجاده إلا الله تعالى ، إلا أنه لا يمتنع في العقل أنه - تعالى - أجرى العادة في عالم الملائكة بأن كل من دعا الله ، وتضرع إليه في طلب ذلك الفعل ، فإنه تعالى يفعله إجابة لدعاء ذلك الداعي، وإذا كان هذا المعنى محتملاً ، لم يكن ظهوره على يد هذا الملك : دليلاً على كونه صادق اللهجة في ادعاء الرسالة [ فيثبت أن الملك لا يمكنه تعريف الرسول البشري كونه صادق القول في ادعاء ] كونه رسولاً من عند الله إلى ذلك الإنسان . بقي أن يقال : إنه تعالى يخلق في ذلك الإنسان علما بديهياً ضرورياً ، بأن ذلك الذي وصل إليه ملك صادق ، لا شيطان كاذب فإنه إن لم يحصل هذا المعنى ، امتنع كون الرسول البشري ، عالماً بأن ذلك الواصل ، ملك من عند الله تعالى وإذا وقع الشك في الأصل ، فوقوعه في الفرع أولى [ والله أعلم بالصواب ]
The prophet being a protected angel, not an accursed devil. We say: This also [does not benefit because that act, even if it is an act] that no one can bring into existence except Allah Almighty, yet it is not rationally impossible that He - exalted - made it the custom in the world of angels that whoever calls upon Allah and beseeches Him in seeking that act, then Allah Almighty does it in response to that caller's supplication. And if this meaning is possible, then its occurring at the hand of this angel is not evidence for his being truthful in his claim of prophethood. [Thus it is established that the angel cannot make the human messenger know that he is truthful in his claim] of being a messenger from Allah to that person. It remains to be said: Allah Almighty creates in that person necessary, self-evident knowledge that the one who reached him is a truthful angel, not a lying devil. For if this meaning is not attained, it would be impossible for the human messenger to know that the one reaching him is an angel from Allah Almighty. And if doubt occurs in the principle, its occurrence in the branch is more likely. [And Allah knows best what is correct.]
Source pages p. 87

Commentary
This passage is part of a refutation of an objection regarding the prophet's certainty about receiving revelation from an angel. The author begins by stating the objection: that a prophet cannot be certain whether the being he encounters is a protected angel or an accursed devil. The author then responds by arguing that even if the miracle performed by the angel is an act that only God can do, it could still be that God has made it a custom that whenever an angel prays for a miracle, God grants it in response. Therefore the miracle alone is not proof that the angel is truthful in claiming prophethood. The author then adds a further point: to resolve this, God creates in the human prophet a necessary, self-evident knowledge (علم بديهي ضروري) that the being is a truthful angel, not a lying devil. Without this, the prophet could not be certain. The author concludes that if there is doubt about the original principle (the prophet's certainty), then doubt about the secondary matter (the validity of the message) is even more likely. The passage ends with 'And Allah knows best.'

Correction: Al-Razi argues that the miracle could be a response to the angel's prayer, not a sign of truthfulness; therefore, a miracle alone is not decisive. Instead, Allah creates necessary knowledge in the prophet to provide certainty.

A miracle alone cannot certify an angel's truthfulness; Allah must create necessary knowledge in the prophet to remove all doubt.

First Doubt: Abrogation Cannot Affect Essential Matters of Religion
Al-Razi presents the first doubt and its refutation via essential vs. non-essential distinction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first doubt against prophethood based on abrogation and why it cannot affect the essential truths of knowing God and doing good.

Core Idea
Abrogation only changes secondary laws, not the fixed essentials of religion.

Al-Razi begins Chapter Fourteen by presenting a series of doubts against the validity of prophethood. The first doubt argues that abrogation of earlier laws by later prophets implies inconsistency, but this argument fails because abrogation can only affect non-essential matters. This passage sets up the premise that human happiness depends on knowledge of truth and good deeds, and then argues that abrogation cannot change these essentials.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation to grasp the argument's flow.
Notice how the premise is established before the objection is raised.
2
Identify the premise
The passage states that human perfection lies in knowing truth and doing good, and that everything else is futile. Understand why this premise is crucial.
This premise is the foundation for the argument against abrogation of essentials.
3
Distinguish essential vs non-essential
The argument divides abrogation into two types: affecting the essential goals (knowledge of God and good deeds) or affecting additional secondary matters. See how al-Razi labels the first type as 'absolutely false'.
The text breaks off but clearly implies only non-essential abrogation is possible.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
Even though the passage is incomplete, the logical conclusion is that prophets only abrogate secondary rulings, preserving the core message. This answers the doubt.
This strengthens the case for prophethood by showing consistency.

الفصل الرابع عشر في الشبهات المبنية على أنه ظاهر على الأنبياء أعمال تقدح في صحة نبوتهم فالشبهة الأولى : قالوا : ثبت بالدلائل العقلية : أن كمال حال الإنسان في الدنيا والآخرة في أن يعرف الحق لذاته، والخير لأجل العمل به [ وأشرف المعارف معرفة الله تعالى ] وأشرف الأعمال : الإعراض عن الدنيا ، والإقبال على الآخرة . فيثبت : أن سعادة الخلق مربوطة بتحصيل هذين الأمرين ، وكل ما سوى هذين المطلوبين فهو عبث عديم الفائدة . وإذا ظهرت هذه المقدمة . فنقول : إنا نرى الأنبياء والرسل ينسخ بعضهم ، شرائع بعض المتقدمين ، وهذا النسخ والتبديل إما أن يكون قد وقع في هذا الذي بينا أنه هو المقصود الأصلي ، والغرض الأشرف . أو في أمور زائدة عليها ، مغايرة لها . والقسم الأول : باطل قطعاً . لأنا لما بينا : أن كمال السعادة والخير ، موقوف على تحصيل هذين المطلوبين ، فكل من جاء بتقريره وتأكيده كان محقاً ، وكل من
Chapter Fourteen: On the doubts based on the appearance on prophets of deeds that detract from the soundness of their prophethood. The first doubt: They said: It is established by rational proofs that the perfection of man's state in this world and the Hereafter lies in knowing the truth for its own sake, and [doing] good for the sake of acting upon it. [And the noblest of cognitions is knowledge of Allah Almighty.] And the noblest of deeds is turning away from this world and turning towards the Hereafter. Thus it is established that the happiness of creation is tied to acquiring these two matters. And everything besides these two desired ends is futile and without benefit. Once this premise is clear, we say: We see prophets and messengers abrogating each other's laws. This abrogation and change either occurs in that which we have shown to be the primary intention and the noblest goal, or in matters additional to them and distinct from them. The first type is absolutely false. For when we have shown that perfection of happiness and good depends on acquiring these two desired ends, then whoever comes to confirm and emphasize them is correct, and whoever...
Source pages p. 88

Commentary
This passage begins Chapter Fourteen and presents the first doubt (الشبهة الأولى) against the validity of prophethood based on apparent shortcomings in prophets. The author, likely al-Razi, first establishes a premise: rational proofs show that human perfection lies in knowing the truth and doing good, with the noblest knowledge being knowledge of God and the noblest deed being renouncing this world for the Hereafter. Thus, all happiness depends on these two goals; anything else is futile. Then the objection is raised: prophets sometimes abrogate the laws of earlier prophets. This abrogation must either affect these essential goals or secondary matters. The first possibility (abrogating the essentials) is deemed absolutely false, because the essentials are fixed and confirmed; anyone who comes to affirm and emphasize them is correct. The text breaks off mid-sentence ("and whoever...") but the implication is that abrogation can only involve non-essential matters, which is permissible. This doubt is therefore refuted by distinguishing between essential and non-essential aspects of religion.

مقصود أصلي
شريعة
عبث
سعادة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that abrogation implies change in God's will or knowledge, or that it invalidates the previous prophet.
Correction: This passage shows that abrogation only affects secondary rulings, not the essential truths that are fixed and necessary for human happiness.

Checkpoint
According to the first doubt presented, which type of abrogation is considered absolutely false?
A) Abrogation in secondary matters
B) Abrogation in the essential goals of knowing truth and doing good
C) Abrogation of all laws
D) Abrogation by non-prophets

Key Takeaway
The first doubt is refuted by showing that abrogation only pertains to secondary matters, not the fixed essentials of knowing God and doing good.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you learn that some Islamic rulings changed from previous prophets, does it ever shake your trust that the earlier prophets were sent by God? How does this passage's distinction between essential and non-essential confront that doubt directly? Ask yourself: Have I ever allowed confusion about abrogation to weaken my certainty in the consistency of Allah's message? Commit to reciting the arguments of this passage to yourself the next time such doubt arises.
?
The passage says that anything besides knowing truth and doing good is 'futile and without benefit.' Look at your daily life: how much of your time and energy is spent on matters that do not lead to these two goals? Will you today identify one activity you engage in that is truly 'عبث' and replace it with something that draws you closer to Allah?
?
Al-Razi asserts that prophets all confirm the same essential matters. If you compare your own understanding of tawheed and sincerity to that of earlier prophets, do you find that you truly affirm the same essentials? Or have you allowed cultural or personal preferences to add or subtract from the core message? This week, examine your heart: Is there any part of your belief or practice that contradicts what every prophet agreed upon?

Refutation of the First Doubt: Abrogation and Essential vs. Secondary Matters
Razi presents the first doubt and counters it with an example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the first doubt attempts to impugn prophethood through abrogation and how Razi's example exposes its flaw.

Core Idea
The doubt confuses essential purposes with secondary forms, missing the wisdom in specific prophetic laws.

This passage presents a skeptical challenge: if abrogation changes either essential or secondary matters, both seem problematic. Razi then provides a concrete example to show that the doubt fails to appreciate the value of specific forms in worship. As you read, notice how the example illustrates that the essential purpose is preserved even when the form changes.

Guided Path
1
Read the doubt's premise and argument
Start by understanding the rational premise that human happiness lies solely in knowledge of God and good deeds. Then see how the doubt uses this to argue against abrogation.
The doubt claims that all else is futile.
2
Identify the two divisions
The doubt splits abrogation into two categories: affecting essential purposes or secondary matters. Notice the arguments against each.
3
Examine the example of prayer, fasting, and qibla
Razi gives examples where the essential purpose is common across religions, yet prophets insist on specific forms. See how this undermines the doubt.
The qibla example is incomplete but the pattern is clear.
4
Reflect on the refutation
Consider why the prophets' insistence on specific forms is not futile. The example shows that secondary matters have significance beyond mere benefit.

الفصل الرابع عشر في الشبهات المبنية على أنه ظاهر على الأنبياء أعمال تقدح في صحة نبوتهم. فالشبهة الأولى: قالوا: ثبت بالدلائل العقلية: أن كمال حال الإنسان في الدنيا والآخرة في أن يعرف الحق لذاته، والخير لأجل العمل به وأشرف المعارف معرفة الله تعالى وأشرف الأعمال: الإعراض عن الدنيا، والإقبال على الآخرة. فيثبت: أن سعادة الخلق مربوطة بتحصيل هذين الأمرين، وكل ما سوى هذين المطلوبين فهو عبث عديم الفائدة. وإذا ظهرت هذه المقدمة. فنقول: إنا نرى الأنبياء والرسل ينسخ بعضهم شرائع بعض المتقدمين، وهذا النسخ والتبديل إما أن يكون قد وقع في هذا الذي بينا أنه هو المقصود الأصلي، والغرض الأشرف. أو في أمور زائدة عليها، مغايرة لها. والقسم الأول: باطل قطعاً. لأنا لما بينا: أن كمال السعادة والخير، موقوف على تحصيل هذين المطلوبين، فكل من جاء بتقريره وتأكيده كان محقاً، وكل من جاء برفعه وإزالته وإبطاله ونسخه كان مبطلاً، فلو كان النسخ والتغيير والتبديل واقعاً في هذا القسم، كان الآتي بالنسخ مبطلاً كاذباً، وحينئذ يلزم تكذيب الأنبياء وذلك لا يجوز. وأما القسم الثاني وهو أن يقال: التفاوت في الشرائع ما وقع في هذه القواعد الشريفة، والمهمات الأصلية. وإنما وقع في الفروع والزوائد. فنقول: الاختلاف في مثل هذه الزوائد والتوابع إما أن لا يفيد منفعة أصلاً، أو إن أفاد منفعة فإن تلك المنفعة تكون قليلة جداً. ومثل هذا الاختلاف لا يليق به حمل الناس على أحد القولين، ومنعهم من القول الثاني، بالقتل والنهب والإيلام والإيذاء ورأينا أن الأنبياء يفعلون ذلك. فكان هذا قادحاً في طريقتهم

ومثاله: إن المقصود الأصلي من الصلاة: أن يكون القلب مشتغلاً بنية العبودية، واللسان بالذكر والثناء، والأعضاء مزينة بأنواع الخدمة. وهذا المقصود حاصل بالصلاة التي يؤتى بها على مذهب اليهود، وبالصلاة التي يؤتى بها على مذهب النصارى، وبالصلاة التي يؤتى بها على مذهب المسلمين. فثبت: أن ما هو المقصود الأصلي حاصل على كل التقديرات ثم إنا نرى الأنبياء يبالغون في حمل الناس على طريقتهم وفي منع الناس عن طريقة من تقدمهم ويزعمون: أن العمل بالطريقة المتقدمة: كفر. يوجب حل الدم، ويوجب العذاب الدائم ويوجب نهب الأموال وسبي وأيضاً: المقصود من الصوم: قهر النفس. وذلك لا يتفاوت بأن يقع ذلك الصوم في شهر رمضان، أو في شهر آخر. فالمبالغة في تعيين هذا الشهر، والمنع من سائر الشهور يكون عديم الفائدة. وكذلك المقصود من القبلة: أن يكون الإنسان عند اشتغاله بخدمة الله تعالى، مستقراً ثابتاً، حتى يتفرغ قلبه للاشتغال بخدمة الله تعالى. وهذا المقصود لا يتفاوت بأن تكون القبلة هي
Chapter Fourteen on the doubts based on the appearance of acts that impugn the validity of prophethood upon the prophets. The first doubt: They said: It is established by rational proofs that the perfection of man's state in this world and the Hereafter lies in knowing truth for its own sake and good for the sake of acting upon it, and the most noble knowledge is knowledge of God, and the most noble acts are turning away from this world and turning toward the Hereafter. So it is established that the happiness of creatures is linked to acquiring these two matters, and everything other than these two desired things is futile, devoid of benefit. And when this premise is clear, we say: We see the prophets and messengers abrogating the laws of some of their predecessors. This abrogation and change either occurs in that which we have shown to be the primary purpose and noblest aim, or in matters additional to them, different from them. The first division is absolutely false. Because when we have shown that the perfection of happiness and good depends on acquiring these two desired objects, then whoever comes to confirm and reinforce them is truthful, and whoever comes to remove, abolish, invalidate, and abrogate them is false. So if abrogation, change, and alteration occur in this division, then the one who brings abrogation would be false and lying, and then the necessitation of lying against the prophets would follow, and that is not permissible. As for the second division—that it is said: The variance in laws does not occur in these noble principles and fundamental important matters, but only occurs in the branches and auxiliary matters—we say: The difference in such auxiliary and dependent matters either yields no benefit at all, or if it yields benefit, then that benefit is very little. And such a difference is not suitable for compelling people to one of the two opinions and preventing them from the second opinion through killing, plunder, infliction of pain, and harm. And we see that the prophets do that. So this was an impugnment of their way.

And an example: The primary purpose of prayer is that the heart be occupied with the intention of servitude, the tongue with remembrance and praise, and the limbs adorned with types of service. This purpose is achieved by the prayer performed according to the doctrine of the Jews, by the prayer performed according to the doctrine of the Christians, and by the prayer performed according to the doctrine of the Muslims. So it is established that the primary purpose is achieved on all suppositions. Then we see the prophets exaggerating in compelling people to their way and in preventing people from the way of their predecessors, and they claim that acting according to the previous way is unbelief that necessitates shedding blood, eternal punishment, plunder of wealth, and capture of offspring. Also, the purpose of fasting is subduing the soul. That does not differ whether the fast occurs in the month of Ramadan or in another month. So the exaggeration in specifying this month and preventing other months is devoid of benefit. Similarly, the purpose of the prayer direction is that a person, when occupied with the service of God, be stable and fixed so that his heart may be free for occupying himself with the service of God. This purpose does not differ whether the prayer direction is...
Source pages p. 88

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the first doubt (شبهة) concerning the prophethood of prophets. The doubt begins by establishing a rational premise: the perfection of human happiness in this world and the Hereafter lies solely in knowing truth for its own sake and doing good for its sake, with the most noble knowledge being knowledge of God, and the most noble action being turning away from this world and toward the Hereafter. All else is futile. Using this premise, the objector argues that prophets abrogate previous laws, and this abrogation must either affect these essential purposes or affect secondary matters. The objector argues that the first case is impossible because if abrogation touched the essential purposes, then the abrogator would be false, necessitating the lie against prophets. As for the second case, the objector claims that differences in secondary matters either yield no benefit or very little, and therefore it is inappropriate for prophets to impose one way by force, killing, plunder, and harm—yet prophets do so. This, according to the doubt, impugns their way. The refutation is implicit in the structure: the author presents the doubt without explicitly rebutting it here, but the example that follows serves to illustrate the weakness of the doubt. The example uses prayer, fasting, and prayer direction to show that the essential purpose (e.g., humility, remembrance, subduing the soul, stability) is common across different forms, but the prophets still insist on specific forms. This suggests that the secondary differences have a significance beyond mere benefit, and the prophets' insistence is not futile. The passage is incomplete; the example of the prayer direction is cut off.

نسخ
مقصود أصلي
فروع وزوائد
إبطال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Abrogation of secondary matters is futile and unworthy of a prophet.
Correction: Secondary matters in worship carry divine wisdom and obligation; their specific forms are not arbitrary but are part of the revealed law.

Checkpoint
What does the first doubt claim about abrogation?
A) Abrogation proves that prophets were mistaken.
B) Abrogation either affects essential matters (making prophets false) or secondary matters (too trivial to warrant forceful imposition).
C) Abrogation is permissible only in secondary matters.
D) Abrogation shows that later prophets are superior.

Key Takeaway
The doubt fails because it assumes secondary matters are trivial, but prophets' insistence on specific forms shows they have profound wisdom and divine command.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The doubter argued that secondary matters yield little benefit, yet prophets enforced them with severe consequences. In what area of your life have you been lax in following the precise form of worship (e.g., prayer, fasting, dress) because you couldn't see its immediate benefit? How will you change that starting today?
?

What does that tell you about the seriousness of adhering to the specific guidance brought by the final Prophet (peace be upon him)? Prophethood

First Objection: Differences in Religious Laws
First objection against prophethood: differences in laws are only secondary, yet prophets enforce them violently.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first objection against prophethood: that differences in religious laws are merely secondary, and the harsh enforcement by prophets suggests worldly motives.

Core Idea
If essential purposes are the same, why enforce minor differences with violence?

The first objection against prophethood challenges the necessity of prophetic specificity. If the core aims of worship—devotion, humility, gathering—are achieved across various forms, what justifies the harsh exclusivity? This lesson presents the objector's argument step by step, from the claim that differences are only in branches to the conclusion that prophets seek worldly leadership.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection statement
Read the opening lines where the objector claims that differences in religious laws only occur in branches, not in fundamental principles. Notice the implication that these differences are trivial.
The objector sets up a premise that variations are insignificant.
2
Examine the first example: prayer
The objector argues that the essential purpose of prayer (heart in servitude, tongue in remembrance, limbs in service) is achieved in Jewish, Christian, and Muslim forms. Yet prophets enforce their specific forms harshly.
This example is meant to show that the core benefit is unchanged.
3
Review the other examples: fasting, qibla, gathering
The objector extends the argument to fasting (purpose: subduing the soul), qibla (purpose: stillness for devotion), and gathering (purpose: collective seeking of mercy). In each case, the specific form is claimed to be irrelevant.
These examples build the case that the prophets' insistence on specific forms is arbitrary.
4
Analyze the conclusion
The objector concludes that since the differences have no essential benefit, the only explanation for the harsh enforcement (killing, plundering) is the desire for worldly leadership and superiority.
This is the core accusation: prophets are motivated by power, not divine command.
5
Reflect on the objection's force
Consider why this objection might be persuasive to someone unfamiliar with Islamic theology. What assumptions does it make? What might a refutation need to address?
This step prepares you to engage with the response in subsequent lessons.

وأما القسم الثاني. وهو أن يقال : التفاوت في الشرائع ما وقع في هذه القواعد الشريفة ، والمهمات الأصلية . وإنما وقع في الفروع والزوائد . فنقول : الاختلاف في مثل هذه الزوائد والتوابع إما أن لا يفيد منفعة أصلاً ، أو إن أفاد منفعة فإن تلك المنفعة تكون قليلة جداً . ومثل هذا الاختلاف لا يليق به حمل الناس على أحد القولين ، ومنعهم من القول الثاني ، بالقتل والنهب والإيلام والإيذاء ورأينا أن الأنبياء يفعلون ذلك . فكان هذا قادحاً في طريقتهم ومثاله : إن المقصود الأصلي من الصلاة : أن يكون القلب مشتغلاً بنية العبودية ، واللسان بالذكر والثناء ، والأعضاء مزينة بأنواع الخدمة . وهذا المقصود حاصل بالصلاة التي يؤتى بها على مذهب اليهود ، وبالصلاة التي يؤتى بها على مذهب النصارى ، وبالصلاة التي يؤتى بها على مذهب المسلمين . فثبت : أن ما هو المقصود الأصلي حاصل على كل التقديرات ثم إنا نرى الأنبياء يبالغون في حمل الناس على طريقتهم وفي منع الناس عن طريقة من تقدمهم ويزعمون : أن العمل بالطريقة المتقدمة : كفر . يوجب حل الدم، ويوجب العذاب الدائم ويوجب نهب الأموال وسبي الأولاد . وأيضاً : المقصود من الصوم : قهر النفس . وذلك لا يتفاوت بأن يقع ذلك الصوم في شهر رمضان ، أو في شهر آخر. فالمبالغة في تعيين هذا الشهر ، والمنع من سائر الشهور يكون عديم الفائدة. وكذلك المقصود من القبلة : أن يكون الإنسان عند اشتغاله بخدمة الله تعالى ، مستقراً ثابتاً ، حتى يتفرغ قلبه للاشتغال بخدمة الله تعالى. وهذا المقصود لا يتفاوت بأن تكون القبلة هي الكعبة أو غيرها . وكذلك المقصود من اجتماع الخلق الكثير في الموضع الواحد ، لأجل أداء الطاعات والعبادات أن تصير كثرة الأرواح المتوجهة إلى استنزال رحمة الله تعالى ، سبباً لقوة ذلك التأثير ولتكميله ، وهذا لا يتفاوت بأن تكون تلك الجمعية حاصلة في يوم الجمعة، أو في يوم السبت ، أو في يوم الأحد. إذا عرفت هذا ، فنقول : ظهر بهذا البحث : أن الأشياء التي اختلفت الشرائع فيها : أمور لا فائدة البتة ، بحسب المطالب الأصلية . فلم يبق إلا أن يقال : الغرض من التشديد في إظهارها : أن يصير ذكر ذلك المتقدم مندرساً . وأن يصير ذكر هذا الثاني باقيا ، فيما بين الناس. ولا فائدة في ذلك إلا طلب الرئاسة في الدنيا والتفوق على الخلق . فلما شرعوا القتل والنهب والإيذاء والإيلام ، لتقرير هذه المعاني ، علمنا : أنه ليس بصواب.. وظهر أن المقصود منه ليس إلا طلب الرئاسة في الدنيا ، فيكون باطلاً .
As for the second section: it is that it is said: variation in the religious laws did not occur in these noble principles and fundamental matters. Rather, it occurred in the branches and additions. So we say: difference in such additions and dependencies either does not yield any benefit at all, or if it yields benefit, that benefit is very small. Such difference is not fitting to compel people to one of the two opinions and prevent them from the second opinion, by killing, plundering, afflicting, and harming. And we see that the prophets do that. So this was an objection against their way. And its example: the original purpose of prayer is that the heart be occupied with the intention of servitude, the tongue with remembrance and praise, and the limbs adorned with types of service. This purpose is attained by the prayer performed according to the school of the Jews, and by the prayer performed according to the school of the Christians, and by the prayer performed according to the school of the Muslims. So it is established that what is the original purpose is attained in all cases. Then we see the prophets exaggerating in urging people to their way and in preventing people from the way of those before them, and they claim that acting by the previous way is unbelief, which necessitates shedding blood, and necessitates eternal punishment, and necessitates plundering wealth and capturing children. Also, the purpose of fasting is subduing the soul. That does not differ whether that fasting occurs in the month of Ramadan or in another month. So exaggeration in specifying this month and preventing from other months is useless. Likewise, the purpose of the direction of prayer (qibla) is that the person, when occupied with the service of God Almighty, be settled and still, so that his heart may be free for occupying himself with the service of God Almighty. This purpose does not differ whether the qibla is the Kaaba or something else. Likewise, the purpose of gathering many creatures in one place for performing acts of obedience and worship is that the multitude of souls turning to seek the descent of God's mercy become a cause for the strength of that effect and its completion. This does not differ whether that gathering occurs on Friday, or on Saturday, or on Sunday. When you have understood this, we say: it has become clear through this investigation that the things concerning which the laws differ are matters of absolutely no benefit in terms of the original objectives. So nothing remains except to say: the purpose of the severity in manifesting them is that the remembrance of that former one become erased, and that the remembrance of this latter one remain among the people. There is no benefit in that except seeking leadership in this world and superiority over creation. So when they legislated killing, plundering, harming, and afflicting to establish these meanings, we knew that it is not correct. And it became clear that the purpose thereof is nothing but seeking leadership in this world, so it is false.
Source pages p. 89

Commentary
This passage presents the first objection (shubhah) against prophethood. The objector argues that differences among religious laws are only in secondary branches, not in fundamental principles. Such minor differences do not warrant violent enforcement, yet prophets impose their specific laws with killing, plundering, and harm. The objector then gives examples: prayer, fasting, qibla, and communal gatherings. In each case, the essential purpose (e.g., devotion, subduing the soul, stillness, and gathering for mercy) is achieved regardless of the particular form. Yet prophets insist on their specific forms, declaring others as kufr and justifying bloodshed. This, the objector claims, reveals that the true motive is not divine command but worldly leadership and superiority. Therefore, the prophets' enforcement is invalid.

Key Terms
الشريعة
الفروع
الأصول
الرئاسة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the first objection presented in this passage, what is the real motive behind the prophets' strict enforcement of their specific laws?
A) To preserve the essential purposes of worship
B) To seek worldly leadership and superiority
C) To ensure uniformity in religious practice
D) To prevent innovation in religion

Key Takeaway
The first objection against prophethood argues that since differences in laws are only superficial, the harsh enforcement by prophets reveals a worldly desire for leadership.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you perform acts of worship like prayer or fasting, do you ever focus so much on the external form that you forget the inner purpose of drawing closer to Allah? How might this objection expose a spiritual danger in your own practice? Commit now to checking your intention before each act of worship.
?
If someone accused the Prophet Muhammad (peace be upon him) of enforcing laws only for personal leadership, how would you respond? Does the strength of your conviction in the wisdom of Islamic specifics waver when challenged? What is one specific teaching you accept purely because Allah commanded it, even if its wisdom is unclear?
?
Think of a time you judged another Muslim for a difference in practice (e.g., raising hands in prayer, celebrating mawlid). Did that judgment come from arrogance? How does the objector’s critique—that enforcing minor differences reveals worldly desire—force you to examine your own heart? Resolve today to prioritize unity in essentials and to learn the wisdom in differences.
