The Second Objection: Reason vs. Prophethood
The author presents the second objection: rational judgment of good and evil conflicts with prophetic teaching on the necessity of belief in prophethood.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second objection against prophethood that claims a contradiction between rational judgment and prophetic teaching on salvation.

Core Idea
Reason says the pious are saved; revelation says they must believe in prophets.

This passage presents a powerful objection from rationalist philosophers. They argue that if reason can determine moral good and evil, then it conflicts with the prophets' insistence on belief in prophethood for salvation. Let's examine their reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own argumentative flow come through.
Notice that the passage sets up a dilemma with two horns.
2
Identify the dilemma
The objection presents two possibilities: either rational judgment is valid or it is not. Focus on how this creates a problem for prophethood regardless.
3
Understand the first horn
If rational judgment is accepted, then reason deems a person fully devoted to God but not believing in prophethood as among the blessed righteous. Note the criteria used by reason.
This is a crucial step: reason's verdict is based on the person's spiritual state.
4
See the contradiction
The prophets declare that such a person, despite his devotion, is a disbeliever deserving eternal punishment. This directly contradicts reason's verdict.
5
Reflect on the implication
The objector uses this contradiction to argue that prophethood is problematic because it opposes rational judgment. Ask yourself: does this objection succeed?
We will explore the answer in later lessons.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الشبهة الثانية للقوم : قالوا : إن حكم العقل في التحسين والتقبيح . إما أن يكون معتبراً ، وإما أن لا يكون ، وعلى التقديرين ، فالقول بالنبوة : مشكل . أما القسم الأول : وهو أن يكون التقدير : أن يكون حكم العقل في التحسين والتقبيح مقبولاً ، فنقول : إنه متى كان الأمر كذلك ، كانت بدائه العقول قاضية بأن الإنسان إذا كان قلبه خالياً عن الالتفات إلى الدنيا وشهواتها ، وكان خالياً عن الاشتغال بغير الله تعالى ، وكان غريقاً في نور معرفة الله تعالى، وفي ذكره ، فهذا الإنسان إذا مات ، وجب أن يكون من السعداء الأبرار ، لكنا نرى أن الأنبياء يقولون : إن من كان حاله على ما ذكرناه ، ثم إنه غفل عن الإقرار بالنبوة والرسالة ، أو كان متوقفاً فيه ، كان كافراً من أهل العذاب الدائم ، والعقاب المخلد ، ولما ثبت أن حكم العقل في الإنسان المذكور أن يكون من السعداء الأبرار ، وثبت أن حكم الشرع فيه أن يكون من الأشقياء الكفار ، ثبت : أن حكم الشرع مخالف لحكم العقل ، ولما
The second objection of the people: they said: The judgment of reason concerning the good and evil (tahsin wa taqbih) is either considered valid or not considered valid. On both suppositions, the claim of prophethood is problematic. As for the first section: which is the supposition that the judgment of reason concerning good and evil is accepted, we say: when this is the case, the primary intuitions of intellects judge that if a person's heart is free from turning to this world and its desires, and is free from occupation with other than God Almighty, and is immersed in the light of knowledge of God Almighty and in His remembrance, then this person, if he dies, must be among the blessed righteous. But we see that the prophets say: whoever's state is as we described, then he neglects to affirm prophethood and the message, or is hesitant about it, is a disbeliever among the people of eternal punishment and everlasting torment. And since it is established that the judgment of reason concerning the mentioned person is that he should be among the blessed righteous, and it is established that the judgment of divine law concerning him is that he should be among the wretched disbelievers, it is established that the judgment of divine law contradicts the judgment of reason. And since...
Source pages p. 90

Commentary
This passage presents the second objection (shubhah) against prophethood. The objector frames a dilemma: either rational judgment about good and evil is valid or it is not. On both options, prophethood becomes problematic. The objection explores the first option: if rational judgment is accepted, then reason dictates that a person who is completely devoted to God, free from worldly distractions and immersed in divine knowledge, must be among the blessed righteous when he dies. Yet the prophets declare that such a person, if he does not affirm prophethood and the message, is a disbeliever destined for eternal punishment. This creates a contradiction: divine law (shar') opposes rational judgment (aql). The objection then cuts off, but the reader can infer that this contradiction is used to undermine the truth of prophethood.

First Horn: Accepting Rational Judgment Leads to Contradiction
First horn: accepting rational judgment yields contradiction with revelation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the objectors use the assumption that rational judgment is accepted to argue that revelation contradicts reason, thereby challenging prophethood.

Core Idea
Accepted reason saves the devoted; revelation damns them—a clash that must be resolved.

This passage presents the first branch of a dilemma against prophethood. The objectors assume that reason's judgments about good and evil are valid and then construct a scenario where reason deems a God-devoted person saved, but revelation declares the same person damned for not accepting prophethood. The contradiction is used to cast doubt on revelation.

Guided Path
1
Read the dilemma and first branch
Start by reading the opening lines that set up the dilemma and then focus on the first branch where rational judgment is accepted.
Notice the clear logical structure: 'either...or...'
2
Understand the scenario of the devoted person
The objectors describe a person utterly devoted to God—free from worldly concerns, immersed in divine knowledge. Reason would judge such a person as saved if he dies.
This is meant to be an intuitive rational judgment.
3
Identify the contradiction
Prophets declare that such a person, if he neglects prophethood, is an eternal unbeliever. Thus reason and revelation give opposite verdicts, leading to a contradiction.
The objectors conclude that since reason is valid, revelation must be false.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الشبهة الثانية للقوم : قالوا : إن حكم العقل في التحسين والتقبيح . إما أن يكون معتبراً ، وإما أن لا يكون ، وعلى التقديرين ، فالقول بالنبوة : مشكل . أما القسم الأول : وهو أن يكون التقدير : أن يكون حكم العقل في التحسين والتقبيح مقبولاً ، فنقول : إنه متى كان الأمر كذلك ، كانت بدائه العقول قاضية بأن الإنسان إذا كان قلبه خالياً عن الالتفات إلى الدنيا وشهواتها ، وكان خالياً عن الاشتغال بغير الله تعالى ، وكان غريقاً في نور معرفة الله تعالى، وفي ذكره ، فهذا الإنسان إذا مات ، وجب أن يكون من السعداء الأبرار ، لكنا نرى أن الأنبياء يقولون : إن من كان حاله على ما ذكرناه ، ثم إنه غفل عن الإقرار بالنبوة والرسالة ، أو كان متوقفاً فيه ، كان كافراً من أهل العذاب الدائم ، والعقاب المخلد ، ولما ثبت أن حكم العقل في الإنسان المذكور أن يكون من السعداء الأبرار ، وثبت أن حكم الشرع فيه أن يكون من الأشقياء الكفار ، ثبت : أن حكم الشرع مخالف لحكم العقل ، ولما كان التقدير في هذا القسم أن يكون حكم العقل في التحسين والتقبيح معتبراً ، وجب أن يكون ضده مردوداً باطلا . وأما القسم الثاني : وهو أن يكون التقدير : هو أن يكون حكم العقل في التحسين والتقبيح مردوداً باطلا . فنقول : يبطل القول بالنبوة والرسالة، لأن على هذا التقدير لا يصح من الله تعالى إظهار المعجز على يد الكاذب ولا منه أيضاً أن يكذب في وعده ووعيده. وعلى هذا التقدير ، فإنه يبطل القول بالنبوة والرسالة.
The second objection of the people: They said: The judgment of reason concerning good and evil; either it is considered valid, or it is not considered valid. On both suppositions, the claim of prophethood is problematic. As for the first division: that the supposition is that the judgment of reason concerning good and evil is accepted. We say: When this is the case, the primary [dictates] of minds decree that the person whose heart is devoid of worldly pursuits and cravings, and is devoid of preoccupation with other than God exalted, and is immersed in the light of knowledge of God exalted and in His remembrance, this person, if he dies, must be among the blessed righteous. But we see the prophets say: Whoever is in the state we described, yet neglects to affirm prophethood and messengership, or is hesitant about it, is an unbeliever, among the people of eternal torment and everlasting punishment. Since it is established that the judgment of reason concerning the mentioned person is that he is among the blessed righteous, and it is established that the judgment of the Law concerning him is that he is among the wretched unbelievers, it is established that the judgment of the Law contradicts the judgment of reason. And since the supposition in this division is that the judgment of reason concerning good and evil is considered valid, it follows that its opposite is rejected and false. As for the second division: that the supposition is that the judgment of reason concerning good and evil is rejected and false. We say: It invalidates the claim of prophethood and messengership, because on this supposition it is not valid for God exalted to show a miracle at the hand of a liar, nor also for Him to lie in His promise and threat. On this supposition, the claim of prophethood and messengership is invalidated.
Source pages p. 90

Commentary
This passage presents the second objection against prophethood. The objectors argue that reason's judgment about good and evil (taḥsīn wa taqbīḥ) leads to a dilemma. They first consider the case where rational judgment is accepted. In that case, reason alone would deem a person who is purely devoted to God and dies without explicitly affirming prophethood as saved, but revelation declares such a person an eternal unbeliever—thus revelation contradicts reason. Since reason is accepted, revelation must be false. Second, if rational judgment is rejected, then it cannot be used to attest to the truth of prophethood through miracles, nor can it ensure God's truthfulness in promises and threats. Hence, both alternatives lead to the invalidity of prophethood.

Key Terms
حكم العقل
التحسين والتقبيح
الشرع
معتبر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) He is an unbeliever.
B) He is among the blessed righteous.
C) His fate is unknown.
D) He is a hypocrite.

Key Takeaway
Under the assumption that reason's judgment is valid, the objectors claim that revelation contradicts reason, thus prophethood is undermined.

Second Horn: Rejecting Rational Judgment Undermines Prophethood
Second horn: rejecting rational judgment invalidates the foundations of prophethood.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how rejecting rational judgment about good and evil leads to the invalidation of prophethood, according to the objectors.

Core Idea
Without reason, miracles and God's truthfulness become meaningless, so prophethood collapses.

This lesson covers the second branch of the dilemma. If rational judgment about good and evil is rejected, then the rational grounds for confirming prophethood—such as verifying miracles and trusting God's promises—are lost. Consequently, prophethood cannot be established.

Guided Path
1
Read the second branch
Focus on the second part of the passage where the objectors assume rational judgment is rejected (مردود).
Notice the shift from the first branch.
2
Understand the consequence for miracles
The objectors argue that if reason's judgment is not valid, then it cannot be used to attest that a miracle is truly from God (i.e., that God would not support a liar with a miracle).
This undermines the proof of prophethood through miracles.
3
Understand the consequence for God's truthfulness
Similarly, without rational judgment, there is no basis to say God cannot lie in His promises and threats, which are essential for prophethood.
Thus, the entire concept of prophethood becomes baseless.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Third Objection: Anthropomorphism in Laws
Author presents the third objection: laws contain anthropomorphism, using Islam as example, and begins rebuttal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third objection against prophethood—that religious laws contain anthropomorphism—and the beginning of its rebuttal.

Core Idea
Anthropomorphism in scripture is a key argument against prophethood, but it can be refuted.

In this passage, the author presents the third objection raised by skeptics: that the revealed laws are filled with anthropomorphic descriptions of God, which lead to ignorance. The objectors use Islam as an example, pointing to the Qur'an and hadith. The author then begins his response by rejecting the excuse of metaphor, arguing that the texts are too explicit. We'll examine this objection and the start of its refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Notice the structure: the objection, the proof, the example, and the rebuttal.
The passage ends mid-sentence; this is just the beginning of the author's response.
2
Identify the objection and its first proof
The objectors claim laws contain false matters (bāṭila). The first proof is that anthropomorphism (tashbīh) dominates, causing ignorance of God, thus the messenger cannot be true.
This is the first of several 'وجوه' (proofs).
3
Focus on the example of Islam
The objectors use Islam as evidence: the Qur'an and hadith are filled with anthropomorphic expressions, leading many narrators to affirm anthropomorphism.
This is a challenge to Muslim theology.
4
Understand the beginning of the rebuttal
The author rejects the excuse that these are metaphors. He gives the first reason: figurative language has known limits, but the texts in question are explicit and cannot be interpreted away.
The second reason is cut off; it would follow on the next page.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والشبهة الثالثة : قالوا إنا وجدنا هذه الشرائع مشتملة على أمور باطلة ، فكان القول بها باطلا . بيان الأول من وجوه : الأول : إن القول بالتشبيه غالب على الشرائع ، وذلك يوجب الجهل بالله تعالى ، والجاهل بالله - تعالى - لا يمكن أن يكون رسولاً حقاً من عند الله - تعالى - وإنما قلنا : إن القول بالتشبيه غالب على الشرائع ، وهو لأنا نبين حصول هذا المعنى في هذه الأديان الأربعة الظاهرة . أما دين الإسلام : فالقول بالتشبيه ظاهر في القرآن . وأما الأحاديث فإنها مملوءة من ذلك . ولذلك فإن أكثر من شرع في رواية الأخبار : جزم بالتشبيه وليس لقائل أن يقول : إنه إنما ذكر هذه الألفاظ على سبيل الاستعارة والمجاز . لأنا نقول : هذا باطل من وجهين : الأول : إن للتعبير عن المعاني الصحيحة ، بالعبارات المشتملة على المجازات والاستعارات ؛ حداً معيناً، وضابطاً معلوماً . فأما هذه الآيات الكثيرة، والأخبار الكثيرة ، فهي ألفاظ صريحة في الدلالة على المعاني الموجبة للتشبيه ، حتى أنا لو أردنا أن نعبر عن تلك المعاني بألفاظ أقوى منها وأكد
And the third objection: They said: We find that these laws contain false matters, so holding them is false. The explanation of the first of the proofs: The first: The claim of anthropomorphism dominates the laws, and that necessitates ignorance of God exalted, and the ignorant of God exalted cannot be a true messenger from God exalted. We say only that the claim of anthropomorphism dominates the laws because we show the occurrence of this meaning in these four apparent religions. As for the religion of Islam: The claim of anthropomorphism is apparent in the Qur'an. As for the hadiths, they are filled with that. Therefore, most of those who engaged in narrating traditions affirmed anthropomorphism. It cannot be said that these words were mentioned metaphorically and figuratively. We say: This is false for two reasons: First: Expressing correct meanings through phrases containing metaphors and figurative expressions has a specific limit and known parameter. But these many verses and many traditions are explicit words indicating meanings necessitating anthropomorphism, to the extent that if we wanted to express those meanings with stronger and more emphatic words,
Source pages p. 91

Commentary
This passage presents the third objection against prophethood. The objectors claim that the religious laws (sharā'iʿ) contain false things (umūr bāṭila), so the claim that these laws are from God is false. The first proof they give is that anthropomorphism (tashbīh) dominates the laws, which leads to ignorance of God, and a person ignorant of God cannot be a true messenger. They focus on Islam as an example, stating that anthropomorphism is apparent in the Qur'an and abundant in hadiths. They refute the argument that these expressions are metaphorical (istiʿāra) or figurative (majāz) by saying that such figurative usage has known limits, while the texts in question are clearly and explicitly anthropomorphic, even stronger expressions could not be found. The passage ends in the middle of the second reason, which likely continues.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The objection treats anthropomorphic expressions in scripture as literal, ignoring the doctrine of tanzīh (transcendence).
Correction: The author argues that these expressions are not literal but have metaphorical interpretations; however, the objectors insist they are too explicit to be metaphorical.

D) They have no divine origin.

Key Takeaway
The third objection claims anthropomorphism in scripture proves laws are false, but the author rebuts by noting the explicit nature of the texts, beginning a defense of tanzīh.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

The objectors in this lesson use that tension to deny prophethood. How do you personally reconcile these verses with tanzīh? What specific step will you take to deepen your understanding of this issue, rather than ignoring the tension?
?
The author states that the anthropomorphic texts are so explicit that they cannot be dismissed as mere metaphor. Does this challenge your comfort zone? Are you willing to admit that these verses are genuinely problematic for someone who hasn't studied tafsīr? What does this say about your own responsibility to learn how to interpret them properly?
?
The objectors claim that ignorance of God leads to false prophethood. Have you ever felt ignorant about God's attributes when reading certain verses? Instead of dismissing the difficulty, what concrete action will you take this week to seek knowledge that removes that ignorance, so that your faith in the Prophet ﷺ is strengthened?

The Prophets' Supposed Neglect of Theology
Argues prophets neglected theology for worldly aims.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third aspect of the critique of prophethood, which claims prophets neglected theological discussions and focused on worldly objectives.

Core Idea
Prophets are accused of abandoning theology for worldly gain.

This lesson introduces the third objection against prophethood: the claim that prophets did not seriously investigate God's essence, attributes, or the soul. Instead, they emphasized new laws and abrogated previous ones. The passage argues that this leniency on high matters indicates a worldly purpose.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the two parts: what prophets neglected and what they emphasized.
The passage is a single argument with a premise and conclusion.
2
Identify the neglected topics
List the theological topics the passage claims prophets ignored: God's essence, attributes, modal categories, actions, and the soul's origination and continuance.
These are central to Islamic theology but the objection asserts they were overlooked.
3
Identify the emphasized topics
Find what the passage says prophets focused on: establishing new laws and abrogating previous ones.
The contrast is meant to show a worldly orientation.
4
Understand the conclusion
The author infers that the prophets' priorities indicate the true purpose of their religions is worldly demands.
This is a polemical rejection of prophethood.
5
Reflect on the critique's validity
Consider whether this critique is consistent with Islamic teachings, which affirm that prophets taught both theology and law.
The misconception field will address this.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه الثالث : إنه ما ظهر من الأنبياء سعي تام في البحث عن ذات الله تعالى وصفاته ، وكيفية ما يجب ويجوز ويستحيل عليه ، وكيفية أفعاله . ولم يتكلموا في إثبات النفس وحدوثها وبقائها ، بل تركوا هذه الأصول بالكلية ، وأكثروا المبالغة في تقرير موضوعاتهم الجديدة ، وفي نسخ موضوعات المتقدمين . إذا ثبت هذا ، فنقول : إطباقهم على التساهل في الأمور العاليـة العظيمة ، وعلى التشديد في الأمور الهيئة (١) يدل على أن المطلوب من هذه الشرائع : تقرير مطالب الدنيا .
The third aspect: That there did not appear from the prophets a complete striving in researching the essence (dhat) of God, may He be exalted, and His attributes (sifat), and the modality of what is necessary, possible, and impossible for Him, and the modality of His actions. And they did not speak about establishing the soul (nafs), its origination (huduth) and its continuance (baqa'), rather they abandoned these principles entirely. And they greatly exaggerated in establishing their new topics, and in abrogating the topics of the predecessors. When this is established, we say: Their consensus on leniency in the great high matters, and on strictness in the lowly matters (al-umur al-hiyya) indicates that the purpose of these religions is to establish worldly demands.
Source pages p. 92

Commentary
This passage presents the third objection (al-wajh al-thalith). It claims that the prophets did not seriously investigate the essence and attributes of God, nor the modal categories (necessary, possible, impossible) regarding Him, nor His actions. They also did not discuss the existence, origination, and continuance of the soul. Instead, they focused on establishing their new laws and abrogating previous ones. The author then infers that the prophets' leniency on these great theological matters and strictness on trivial worldly matters proves that the aim of these religions is only to secure worldly benefits (tahsil mutalib al-dunya). This is a polemical rejection of prophethood, arguing that true divine guidance would prioritize higher knowledge.

الواجب
الجائز
المستحيل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Prophets never discussed theology but only worldly laws.
Correction: Islamic teachings show that prophets conveyed both theological truths (e.g., tawhid, resurrection) and legal guidance. The objection selectively ignores the theological content.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
The third objection claims prophets neglected theology and focused on worldly laws, but this critique overlooks the theological core of prophetic messages.

Fifth Objection: Knowledge of God and Worldly Benefits Independent of Messengers
Presents the fifth objection against the necessity of following a messenger

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the fifth objection against the necessity of following a specific messenger, which claims that knowledge of God and worldly benefits are attainable without prophetic guidance.

Core Idea
Prophetic guidance is deemed unnecessary by arguing that core benefits are achievable without it.

This lesson examines the fifth and final objection presented against the necessity of following a particular messenger. The objection asserts that the only things that truly matter—knowledge of God and worldly benefits—can be attained without adhering to any messenger. This challenges the very purpose of prophethood. Let's explore the reasoning behind this claim.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to grasp the structure of the objection.
Notice that the objection distinguishes two types of benefits: knowledge of God and worldly gains.
2
Identify the core claim
Find the sentence that states that both matters are not dependent on following a specific messenger. This is the central thesis.
3
Analyze the evidence
The objection supports its claim by pointing to those who abandoned following messengers yet attained these benefits. Consider what this evidence assumes.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
The objection concludes that no benefit depends on following messengers. Ask yourself if this conclusion is valid from an Islamic perspective.
This will prepare you to understand the refutation in subsequent lessons.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه الخامس : وهو أن المعتبر إما معرفة الله تعالى بذاته وصفاته ومعرفة اليوم الآخر . وإما تحصيل مصالح المعاش . وكلا الأمرين غير موقوف على متابعة الرسول المعين ، لأن الذين تركوا هذه المتابعة ، فقد فازوا بهذين النوعين من المصالح . فثبت : أنه لا يتوقف شيء من المصالح على متابعتهم فهذا منتهى القول في حكاية (4) شبهات الطاعنين في النبوات
The fifth aspect: It is that the consideration (al-muʿtabar) is either knowledge of God, Exalted is He, in His essence and attributes, and knowledge of the Last Day; or the acquisition of worldly benefits. And both matters are not dependent on following the specific messenger (al-rasul al-muʿayyan), because those who abandoned this following have attained these two types of benefits. So it is established: that nothing of the benefits depends on following them. So this is the end of the statement regarding the narration of the doubts (shubuhat) of the attackers on prophethoods.
Source pages p. 93

Commentary
This passage presents the fifth and final objection (al-wajh al-khamis) against the necessity of following a particular messenger. The author argues that the only things that truly matter (al-muʿtabar) are: (1) knowledge of God (His essence and attributes) and knowledge of the Last Day, and (2) securing worldly benefits. Both of these, it is claimed, are attainable without following any specific messenger, since those who have not followed messengers have still achieved them. Therefore, no real benefit depends on following the messengers. The passage then concludes the entire section on narrating the doubts (shubuhat) of those who attack prophethood (al-taʿineen fi al-nubuwwat). This is a summary objection that denies the necessity of prophetic guidance.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One can achieve complete knowledge of God and worldly success without any divine revelation or prophetic guidance.
Correction: Islamic belief holds that while some knowledge of God is possible through reason, detailed knowledge of His attributes, will, and the afterlife requires revelation. Worldly benefits are also guided by prophetic teachings for justice and morality.

Checkpoint

A) They require following a specific messenger.
B) They are independent of following any messenger.
C) Only knowledge of God requires a messenger.
D) Worldly benefits require a messenger.

Key Takeaway
The fifth objection denies the necessity of a specific messenger by claiming that the core benefits of religion and worldly life are attainable without following one.

The Fourth Objection: Killing and Wealth Seem Irrational
Presents the fourth objection against prophethood and its refutation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the fourth objection against prophethood—that divine commands to kill seem irrational—and the response that God's wisdom transcends apparent harms.

Core Idea
God's commands transcend apparent rationality.

Critics argued that shari'ah's command to kill disbelievers contradicts rationality because God could have simply not created or ended their lives without human action. This objection and its response highlight deeper questions about divine wisdom and human benefit.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection statement
Read the opening sentence of the passage where the objection is presented: shari'ah includes obligation to kill and take wealth, which seems against reason.
Notice that the objection is not about ethics but about rational consistency with God's power.
2
Understand the dilemma
Examine the dilemma: if it was good to annihilate the disbeliever, why create him? If good to preserve him, why command killing? This challenges divine wisdom.
The objector uses a logical 'if-then' structure.
3
Follow the response
Read the response: the killer's benefit is outweighed by the greater harm to the killed (death and severe punishment). It is not more fitting to cause torment to one for another's benefit.
The response does not deny the harm but argues that divine decree is not arbitrary.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه الرابع : إن الشرائع مشتملة على التكليف بالقتل ، وأخذ المال . وهذا على خلاف العقل . لأنا نقول : خالق هذا الكافر ، كان في أول الأمر قادراً على أن لا يخلقه ، وبعد أن خلقه فهو قادر على أن يميته . فإن كان الصلاح في إفنائه وإعدامه . فلم خلقه ؟ وإن كان الصلاح في إبقائه وإحيائه . فلم أمر بقتله ؟ فإن قالوا : لم لا يجوز أن يحصل للقاتل بسببه ذلك المقتول نوع مصلحة ؟ فنقول : لكنه حصل للمقتول أعظم أنواع الضرر ، وهو القتل في الدنيا ، والوصول إلى أشد العذاب في الدنيا والآخرة ، لأجل أن يصل العبد الثاني إلى نوع من أنواع الخير والراحة. فإنه ليس السعي في تعذيب أحدهما لأجل إيصال النفع إلى الثاني . أولى من العكس .
The fourth aspect: Verily, the divine laws (shara'i') include the obligation of killing and taking wealth. And this is contrary to reason. For we say: The Creator of this disbeliever was able from the first not to create him, and after creating him He is able to cause him to die. So if the benefit was in annihilating him and eliminating him, why did He create him? And if the benefit was in preserving him and giving him life, why did He command to kill him? If they say: Why is it not possible that the killer obtains due to that killed one a type of benefit? We say: But the killed one experiences the greatest types of harm, which is killing in this world, and reaching the severest punishment in this world and the Hereafter, in order that the second servant attains a type of good and comfort. For indeed, striving to torment one of them in order to convey benefit to the other is not more appropriate than the opposite.
Source pages p. 93

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth objection of the critics of prophethood and provides an answer. The objection states that the divine laws command killing and taking wealth, which seems irrational because God could have simply not created the disbeliever or could have caused him to die without commanding his killing. The objection poses a dilemma: if it was good to annihilate him, why create him? If it was good to keep him alive, why command killing him? The imagined response from the objector is that the killer might gain some benefit. The author refutes this by pointing out that the harm to the killed person—death in this world and severe punishment in the Hereafter—is far greater than any benefit to the killer. It is not more fitting to cause torment to one to benefit another than the reverse.

Key Terms
القتل
العقل
الصلاح
الشبهات
النبوات
التكليف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the objection is simply about the morality of killing.
Correction: The objection is actually about the rational consistency of divine commands with divine power, not about whether killing is ethical.

Checkpoint
What is the core dilemma presented in the fourth objection?
A) Why must a prophet be sent?
B) If God is all-powerful, why does He command killing instead of simply causing death without human agency?
C) Why does God allow evil?
D) Is killing ever justified?

Key Takeaway
The objection challenges the apparent contradiction between God's power and His commands, but the response shows that divine wisdom considers greater goods beyond immediate harm.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever questioned a command of Allah because it seemed irrational or unfair? What does this passage teach you about your limited perspective? Will you now commit to trusting His wisdom even when you don't understand?

The First Principle: Prophethood Depends on the Free Agent
States that three principles remove all objections and begins the first principle: prophethood depends on free agent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that affirming prophethood logically depends on first establishing that God is a free agent, and that once this is established, no other effecting agent can be recognized.

Core Idea
Prophethood is a branch of the free agent principle.

This passage opens the answers to all objections. The author declares that three principles suffice to remove all doubts. He then begins the first principle: before discussing prophethood, one must affirm that God is a free agent. Without this foundation, the discussion of prophethood is invalid. Once the free agent is accepted, we must also affirm that only He brings things into existence, invalidating any other supposed agents like planets or souls.

Guided Path
1
Understand the purpose of the three principles
The author claims that mastering three principles removes all objections. Note the confident tone.
This is the overarching claim.
2
Identify the logical dependency
Notice that the first principle makes prophethood a branch (far') of affirming the free agent. This means the free agent is foundational.
Ask yourself why this dependency is necessary.
3
Analyze the exclusive effect claim
Once the free agent is accepted, the author asserts that only He brings things into existence, thereby invalidating all other potential agents.
List the other agents mentioned: planet, sphere, intellect, soul, high spirit, low spirit.
4
Examine the beginning of the proof
The first proof starts by linking God's power to the inherent possibility of things. Though incomplete, understand its direction.
This proof will be continued in subsequent lessons.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أعلم : أنا وإن بالغنا في حكاية هذه الشبهات . إلا أن العاقل إذا أحكم معرفة أصول ثلاثة ، ووقف على قوتها، زالت عنه هذه الشبهات بأسرها ، وذلك من كمال تعم الله تعالى على العباد ، حيث هداهم إلى هذه الأصول الثلاثة ، ليتوسلوا بها إلى دفع هذه الشبهات. فالأصل الأول : أن نقول : لاشك أن القول بإثبات النبوات ، فرع على القول بإثبات الفاعل المختار . فمن نازع في ذلك الأصل ، فإنه لا يجوز له الخوض في إثبات النبوات البتة . بل يجب عليه الشروع في تلك المسألة . وأما من سلم أن إله العالم فاعل مختار ، فنقول : إنا ندعي أنه لا مؤثر البتة لإخراج شيء من العدم إلى الوجود ، إلا ذلك الواحد . وإذا ثبت هذا ، فقد بطل القول بوجود مؤثر آخر سواه ، سواء قيل : إنه كوكب ، أو فلك ، أو عقل ، أو نفس ، أو روح علوي ، أو روح سفلى . والذي يدل على صحة هذا الأصل وجوه : الأول : أنه لما ثبت أنه تعالى قادر على بعض المقدورات ، وجب أن يكون ذلك الشيء ، إنما صار بحيث يصح أن يكون مقدوراً له لإمكانه . لأنا
Know: Although we have elaborated in recounting these objections, yet the intelligent person, if he masters knowledge of three principles and understands their strength, these objections will all disappear from him. And that is from the perfection of Allah's favor upon His servants, as He guided them to these three principles, so that they may use them to repel these objections. So the first principle: We say: There is no doubt that the statement affirming prophethood is a branch of the statement affirming the free agent. So whoever disputes that principle, it is not permissible for him to engage in proving prophethood at all. Rather, he must start with that issue. And as for one who accepts that the God of the world is a free agent, we say: We claim that there is absolutely no effecting agent for bringing anything from non-existence into existence except that One. And when this is established, then the statement of the existence of any other effecting agent besides Him is invalid, whether it is said: it is a planet, or a sphere, or an intellect, or a soul, or a high spirit, or a low spirit. And what indicates the soundness of this principle are proofs. The first: When it is established that He, exalted, is able over some possibles, it must be that that thing became such that it is possible for it to be within His power due to its possibility. Because we...
Source pages p. 94

Commentary
This passage opens the fifteenth chapter on answers to the objections. The author states that despite elaborating the objections, three principles suffice to remove all doubts. He then begins the first principle: affirming prophethood depends on proving a free agent (al-fa'il al-mukhtar). He argues that one who denies this principle cannot discuss prophethood. Once the free agent is accepted, the author claims that only He brings things into existence, invalidating any other claimed agents such as celestial bodies or souls. He then begins the first proof, which is incomplete in this text, stating that since God is able over some possibles, the thing must be possible in itself.

فاعل مختار
مؤثر

إمكان

Correction: The passage only introduces the first principle; the others follow in later sections.

Key Takeaway
The first principle for answering all objections is that prophethood logically depends on affirming God as a free agent, and once that is accepted, all other claimed agents are invalid.

The Second Principle: Certain Knowledge Despite Possibility
Introduces the second principle and the example with the tripartite division of causation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second principle for proving prophethood: that we can have certain knowledge that something did not occur even when it is logically possible.

Core Idea
Certain knowledge can coexist with logical possibility of the opposite.

The author introduces a foundational principle for establishing prophethood: the possibility of an event does not negate our certain knowledge that it did not occur. The example of seeing Zayd twice illustrates this. To fully grasp the argument, we need to understand the three possible theories of causation that underpin the possibility of replacement. This lesson presents the principle and begins the analysis of causation.

Guided Path
1
Read the principle statement
Read the first two sentences carefully. Note the key terms: 'known to be permissible' (معلوم الجواز) and 'certainty and decisiveness' (الجزم والقطع).
The principle is the core claim to be proven.
2
Examine the example of Zayd
Read the example of seeing Zayd, closing the eye, and seeing again. The author claims we know by necessity it's the same person, despite the possibility of replacement.
This example makes the abstract principle concrete.
3
Understand the tripartite division
The author introduces three possible theories of causation: no cause, necessitating cause, or free agent. This framework is used to show that under any theory, replacement is logically possible.
This sets up the proof that certain knowledge is not undermined by possibility.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأصل الثاني من الأصول التي عليها مدار إثبات النبوات: أنه لا أن يكون الشيء معلوم الجواز والإمكان، ومع ذلك فإنه يكون الجزم والقطع حاصلاً بأنه لم يوجد، ولم يحصل.

وبيانه: أني إذا رأيت زيداً، ثم غمضت العين، ثم إني نظرت إليه في المرة الثانية، فإني أعلم بالضرورة أن هذا الذي أراه ثانياً، عين الذي رأيته أولاً، مع أن التجويز القطعي قائم بكونه مغايراً لذلك الأول. وبيانه: أن الحادث إما أن لا يفتقر إلى المؤثر أو يفتقر إليه، والقسم الثاني فمؤثر العالم إما أن يكون موجباً أو مختاراً. وظاهر أنه ليس وراء هذه الأقسام الثلاثة قسم البتة.

أما القسم الأول: وهو أن يقال: لا مؤثر لهذه الحوادث. فعلى هذا التقدير يكون الوجود بعد العدم، والعدم بعد الوجود: حاصلا بمحض الاتفاق من غير سبب أصلا. وإذا جاز ذلك، جاز أيضاً أن يقال: اتفق أن ذلك الشخص الأول قد عدم في تلك اللحظة اللطيفة، وحصل الشخص الثاني على سبيل الاتفاق. لأن القول بالاتفاق، لما كان واقعاً في بعض الصور، كان احتماله قائماً في كل الصور.

وأما القسم الثاني: وهو أن يقال: مؤثر العالم موجب بالذات، فذلك الاحتمال أيضاً، قائم، لأن فيضان الآثار الحادثة عن ذلك المبدأ القديم، لابد وأن يكون موقوفاً على حصول شكل فلكي خاص. وإذا ثبت هذا فنقول: لم لا يجوز أن يقال: إنه حصل شكل فلكي عجيب غريب، اقتضي فناء ذلك الشخص الأول في تلك اللحظة اللطيفة، وحصل شيء آخر يماثله دفعة؟

وأما القسم الثالث: وهو أن يقال: مؤثر العالم فاعل مختار. فعلى هذا التقدير: القول بأنه أفنى الشخص الأول في تلك اللحظة اللطيفة، وأوجد شخصاً آخر يماثله من كل الوجوه: محتمل. فثبت بهذا البرهان القاطع: أن تجويز هذا المعنى قائم على جميع التقديرات. ثم إنا مع علمنا بهذا التجويز، نعلم بالضرورة أن هذا الشخص الذي أراه في هذه اللحظة هو عين الشخص الذي رأيته قبل ذلك بلحظة أو بيوم أو بشهر.

واعلم أنك إذا عرفت حقيقة الحال في هذا المثال، أمكنك معرفة أمثلة
The second principle among the principles upon which rests the proof of prophecies: that a thing may be known to be permissible and possible, yet certainty and decisiveness obtain that it did not occur and did not happen.

Its explanation: that if I see Zayd, then close my eye, then look at him a second time, I know by necessity that what I see second is the very same as what I saw first, even though a categorical possibility stands that it is different from that first. Its explanation: that the event either does not need an effector or needs it, and the second section: the effector of the world is either necessitating (mujib) or choosing (mukhtar). And it is apparent that there is no division beyond these three divisions at all.

As for the first division: that it is said there is no effector for these events. On this assumption, existence after non-existence and non-existence after existence occur by mere coincidence without any cause. And if that is permissible, then it is also permissible to say: it happened that that first person ceased to exist in that subtle moment, and a second person came into existence by coincidence. Because the assertion of coincidence, since it occurs in some cases, its probability stands in all cases.

As for the second division: that it is said the effector of the world is a necessitating cause by essence, then that probability also stands, because the emanation of temporal effects from that eternal principle must be dependent upon the occurrence of a particular celestial configuration. And if this is established, we say: why is it not permissible to say that a strange and wonderful celestial configuration occurred, necessitating the annihilation of that first person in that subtle moment, and another thing similar to him came into existence at once?

As for the third division: that it is said the effector of the world is a free agent (fa'il mukhtar). On this assumption, the statement that He annihilated the first person in that subtle moment and brought into existence another person similar to him in all respects is possible. Thus it is established by this decisive proof that the possibility of this meaning stands under all assumptions. Then, even with our knowledge of this possibility, we know by necessity that this person whom I see in this moment is the very person whom I saw a moment or a day or a month before.

And know that if you understand the reality of the case in this example, you can understand other examples.
Source pages p. 98

Commentary
This passage presents the second principle for proving prophethood: that one can have certain knowledge that something did not occur despite its logical possibility. The author uses the example of seeing Zayd, closing one's eye, and seeing him again. We know with necessity (darura) that it is the same person, even though it is logically possible (jawaaz) that he was replaced. The author then categorizes all possible theories of causation into three divisions: (1) no cause (pure accident), (2) a necessary cause (a necessitating principle like the celestial spheres), and (3) a free agent (a choosing creator). Under each division, the possibility of replacement exists: under accident, it could be coincidence; under necessity, a special configuration could cause a replacement; under free will, God could directly replace the person. Yet despite this possibility, we still know with certainty that the person is the same. This proves that certain knowledge is possible even where logical alternatives exist, which is essential for establishing prophethood (since we need certainty that prophets are truthful despite possible deception). The author concludes that understanding this example helps in grasping other similar cases.

Key Terms
الأصل الثاني
الجزم والقطع
التجويز القطعي
الموجب
المختار

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the argument denies the possibility of change altogether.
Correction: The argument affirms the logical possibility of change while asserting our necessary knowledge of identity.

Checkpoint
What is the main claim of the second principle?
A) That logical possibility always leads to doubt.
B) That certain knowledge can coexist with logical possibility.
C) That we cannot be certain of anything.
D) That all events are caused.

Key Takeaway
The second principle shows that logical possibility does not automatically negate certain knowledge, which is essential for proving prophethood.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your daily life, you act with certainty about many things (e.g., that your car will start, that your friend is the same person). Yet logically, alternatives are possible. Does your certainty ever waver when you consider the possibility? If not, why do you suddenly demand absolute impossibility when it comes to prophethood?
?
The Zayd example shows you know the same person is before you despite a categorical possibility of change. Think of a time you doubted a clear truth because of a mere possibility. What would it cost you to trust the certainty Allah has placed in your fitra?
?
You accept this principle for everyday knowledge—so what prevents you from accepting it for the proof of prophethood? Is it intellectual honesty, or is it a deeper unwillingness to submit? Let this question unsettle you until you answer it sincerely before Allah.

7 min
The Possibility of Replacement Under Each Theory of Causation
Details the three divisions of causation and shows replacement is possible under each.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author demonstrates that the possibility of replacement (imagining that Zayd was replaced) is logically possible under each of the three theories of causation.

Core Idea
No matter how you explain causation, replacement is always logically possible.

In the previous lesson, we learned the second principle and the three-fold division of causation. Now the author shows that under each division—no cause, necessitating cause, or free agent—the possibility of Zayd being replaced is logically valid. This is a crucial step in the argument that certain knowledge can persist despite such possibilities.

Guided Path
1
First division: No cause
Read the section on the first division. Understand why pure accident allows the possibility of replacement.
Coincidence is the key concept here.
2
Second division: Necessitating cause
Read the section on the second division. Note how the author argues that a celestial configuration could necessitate replacement.
Even deterministic causation leaves room for replacement via unusual configurations.
3
Third division: Free agent
Read the section on the third division. The author states that a free agent (God) could directly replace the person.
This is the most straightforward possibility.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
After reading all three, notice that the possibility is established under every theory, making it a universal logical possibility.

?

?

?

Certain Knowledge Persists Despite Universal Possibility
Concludes that certain knowledge is possible despite universal logical possibility and suggests broader application.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author concludes that we possess necessary knowledge of identity despite the universal logical possibility of replacement, and how this principle applies to proving prophethood.

Core Idea
Universal logical possibility does not erase our necessary knowledge.

Having established that the possibility of Zayd's replacement stands under every theory of causation, the author now draws the conclusion: despite this universal possibility, we still know with necessity that the person we see is the same as before. This shows that certain knowledge is not undermined by mere logical possibility. The author also hints at the broader application of this principle.

Guided Path
1
Read the concluding argument
Read the paragraph starting with 'فثبت بهذا البرهان القاطع'. Notice the phrase 'ثم إنا مع علمنا بهذا التجويز، نعلم بالضرورة'.
This is the key conclusion.
2
Understand the implication
The author concludes that this principle is a 'decisive proof' (برهان قاطع) that certain knowledge can coexist with logical possibility.
The proof is decisive because it covers all theories.
3
Connect to prophethood
Reflect on why this principle is crucial for proving prophethood: we need to be certain that a prophet is truthful despite the logical possibility of deception.
This is the broader purpose of the second principle.
4
General application
The author says that understanding this example unlocks understanding of other examples. Think about other areas where this principle applies.
The principle extends beyond this specific case.

?

?

?

Applying Principles to Prove Prophethood through Miracles
Applying two principles to prove prophethood via miracles with the Moses example

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author uses the first two principles (only God creates miracles and possibilities do not negate certainty) to argue that miracles provide certain proof of prophethood, using the example of Moses and the mountain.

Core Idea
Miracles give certain knowledge of prophethood because their context leaves no alternative purpose.

This lesson shows how the three principles established earlier are applied to the question of prophethood. The author argues that miracles, when witnessed in context, yield necessary knowledge that they are sent to confirm a prophet. He uses the striking example of Moses suspending a mountain over the Israelites to demonstrate how observable conditions can generate certainty despite theoretical possibilities.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and note the two principles used
The author refers to the first and second principles. Identify what they are: first, only God creates miracles; second, God creates miracles only for confirmation. Observe how they are introduced.
2
Identify the objection and the author's response
The objection is that God might have created the miracle for other purposes. The author responds that such possibilities do not prevent certainty, as he previously explained in the section on divided property.
3
Examine the example of Moses
Read the example carefully: Moses asked God to suspend the mountain over the Israelites. They noticed that it receded when they obeyed and approached when they rebelled. This gave them necessary knowledge that the miracle was meant to compel obedience.
The example illustrates how context (the correlation between their actions and the mountain's movement) creates certainty.
4
Understand how the principles combine for certainty
The first principle establishes that only God created the miracle; the second that the purpose was confirmation. Together they yield certain knowledge that the prophet is truthful. The passage ends mid-sentence, but the conclusion is clear.
The argument is that miracles are not just any event – they are tied to the claim of prophethood in a way that leaves no reasonable doubt.

وإذا عرفت هذه الأصول الثلاثة، فحينئذ يظهر القول بصحة النبوات، ظهوراً، لا يبقى فيها شك ولا شبهة. وتقريره: أن نقول: فاعل جميع المعجزات هو الله تعالى لأنا بينا في الأصل الأول أنه لا مؤثر ولا موجد ولا مكون إلا الله تعالى. ثم نقول: وإنه تعالى إنما خلقها لأجل تصديق هذا المدعي: فأما قولهم: إنه يجوز أن يكون قد خلقها لأغراض أخرى. فنقول: ذلك التجويز قائم في الجملة. لأنا نقول: قد بينا أن قيام تجويز الوجوه الكثيرة قد لا يمنع من حصول القطع والجزم بواحد منها، كما ذكرناه في المثال في القسم: أن الأمر في هذه المسألة كذلك. فندعي والدليل عليه: أن موسى - عليه السلام - لما أمر القوم ببعض التكاليف، فأبوا، وأصروا طلب من الله - تعالى - أن يوقف الجبل فوق رؤوسهم. ثم إنهم كانوا يشاهدون أنهم كلما قصدوا الطاعة والامتثال، فذلك الجبل، يتباعد عن رؤوسهم، وكلما قصدوا العود إلى العتو والإصرار والكفر. فذلك الجبل يقرب منهم بحيث خافوا من وقوعه عليهم. ومن المعلوم أن حصول هذه الحالة على هذا الوجه، يفيد العلم الضروري بأنه تعالى إنما أوقف الجبل فوق رؤوسهم، لأجل أن يتبادروا إلى الطاعة والامتثال. وتجويز أن يكون الغرض من إظلال ذلك الجبل شيئاً آخر سوى هذا المقصود، لا يقدح في حصول العلم الضروري، بأنه لا مقصود منه سوى ذلك. فإنا قد بينا أنه قد يحصل القطع والجزم مع قيام مثل هذا التجويز. فثبت بالأصل الأول: أن خالق كل المعجزات هو الله تعالى، وثبت بالأصل الثاني: أنه لا حكمة الله - تعالى - في خلق تلك المعجزات، إلا التصديق. وهذا يفيد العلم اليقيني بأن الله تعالى إنما
And if you know these three principles, then the statement on the validity of prophethoods appears, an appearance in which no doubt or uncertainty remains. Its exposition: we say: the agent of all miracles is God Most High, because we showed in the first principle that there is no effect, nor bringer into existence, nor creator except God Most High. Then we say: He Most High only created them for the confirmation of this claimant. As for their saying: it is possible that He created them for other purposes. We say: that possibility stands in general. Because we say: we have shown that the existence of the possibility of many aspects may not prevent the occurrence of certainty and decisiveness about one of them, as we mentioned in the example in the section: that the matter in this question is likewise. So we claim and the proof for it is: that Moses - peace be upon him - when he commanded the people with some duties, they refused and persisted, he asked God Most High to suspend the mountain over their heads. Then they were witnessing that whenever they intended obedience and compliance, that mountain would distance itself from their heads, and whenever they intended to return to insolence, persistence, and disbelief, that mountain would draw near to them so that they feared it falling upon them. And it is known that the occurrence of this state in this manner yields necessary knowledge that He Most High suspended the mountain over their heads only in order that they hasten to obedience and compliance. And the possibility that the purpose of overshadowing them with that mountain is something other than this intention does not impair the occurrence of necessary knowledge that there is no intention besides that. For we have shown that certainty and decisiveness may occur while such a possibility exists. So it is established by the first principle that the creator of all miracles is God Most High, and it is established by the second principle that there is no wisdom of God Most High in creating those miracles except confirmation. And this yields certain knowledge that God Most High indeed...
Source pages p. 100

Commentary
This passage applies the three previously established principles to prove the validity of prophethood. The three principles are: (1) Only God creates and brings things into existence, (2) The possibility of alternative explanations does not negate certainty when evidence is strong, and (3) Rational good and evil do not apply to God's actions (though this third principle is not explicitly used here). The author argues that miracles are created by God solely to confirm the truthfulness of a prophet. He anticipates the objection that God might have created the miracle for other purposes, but responds that such possibilities do not prevent certainty when the evidence points overwhelmingly to one purpose. He then provides the example of the Prophet Moses: when the Israelites disobeyed, Moses asked God to suspend a mountain over them. They observed that whenever they obeyed, the mountain receded, and whenever they rebelled, it approached. This gave them necessary knowledge that the miracle was intended to compel obedience. The same logic applies to any miracle: the context and timing make it certain that the purpose is confirmation of the prophet. The passage ends mid-sentence, but the intended conclusion is clear: such miracles provide certain knowledge of the truthfulness of the prophet.

Proof of Prophethood Using the Three Principles
The author demonstrates how the three principles prove the truth of prophethood through miracles.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the three rational principles establish the validity of prophethood through miracles, achieving certainty despite possible alternative explanations.

Core Idea
Miracles produce certainty of prophethood, even if other explanations are conceivable.

In this passage, the author completes his earlier argument by demonstrating how the three principles—God as sole creator, the purpose of His actions, and the impossibility of lying for God—combine to prove the truth of prophethood. Using a compelling example from the story of Moses, he shows that direct experience of a miracle yields necessary knowledge of its purpose, even when abstract possibilities of other purposes exist. This lesson unpacks that argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Understand the three principles
Recall that the first principle establishes God as the sole creator, the second clarifies that God's actions have a purpose (usually confirmation), and the third affirms that lying is impossible for God. This groundwork is essential for the proof.
If you haven't studied the principles, review the previous lesson.
2
Follow the logical exposition
Read the passage carefully. Notice how the author applies the first two principles to miracles: all miracles are from God, and their purpose is to confirm the claimant. Then he addresses an objection.
Look for the shift from positive argument to refutation.
3
Examine the objection and response
The objection is that God might have other purposes for miracles. The author responds by saying that such possibilities do not negate certainty, using the mountain example to illustrate that direct experience yields necessary knowledge.
Focus on the example: it shows how the Israelites knew the mountain's purpose through direct observation.
4
Grasp the concept of necessary knowledge
Understand that 'necessary knowledge' (al-ʿilm al-ḍarūrī) is immediate and certain, not based on reasoning. The mountain example produces such knowledge despite abstract alternatives.
This is key: certainty can coexist with mere possibility.
5
Appreciate the final conclusion
The author concludes that from the first two principles we know that miracles are from God for confirmation, and from the third that God cannot lie, so miracles decisively prove the truthfulness of prophets.
Reflect on how all three principles work together.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإذا عرفت هذه الأصول الثلاثة، فحينئذ يظهر القول بصحة النبوات، ظهوراً، لا يبقى فيها شك ولا شبهة. وتقريره: أن نقول: فاعل جميع المعجزات هو الله تعالى لأنا بينا في الأصل الأول أنه لا مؤثر ولا موجد ولا مكون إلا الله تعالى ثم نقول: وإنه تعالى إنما خلقها لأجل تصديق هذا المدعي: فأما قولهم: إنه يجوز أن يكون قد خلقها لأغراض أخرى. فنقول: ذلك التجويز قائم في الجملة. لأنا نقول: قد بينا أن قيام تجويز الوجوه الكثيرة قد لا يمنع من حصول القطع والجزم بواحد منها، كما ذكرناه في المثال في القسم: أن الأمر في هذه المسألة كذلك فندعي والدليل عليه: أن موسى - عليه السلام - لما أمر القوم ببعض التكاليف، فأبوا، وأصروا طلب من الله - تعالى - أن يوقف الجبل فوق رؤوسهم. ثم إنهم كانوا يشاهدون أنهم كلما قصدوا الطاعة والامتثال، فذلك الجبل، يتباعد عن رؤوسهم، وكلما قصدوا العود إلى العتو والإصرار والكفر. فذلك الجبل يقرب منهم بحيث خافوا من وقوعه عليهم. ومن المعلوم أن حصول هذه الحالة على هذا الوجه، يفيد العلم الضروري بأنه تعالى إنما أوقف الجبل فوق رؤوسهم، لأجل أن يتبادروا إلى الطاعة والامتثال. وتجويز أن يكون الغرض من إظلال ذلك الجبل شيئاً آخر سوى هذا المقصود، لا يقدح في حصول العلم الضروري، بأنه لا مقصود منه سوى ذلك. فإنا قد بينا أنه قد يحصل القطع والجزم مع قيام مثل هذا التجويز. فثبت بالأصل الأول: أن خالق كل المعجزات هو الله تعالى، وثبت بالأصل الثاني: أنه لا حكمة الله - تعالى - في خلق تلك المعجزات، إلا التصديق. وهذا يفيد العلم اليقيني بأن الله تعالى إنما خلق هذه المعجزات لأجل تصديق الأنبياء والمرسل. والعلم الضروري، حاصل بأن الكذب على الله - تعالى - محال. لأنه صفة نقص. وشهادة الفطرة دالة على أن صفة النقص محال على الله - تعالى - وعند هذا يحصل الجزم واليقين بأن ظهور المعجزات يدل على صدق الأنبياء عليهم السلام.
And when you have known these three principles, then the statement concerning the validity of prophethood becomes evident, an appearance in which there remains no doubt nor ambiguity. And its exposition: we say: The agent of all miracles is God Most High, because we showed in the first principle that there is no effect nor producer nor efficient except God Most High. Then we say: And He Most High only created them for the purpose of confirming this claimant. As for their saying: It is possible that He created them for other purposes. We say: That possibility exists in general. Because we say: We have shown that the existence of a possibility of many aspects may not prevent the attainment of certainty and decisiveness about one of them, as we mentioned in the example in the section: that the matter in this question is likewise. So we claim, and the evidence for it: that Moses—peace be upon him—when he commanded the people with some obligations, they refused and persisted, he asked God Most High to suspend the mountain over their heads. Then they would observe that whenever they intended obedience and compliance, that mountain would move away from their heads, and whenever they intended to return to insolence, persistence, and disbelief, that mountain would approach them such that they feared its falling upon them. And it is known that the occurrence of this state in this manner yields necessary knowledge that He Most High suspended the mountain over their heads only so that they would hasten to obedience and compliance. And the possibility that the purpose of overshadowing that mountain is something other than this intention does not detract from the attainment of necessary knowledge that there is no purpose from it other than that. For we have shown that decisiveness and certainty may be attained with the existence of such a possibility. So it is established by the first principle that the creator of all miracles is God Most High, and it is established by the second principle that there is no wisdom for God Most High in creating those miracles except confirmation. And this yields certain knowledge that God Most High only created these miracles for the purpose of confirming the prophets and messengers. And necessary knowledge exists that lying against God Most High is impossible, because it is an attribute of deficiency. And the testimony of innate nature indicates that the attribute of deficiency is impossible for God Most High. And at this point, decisiveness and certainty are obtained that the appearance of miracles indicates the truthfulness of the prophets, peace be upon them.
Source pages p. 100

Commentary
This passage is a proof demonstrating the validity of prophethood (nubuwwāt) by applying the three principles previously established. The argument proceeds in stages: First, it states that once the three principles are known, the truth of prophethood becomes evident without any doubt. Then it gives the detailed exposition (taqrīr): The first principle (God alone is the creator and agent of all things) establishes that all miracles (muʿjizāt) are created by God. The second principle (God's actions have no ulterior wisdom beyond His will, as established earlier) ensures that God creates miracles solely to confirm the claimant of prophethood. The author anticipates an objection: could God create miracles for other purposes? He responds that such a possibility does not prevent certainty, because certainty can coexist with abstract possibilities, as shown by the example of Moses and the mountain. The example: When the Israelites refused to obey, Moses asked God to suspend the mountain over them. They witnessed that the mountain would approach when they resisted and recede when they complied. This produced necessary knowledge (ʿilm ḍarūrī) that the purpose of the mountain was to compel obedience. Even if one could imagine other purposes, the direct experience yields certainty. Similarly, the miracles of prophets produce certain knowledge that God sent them to confirm truthfulness. The argument then concludes: from the first principle, God is the creator of all miracles; from the second, the only purpose is confirmation; thus miracles are sure signs of prophethood. Moreover, lying is impossible for God because it is a deficiency (ṣifat naqṣ), and innate disposition (fiṭra) testifies that deficiency cannot be attributed to God. Therefore, miracles decisively prove the truthfulness of prophets.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that any unusual event can be a miracle and prove prophethood.
Correction: The author stresses that a miracle must be coupled with a claim of prophethood and its purpose must be clearly confirmation, as shown by the mountain example.

Checkpoint

A) Because God cannot have multiple purposes.
B) Because such possibilities are rationally impossible.
C) Because certainty can coexist with abstract possibilities, as shown by the mountain example.
D) Because the miracle itself disproves other purposes.

Key Takeaway
Miracles, when coupled with a claim of prophethood and the impossibility of other purposes, produce certain knowledge of the prophet's truthfulness.

The Third Principle: Answering Objections on Abrogation and Qibla Change
Objection-and-answer using the third principle.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the third principle — that God's rulings are not subject to rational good and evil — resolves objections about abrogation and the change of qibla.

Core Idea
God's commands are not answerable to human reason.

In this concluding passage of the chapter, the author addresses two common objections: why would God abrogate a ruling, and why change the qibla from Jerusalem to the Kaaba? The answer is not to justify them rationally, but to invoke the third principle that God is sovereign and His actions are not bound by human standards of good and evil. This lesson shows how that principle dismantles doubts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objections
Read the opening sentence to find the two questions about abrogation (naskh) and change of qibla. Notice that they challenge the wisdom behind divine commands.
2
Analyze the answer
The author responds by stating that God's rulings are not dependent on rational deeming good and evil (taḥsīn al-ʿaql wa taqbīḥih). Understand why this is a decisive rebuttal: God does as He wills without needing human justification.
This is the core of the third principle.
3
Reflect on the conclusion
The passage ends by stating that knowing the three principles delivers one from such doubts. Consider how this principle alone can answer many objections.
The doxology expresses gratitude for guidance.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما قولهم بعد ذلك: ما الفائدة في النسخ؟ وما الفائدة في التوجه إلى الكعبة، دون بيت المقدس؟ فجوابه: أن نقول: أحكام الله ـ تعالى ـ غير موقوفة على تحسين العقل وتقبيحه، فيفعل الله ما يشاء، ويحكم ما يريد. فقد ظهر بما ذكرناه: أن الموقوف على معرفة هذه الأصول الثلاثة، يحصل الخلاص عن بحار تلك الشبهات والحمد لله الذي هدانا إلى الدين القويم، والصراط المستقيم. والله أعلم.
And as for their saying after that: What is the benefit in abrogation? And what is the benefit in turning towards the Kaaba instead of Jerusalem? The answer is: we say: The rulings of God Most High are not dependent on rational deeming good and deeming bad; so God does what He wills and judges what He desires. So it has become clear from what we have mentioned that whoever is made to know these three principles obtains deliverance from the oceans of those doubts. And praise be to God who guided us to the upright religion and the straight path. And God knows best.
Source pages p. 101

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer section. The objection (qauluhum) questions the wisdom (fāʾida) behind abrogation (naskh) and the change of qibla from Jerusalem to the Kaaba. The answer (jawāb) invokes the third principle: God's rulings are not dependent on rational judgments of good and evil (taḥsīn al-ʿaql wa taqbīḥih). Therefore, God is free to do as He wills and judge as He desires without needing to justify His actions according to human reason. The author then concludes that knowing these three principles delivers one from all such doubts. The passage ends with a doxology praising God for guidance. This shows how the third principle functions as a decisive rebuttal against objections that rely on rational expectations of divine conduct.

Correction: The author shows that God’s rulings are not subject to human reason; He does as He wills, so abrogation and change are expressions of His sovereignty, not flaws.

Key Takeaway

Miracles Produce Certainty of Prophethood
The author explains that miracles lead to necessary knowledge of prophethood.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how miracles provide necessary knowledge (al-ʿilm al-ḍarūrī) and certainty of prophetic truthfulness.

Core Idea
Miracles generate certainty because they are backed by Allah and cannot be associated with falsehood.

The author begins by stating that miracles are created specifically to verify prophets. He then explains that necessary reason and our innate disposition both testify that lying is impossible for Allah, so when a miracle occurs, we can be certain the prophet is truthful.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on miracles
Read the Arabic passage once to get the flow, then read the translation. Notice the logical progression from purpose to necessary knowledge to fitra to certainty.
2
Understand necessary knowledge
Identify what the author means by 'necessary knowledge' (al-ʿilm al-ḍarūrī) and why it establishes the impossibility of lying about Allah.
Necessary knowledge is immediate and cannot be doubted.
3
Connect fitra with the impossibility of defects
Consider why our innate disposition (fitra) also points to the same conclusion: that deficiency cannot be attributed to Allah.
4
See how certainty follows
Understand that since a miracle is an extraordinary act of Allah at the hands of a prophet, and Allah cannot lie, the prophet must be truthful.
This is the core argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
خلق هذه المعجزات لأجل تصديق الأنبياء والمرسل. والعلم الضروري حاصل بأن الكذب على الله تعالى محال، لأنه صفة نقص. وشهادة الفطرة دالة على أن صفة النقص محال على الله تعالى. وعند هذا يحصل الجزم واليقين بأن ظهور المعجزات يدل على صدق الأنبياء عليهم السلام. وأما قولهم بعد ذلك: ما الفائدة في النسخ؟ وما الفائدة في التوجه إلى الكعبة، دون بيت المقدس؟ فجوابه: أن نقول: أحكام الله تعالى غير موقوفة على تحسين العقل وتقبيحه، فيفعل الله ما يشاء، ويحكم ما يريد. فقد ظهر بما ذكرناه: أن الموقوف على معرفة هذه الأصول الثلاثة، يحصل الخلاص عن بحار تلك الشبهات. والحمد لله الذي هدانا إلى الدين القويم، والصراط المستقيم. والله أعلم.
He created these miracles to verify the prophets and messengers. And necessary knowledge is obtained that lying about Allah – exalted is He – is impossible, because it is a defect. And the testimony of innate disposition indicates that defective attributes are impossible for Allah – exalted is He. At this point, certainty and conviction are obtained that the appearance of miracles indicates the truthfulness of the prophets, peace be upon them. As for their statement after that: 'What is the benefit in abrogation? And what is the benefit in facing the Ka'bah instead of Bayt al-Maqdis?' Its answer is: We say: The rulings of Allah – exalted is He – are not dependent on the moral approval of the intellect, so Allah does as He wills and decrees what He wants. So it has become clear from what we have mentioned that whoever attains knowledge of these three principles obtains deliverance from the seas of these doubts. Praise be to Allah who guided us to the upright religion and the straight path. And Allah knows best.
Source pages p. 101

Commentary
This passage concludes a discussion on prophethood by affirming the role of miracles and answering objections. First, the author states that miracles are created for the purpose of verifying prophets. Necessary knowledge (al-ʿilm al-ḍarūrī) tells us that lying about Allah is impossible because it is a deficiency. The natural disposition (fiṭra) also bears witness that deficiency cannot be attributed to Allah. Therefore, miracles produce certainty that the prophet is truthful. Then the author addresses an objection: what is the benefit of abrogation (naskh) and changing the direction of prayer from Jerusalem to the Kaaba? The answer is that Allah's rulings are not contingent upon human rational judgments of good and evil (taḥsīn al-ʿaql wa taqbīḥih). Allah does whatever He wills. Finally, the author concludes that whoever attains knowledge of the three principles (presumably the principles of tawhid, prophethood, and samʿiyyat) will escape the seas of those doubts.

Key Terms
العلم الضروري
شهادة الفطرة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that mere extraordinary events (like magic) alone prove prophethood without considering the divine context.
Correction: The passage clarifies that miracles produce certainty because they are acts of Allah witnessing the prophet's truthfulness, not just any extraordinary act.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what does the testimony of innate disposition (shahādat al-fiṭra) indicate?
A) That miracles are possible for anyone
B) That deficiency is impossible for Allah
C) That abrogation is permissible
D) That prophets are infallible

Key Takeaway
Miracles produce certainty of prophethood because they are divine acts that cannot be associated with falsehood, as Allah is free from deficiency.

?
Have you ever acted as if the Prophet's message doesn't matter in a particular area of your life? How does the certainty that his prophethood is backed by Allah challenge you to change that? What specific step will you take today to align your life with that certainty?
