The Craft of Prophethood: Calling to Allah
Definition of prophethood's craft and necessity of worldly engagement.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the primary purpose of prophethood and why some worldly involvement is necessary.

Core Idea
The core of prophethood is turning people from creation to the Creator.

Before diving into specific doctrines, the author sets the stage by defining what prophethood truly is. It is not merely a worldly office; it is a divine call to shift human focus from created things to the service of Allah. This lesson presents that definition and explains why prophets also engage with worldly affairs.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition of prophethood's craft
Read the Arabic and translation carefully. Notice how the author uses the term 'craft' (حرفة) for prophethood, implying it is a skill or vocation with a clear purpose.
2
Identify the two shifts in the call
The call involves two movements: from creation to the Truth, and from this world to the Hereafter. Understand that both are complementary.
3
Understand the qualification about worldly matters
The author adds that because people live in the world, prophets must also address worldly needs, but only to the extent necessary. This prevents misunderstanding prophethood as purely otherworldly.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أعلم : أن حرفة النبوة والرسالة عبارة عن دعوة الخلق من الاشتغال بالخلق ، إلى خدمة الحق. ومن الإقبال على الدنيا ، إلى الإقبال على الآخرة . فهذا هو المقصود الأصلي إلا أن الناس لما كانوا حاضرين في الدنيا ومحتاجين إلى مصالحها ، وجب أن يكون له خوض في هذا الباب أيضاً ، بقدر الحاجة . فنقول : خوض الرسول - عليه السلام - إما أن يكون فيهما يتعلق بالدين أو فيهما يتعلق بالدنيا ، أما القسم الأول . وهو فيما يتعلق بالدين ، فيجب عليه البحث في أمور ثلاثة : الماضي والحاضر والمستقبل . أما الماضي : فهو أن يرشدهم إلى أن هذا العالم محدث ، وله إله ، كان موجوداً في الأزل ، وسيبقى في الأبد، وأنه منزه عن مماثلة الممكنات [ وأنه موصوف بالصفات المعتبرة في الإلهية والكمال ، وهي القدرة النافذة في جميع الممكنات ] والعلم الساري في جميع المعلومات [ والوحدانية المطلقة بمعنى كونه منزهاً عن الأجزاء والأبعاض ] والفردانية بمعنى كونه منزهاً عن الضد والند ، والصاحبة والولد ، ثم يجب عليه
Know that the craft of prophethood and messengerhood is a call to the creation from being occupied with creation to the service of the Truth, and from turning towards the world to turning towards the Hereafter. This is the primary objective, except that since people are present in the world and in need of its interests, it is necessary that he also engage in this matter to the extent of need. So we say: the engagement of the Messenger – peace be upon him – is either in matters concerning religion or in matters concerning the world. As for the first category, which is concerning religion, he must investigate three matters: the past, the present, and the future. As for the past: it is that he guide them to the fact that this world is originated, and it has a God who existed in pre-eternity and will remain in eternity, and that He is transcendent above resemblance to contingent things, and that He is described with the attributes considered in divinity and perfection, which are the power effective in all contingent things, and knowledge pervading all known things, and absolute oneness in the sense of being transcendent above parts and divisions, and individuality in the sense of being transcendent above opponent and equal, spouse and child. Then he must...
Source pages p. 116

Commentary
This passage opens Chapter Three and defines the craft (حرفة) of prophethood and messengerhood. The author states that the essence of prophethood is calling people away from preoccupation with created things to serving the Truth (حق), and from focusing on this world to focusing on the Hereafter. That is the primary purpose. However, because people live in the world and need worldly affairs, the prophet must also deal with worldly matters to the necessary extent. The author then splits the prophet's engagement into two halves: religious and worldly. Focusing on the religious half, he divides it into three temporal categories: past, present, and future. For the past, the prophet must teach that the world is created (محدث), that it has a God who is eternal, and that God is beyond resemblance to contingent beings. He enumerates the essential attributes: power over all things, knowledge of all things, absolute oneness (transcendence above parts), and individuality (transcendence above opponent, equal, spouse, child). The passage is cut off after 'then he must,' but the structure indicates further elaboration on the present and future.

Key Terms
حرفة النبوة
دعوة
الخلق
الحق
الدنيا
الآخرة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think prophethood is solely about worldly leadership or purely spiritual with no worldly guidance.
Correction: The passage clarifies that the primary call is to the Hereafter, but worldly engagement is necessary to a limited extent.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the primary objective of prophethood?
A) To engage in worldly politics.
B) To call people from creation to the Truth and from the world to the Hereafter.
C) To perform miracles.
D) To establish a just society.

Key Takeaway
Prophethood's primary mission is spiritual redirection, but it also includes necessary worldly guidance.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider your daily preoccupations: are you more occupied with 'creation' (people, possessions, status) or with serving the Truth (Allah)? What concrete change can you make today to shift your focus even slightly?
?
This lesson says prophets only engage in worldly matters 'to the extent of need.' Examine your own engagement with the world—where have you exceeded need and become absorbed? What step can you take to pull back?
?
The essence of prophethood is redirecting from dunya to akhirah. If you were to honestly assess your heart, where is your primary direction: toward this world or the next? What would it take for your akhirah to become your main concern?

Religious teaching is organized around past, present, and future.

The Craft of Prophethood: Calling to the Truth
Defines the craft of prophethood as calling from creation to the Truth.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the primary purpose of prophethood as a spiritual calling and its necessary but limited engagement with worldly matters.

Core Idea
Prophethood redirects hearts from creation to the Creator, while touching the world only as needed.

This lesson dives into the essence of prophethood as defined by the author. You will see that the prophet's core mission is to turn people away from worldly attachments toward devotion to God and the Hereafter. Yet the author immediately acknowledges that believers live in this world, so the prophet must also address worldly needs—but only to the extent required.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic passage slowly. Notice the key phrase 'حرفة النبوة' and how the author defines it in two complementary parts: calling from creation to the Truth and from this world to the Hereafter.
The word 'حرفة' implies a skilled craft or profession.
2
Understand the primary objective
Identify the 'مقصود أصلي' (primary objective). The author states it clearly: to redirect people from creation to the service of the Truth and from this world to the Hereafter. This is the spiritual core of prophethood.
3
Examine the worldly balance
Notice the qualification: 'إلا أن الناس لما كانوا حاضرين في الدنيا... وجب أن يكون له خوض في هذا الباب أيضاً، بقدر الحاجة.' The prophet must engage worldly matters, but only as needed. This balances spiritual mission with practical reality.
The phrase 'بقدر الحاجة' highlights proportionality.
4
Reflect on the misconception
Consider the common misunderstanding that prophets should completely ignore worldly affairs. The passage explicitly guards against this by stating that engagement is necessary, but limited. Write down why this balance is important.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أعلم : أن حرفة النبوة والرسالة عبارة عن دعوة الخلق من الاشتغال بالخلق ، إلى خدمة الحق. ومن الإقبال على الدنيا ، إلى الإقبال على الآخرة . فهذا هو المقصود الأصلي إلا أن الناس لما كانوا حاضرين في الدنيا ومحتاجين إلى مصالحها ، وجب أن يكون له خوض في هذا الباب أيضاً ، بقدر الحاجة
Know that the profession of prophethood and messengership is the call of creation from preoccupation with creation to the service of the Truth, and from turning towards this world to turning towards the Hereafter. This is the primary objective. However, since people are present in this world and in need of its interests, it is necessary for him to engage in this area as well, to the extent of need.
Source pages p. 116

Commentary
This passage defines the essence of prophethood. It explains that the core mission is spiritual: to redirect people from worldly attachments to devotion to God and focus on the afterlife. But it immediately qualifies this by acknowledging that people live in the world and have worldly needs, so the prophet must also address worldly matters, but only as much as necessary. This sets up a balance between spiritual and temporal concerns.

Prophethood
intermediate
6 min
The Criterion for Prophetic Superiority
Defines a messenger and establishes the criterion for prophetic excellence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the criterion for prophetic superiority according to this text: a messenger's greatness is measured by the quantity and completeness of their success in turning human souls to Allah.

Core Idea
A prophet's greatness is measured by how many souls he turns to Allah.

This lesson presents a key principle for evaluating the rank of prophets: the more effectively a messenger fulfills their purpose of redirecting human souls to Allah, the greater their message. The author applies this criterion to Moses and Jesus, arguing that their influence was limited, setting the stage for the superiority of Muhammad.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition of a messenger
First, read the passage carefully to understand how the author defines a messenger's essential function: treating human souls and turning them from other occupations to the worship of Allah.
This definition is the foundation for the criterion that follows.
2
Identify the criterion for superiority
Next, locate the sentence stating that the more benefits (this transformation) emanate from a messenger, the greater and more complete his message is. That is the criterion.
The quantity and completeness of beneficial influence are key.
3
See the application to Moses and Jesus
Finally, examine how the author applies this criterion to Moses (effect limited to Israelites) and Jesus (effect only on very few, and his true call was not trinitarianism). This comparison sets up why Muhammad is superior.
Note that the passage cuts off before completing the argument for Muhammad.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل الرابع في بيان أن محمدا عليه الصلاة والسلام أفضل من جميع الأنبياء والرسل من أعلم (1) أنا بينا : أن الرسول هو الذي يعالج الأرواح البشرية ، وينقلها عن الاشتغال بغير الله تعالى، إلى الاشتغال بعبادة الله تعالى . ولما كان المراد من الرسالة والنبوة : هو هذا المعنى ، فكل من كان صدور هذه الفوائد عنه أكثر وأكمل ، وجب القطع بأن رسالته أعظم وأكمل . وإذا عرفت هذا ، فنقول : إن تأثير دعوة موسى عليه السلام كانت مقصورة على بني إسرائيل فقط وأما دعوة عيسى عليه السلام فكأنه لم يظهر لها تأثير إلا في القليل القليل ، وذلك لأنا نقطع بأنه عليه السلام ما دعا إلى الدين الذي يقول به هؤلاء النصارى ، لأن القول بالأب ، والأبن وروح القدس : قول بالتثليث والتثليث : أقبح الكفر وأفحش أقسام الجهل ، ومثل هذا لا يليق بأجهل الناس ، فضلا
The Fourth Chapter: On explaining that Muhammad, peace and blessings be upon him, is the best of all prophets and messengers. From know (1) that we have explained: that the messenger is the one who treats human souls and transfers them from occupation with other than Allah, the Exalted, to occupation with the worship of Allah, the Exalted. And since the intended meaning of messengership and prophethood is this meaning, everyone from whom these benefits emanate more and more completely, it is necessary to judge that his message is greater and more complete. And if you have understood this, we say: Indeed, the effect of the call of Moses, peace be upon him, was confined to the Children of Israel only. As for the call of Jesus, peace be upon him, it seems that no effect appeared for it except in [the] very few, and that is because we are certain that he, peace be upon him, did not call to the religion that these Christians profess, because the saying of the Father, the Son, and the Holy Spirit is a saying of trinitarianism, and trinitarianism is the ugliest disbelief and the most obscene types of ignorance, and such is not befitting for the most ignorant of people, let alone
Source pages p. 122

Commentary
This passage introduces the fourth chapter on the superiority of Prophet Muhammad over all prophets and messengers. The author first defines the role of a messenger: one who treats human souls and turns them from preoccupation with other than Allah to worship of Allah. The purpose of messengership and prophethood is precisely this transformative effect. The author then establishes a criterion for superiority: the more and the more complete these benefits (i.e., turning people to Allah) emanate from a messenger, the greater and more complete his message is. Applying this criterion, the author compares Moses and Jesus. He asserts that Moses' call was confined to the Children of Israel only. As for Jesus, his call had effect only on a very few people. The author then provides a reason: Jesus did not call to the religion that Christians profess today, because trinitarianism (belief in Father, Son, and Holy Spirit) is the ugliest disbelief and the most obscene ignorance, not befitting even the most ignorant person. The passage cuts off at 'let alone' (فضلا), but the argument is clear: by this criterion, Muhammad's message, being universal and enduring, is superior. The author emphasizes the doctrine of tawhid against trinitarianism.

Key Terms
الرسالة
النبوة
الأرواح البشرية
العبادة
التأثير
موسى
عيسى
التثليث

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The criterion for a prophet's superiority is often misunderstood as miracles or personal status.
Correction: The author emphasizes the quantity and completeness of beneficial influence in turning people to Allah.

Checkpoint
What is the criterion for a messenger's superiority according to this passage?
A) The number of miracles performed
B) The quantity and completeness of benefits emanating from him in turning others to Allah
C) The length of his life
D) The size of his followers during his lifetime

Key Takeaway
The superiority of a prophet is determined by the extent to which they succeed in turning people to Allah.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you measure your own impact by how many souls you turn to Allah, or by worldly metrics like status, wealth, or convenience? What would change if you truly adopted this criterion for your life?
?
The passage says a messenger's greatness is determined by the completeness of turning people to Allah. In your daily interactions, are you actively and completely turning others to Allah, or are you half-hearted? What one habit can you change to be more effective?
?
Prophet Muhammad's superiority is rooted in the universal and lasting effect of his message. If you believe this, how should it shape your sense of responsibility to convey that message to those who have not heard it? Will you take concrete steps this week to share the message of tawhid with at least one person?

Prophethood
intermediate
6 min
The Criterion for Prophetic Superiority
Al-Razi defines the messenger's purpose and the measure of prophetic superiority.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the criterion al-Razi uses to measure prophetic superiority: the effectiveness in treating souls and turning them to worship of Allah alone.

Core Idea
Prophetic greatness is measured by the transformation of souls from falsehood to truth.

Before comparing prophets, al-Razi establishes a foundational principle: the very purpose of prophethood is to heal souls and direct them to Allah. The more a prophet accomplishes this, the greater his prophethood. This lesson unpacks that criterion, which will then be applied to Moses, Jesus, and Muhammad.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition of a messenger
Read the Arabic passage where al-Razi defines the messenger's role. Notice the emphasis on 'treating human souls' and transferring them from preoccupation with other than Allah to worship of Allah alone.
This definition is the foundation of the entire argument.
2
Identify the measure of superiority
After the definition, al-Razi states that the more complete and abundant the emission of these benefits, the greater the messengership. Understand that quantity and quality of spiritual transformation are the metrics.
3
Reflect on the logic
Ask yourself: Why does al-Razi use this criterion? How does it connect to the purpose of prophethood? Consider how this principle would apply in comparing prophets.
This sets up the proof in the next lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل الرابع في بيان أن محمدا عليه الصلاة والسلام أفضل من جميع الأنبياء والرسل من أعلم أنا بينا : أن الرسول هو الذي يعالج الأرواح البشرية ، وينقلها من الاشتغال بغير الله تعالى، إلى الاشتغال بعبادة الله تعالى . ولما كان المراد من الرسالة والنبوة : هو هذا المعنى ، فكل من كان صدور هذه الفوائد عنه أكثر وأكمل ، وجب القطع بأن رسالته أعظم وأكمل . وإذا عرفت هذا ، فنقول : إن تأثير دعوة موسى عليه السلام كانت مقصورة على بني إسرائيل فقط وأما دعوة عيسى عليه السلام فكأنه لم يظهر لها تأثير إلا في القليل القليل ، وذلك لأنا نقطع بأنه عليه السلام ما دعا إلى الدين الذي يقول به هؤلاء النصارى ، لأن القول بالأب ، والابن وروح القدس : قول بالتثليث والتثليث : أقبح الكفر ، وأفحش أقسام الجهل ، ومثل هذا لا يليق بأجهل الناس ، فضلا عن الرسول المعظم . فعلمنا : أنه ما كانت دعوته البتة ، إلى هذا الدين الخبيث ، وإنما كانت دعوته إلى التوحيد والتنزيه . ثم إن تلك الدعوة ما ظهرت البتة ، بل بقيت مطوية غير مروية . فثبت أنه لم يظهر لدعوته إلى الحق أثر البتة وأما دعوة محمد عليه السلام إلى التوحيد والتنزيه ، فقد وصلت إلى أكثر بلاد المعمورة ، والناس قبل مجيئه كانوا على الأديان الباطلة . فعبدة الأصنام كانوا مشتغلين بعبادة الحجر والخشب . واليهود كانوا في دين التشبيه ، وصنعة التزوير ، وترويج الأكاذيب، والمجوس كانوا في عبادة الإلهين ونكاح الأمهات والبنات . والنصارى كانوا في تثليث الآب والابن وروح القدس . والصابئة كانوا في عبادة الكواكب . فكان أهل العالم معرضين عن الدين الحق ، والمذهب الصدق ، فلما أرسله الله تعالى إلى هذا العالم ، بطلت الأديان الباطلة ، وزالت المقالات الفاسدة ، وطلعت شموس التوحيد ، وأقمار التنزيه من قلب كل أحد ، وانتشرت تلك الأنوار في بلاد العالم . فثبت : أن تأثير دعوة محمد عليه السلام في علاج القلوب المريضة والنفوس الظلمانية ، كان أتم وأكمل من تأثير دعوة سائر الأنبياء . فوجب القطع بأنه أفضل من جميع الأنبياء والرسل ، في كل ما يتعلق بالنبوة والرسالة . وهذا برهان ظاهر من باب برهان الله. فإنا بحثنا عن حقيقة النبوة والرسالة . ثم بينا : أن كمال تلك الماهية ، ما حصلت لأحد من الأنبياء ، كما حصل لمحمد عليه الصلاة والسلام .
Chapter Four: On explaining that Muhammad, peace and blessings be upon him, is superior to all the prophets and messengers. Know that we have explained that the messenger is the one who treats human souls and transfers them from occupation with other than God Almighty to occupation with the worship of God Almighty. And since the intended purpose of messengership and prophethood is this meaning, then whoever has the emission of these benefits more and more completely, it is obligatory to decisively conclude that his messengership is greater and more complete. And when you know this, we say: The effect of the call of Moses, peace be upon him, was limited only to the Children of Israel. As for the call of Jesus, peace be upon him, it seems that it had no effect except on very few, because we are certain that he, peace be upon him, did not call to the religion that these Christians profess, because belief in the Father, Son, and Holy Spirit is belief in trinity, and trinity is the ugliest disbelief and the most heinous kind of ignorance, and such a thing is not befitting for the most ignorant of people, let alone the revered messenger. So we know that his call was never to this vile religion, but rather his call was to monotheism and transcendence. Then that call never appeared; rather it remained hidden and untold. So it is established that his call to the truth had no effect at all. As for the call of Muhammad, peace and blessings be upon him, to monotheism and transcendence, it reached most of the inhabited lands. And before his coming, people were upon false religions. The idol worshippers were occupied with worshipping stone and wood. The Jews were in the religion of anthropomorphism, the craft of forgery, and dissemination of lies. The Magians were in the worship of two gods and marrying mothers and daughters. The Christians were in the trinity of Father, Son, and Holy Spirit. The Sabians were in the worship of planets. So the people of the world were turning away from the true religion and the honest doctrine. When God Almighty sent him to this world, the false religions were annulled, the corrupt opinions vanished, the suns of monotheism and moons of transcendence rose from the heart of everyone, and those lights spread in the lands of the world. So it is established that the effect of the call of Muhammad, peace and blessings be upon him, in treating sick hearts and dark souls was more complete and perfect than the effect of the calls of the rest of the prophets. So it is obligatory to decisively conclude that he is superior to all the prophets and messengers, in everything related to prophethood and messengership. This is a clear proof from the category of the proof of God. For we have investigated the reality of prophethood and messengership, then we have shown that the perfection of that essence was not attained by any of the prophets as it was attained by Muhammad, peace and blessings be upon him.
Source pages p. 122

Commentary
This passage is a proof for the superiority of Prophet Muhammad over all prophets and messengers. The author, Fakhr al-Din al-Razi, begins by defining the purpose of prophethood and messengership: the messenger treats human souls and turns them away from preoccupation with other than God to worship of God alone. Since this is the essence, the more a prophet achieves this transformation, the greater his prophethood. He then compares the effects of the calls of three major prophets: Moses, Jesus, and Muhammad. Moses' call was limited to the Children of Israel. Jesus' true call to monotheism never spread because what the Christians follow (Trinity) is not what Jesus taught; his real message remained hidden and had almost no effect. Muhammad's call, in contrast, reached most of the inhabited world, and before him humanity was immersed in idolatry, anthropomorphism, dualism, trinity, and astrology. Upon his mission, these false religions were abolished, and the lights of monotheism spread everywhere. Therefore, his influence on healing sick souls is greater, so his prophethood is superior. The author concludes that this is a clear proof based on investigating the very reality of prophethood and showing that its perfection was realized only in Muhammad.

Key Terms
رسول
دعوة
نبوة
رسالة
أفضلية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the intended purpose of messengership and prophethood?
A) To perform miracles to prove divine power.
B) To treat human souls and turn them to worship of Allah.
C) To establish political authority.
D) To convey laws for worldly life.

Key Takeaway
Prophetic superiority is measured by the degree to which a messenger fulfills his purpose: transforming souls from attachment to other than Allah to exclusive worship of Him.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think about your own life: who has been a 'messenger' to you, treating your soul and turning it from distraction to devotion? How has their impact measured up to al-Razi's criterion of 'more and more complete'? Are you grateful enough for that transformation?
?
If prophetic greatness is based on the emission of spiritual benefits, what does that say about your own influence on others? Are you more concerned with how you appear (status, numbers) or with the actual transformation you cause in souls around you? Be honest: what is one specific change you can make today to increase the 'benefits' you emit?
?
Al-Razi says the purpose of prophethood is this transformation. Is that the purpose of your own faith life? Or have you reduced Islam to rituals and routines without actively transferring your heart from attachment to other than Allah to exclusive worship? What attachment are you still holding onto that you need to be transferred from? Commit to one practical step to let go of it today.

Prophethood
intermediate
10 min
Comparative Proof of Muhammad's Superiority
Al-Razi compares the calls of Moses, Jesus, and Muhammad to prove Muhammad's superiority.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Razi applies the criterion of transformative effectiveness to prove that Muhammad is superior to all prophets by comparing the impacts of Moses, Jesus, and Muhammad.

Core Idea
Muhammad's call transformed the world; others' calls had limited or no lasting effect.

Having established the measure of prophetic greatness, al-Razi now applies it. He examines the historical effects of the calls of Moses, Jesus, and Muhammad. His conclusion: Moses' call was confined to one people; Jesus' true message of monotheism never spread; but Muhammad's call eradicated false religions and established monotheism across the known world. Hence, Muhammad is superior.

Guided Path
1
Analyze the effectiveness of Moses and Jesus
Read al-Razi's assessment of Moses' call (limited to Israelites) and Jesus' call (his true monotheistic message remained hidden; Christianity now follows trinity, not Jesus' actual teaching). Note how each falls short in transforming souls per the criterion.
Al-Razi asserts that Jesus did not teach trinity, so the Christian religion is not his legacy.
2
Contrast with Muhammad's impact
Read the vivid description of the world before Muhammad: idolatry, anthropomorphism, dualism, trinity, astrology. Then how his call abolished these false religions and spread the 'suns of monotheism'. This shows the completeness of transformation.
The passage uses strong imagery to emphasize the universal change.
3
Connect to the criterion
Al-Razi concludes that since Muhammad's call produced more complete spiritual healing, his prophethood is superior. Confirm that this matches the criterion from the previous lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Chapter Four: On explaining that Muhammad, peace and blessings be upon him, is superior to all the prophets and messengers. Know that we have explained that the messenger is the one who treats human souls and transfers them from occupation with other than God Almighty to occupation with the worship of God Almighty. And since the intended purpose of messengership and prophethood is this meaning, then whoever has the emission of these benefits more and more completely, it is obligatory to decisively conclude that his messengership is greater and more complete. So we know that his call was never to this vile religion, but rather his call was to monotheism and transcendence. Then that call never appeared; rather it remained hidden and untold. The idol worshippers were occupied with worshipping stone and wood. The Jews were in the religion of anthropomorphism, the craft of forgery, and dissemination of lies. Commentary
This passage is a proof for the superiority of Prophet Muhammad over all prophets and messengers. The author, Fakhr al-Din al-Razi, begins by defining the purpose of prophethood and messengership: the messenger treats human souls and turns them away from preoccupation with other than God to worship of God alone. Since this is the essence, the more a prophet achieves this transformation, the greater his prophethood. He then compares the effects of the calls of three major prophets: Moses, Jesus, and Muhammad. The author concludes that this is a clear proof based on investigating the very reality of prophethood and showing that its perfection was realized only in Muhammad.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think Jesus' call successfully established Christianity, but al-Razi argues that Jesus never taught trinity; his true message was lost.
Correction: The passage asserts Jesus called to monotheism and transcendence, not trinity; the Christian doctrine is a later corruption.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why did Jesus' true call have no effect?
A) He was rejected by his own people.
B) His message of monotheism never spread; it remained hidden and untold.
C) He only preached to a few disciples.
D) He did not perform enough miracles.

Key Takeaway
Muhammad's prophethood is superior because his call alone effectuated the most complete and widespread transformation of souls from falsehood to pure monotheism.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If Muhammad's call was so powerful that it uprooted every false religion from the hearts of people, why do you still find yourself attached to false beliefs, desires, or habits that contradict tawheed? What specific falsehood in your life has remained untouched by his message, and what will you do today to let it be destroyed?
?
Al-Razi says that Jesus' true message was 'hidden and unconnected' while Muhammad's message spread everywhere. Where in your life have you hidden the truth of Islam—through silence, fear, or compromise? Commit to one place where you will openly proclaim the truth this week, no matter the cost.
?
The passage describes the pre-Islamic world as full of ignorance and corruption. Look at your own heart: are you still living like those before Islam—worshipping your own desires, following man-made philosophies, or associating partners with Allah in your actions? What step will you take today to make the light of tawheed dominate every corner of your soul?

Two Proofs of Prophethood: Miracles vs. Spiritual Healing
Distinguishes two proofs of prophethood and declares the second stronger.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between two proofs of prophethood—miracles as a probabilistic demonstration (burhan al-aan) and spiritual medicine as a knowledge-based demonstration (burhan al-ilm)—and why the latter is considered stronger and more complete.

Core Idea
Prophethood is proven more fully by spiritual healing than by miracles.

In this passage, the author introduces two ways to prove that someone is a true prophet. The first, based on miracles, is common but only gives a general indication. The second, based on the prophet's role as a spiritual physician, is more precise and conclusive. Let's explore why he considers the second method to be the stronger one.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Notice the two methods of proof mentioned.
2
Compare the two demonstrations
Identify the terms 'burhan al-aan' and 'burhan al-ilm'. Understand how the first is probabilistic while the second is certain.
The first infers probability; the second provides knowledge.
3
Understand the spiritual physician analogy
See why spiritual diseases require a physician and how observing the Prophet's effective treatment proves his skill.
The observation is direct and removes doubts.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
Read the final part where the author says this method defeats all doubts against prophethood.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل الخامس في بيان أن إثبات النبوة بهذا الطريق أقوى وأكمل من إثباتها بالمعجزات. اعلم : أن التمسك بطريق المعجزات من باب برهان الآن . وهو الاستدلال بالأثر على المؤثر على سبيل الاحتمال فإنا نعرف بظهور المعجز عليه - عليه السلام - كونه مشرفا عند الله على سبيل الإجمال . من غير أن نعرف كيفية ذلك الشرف . وأما هذا الطريق الثاني فهو من باب برهان العلم . وذلك لأنا بينا : أن الأمراض الروحانية غالبة على أكثر النفوس فلا بد لهم من طبيب ، ونشاهد : أن هذا الرجل معالج ويؤثر علاجه ويفيد الصحة بقدر الإمكان . فهذا يدل على كونه طبيباً حاذقاً في هذا الباب . وحينئذ يظهر : أن هذا الإنسان لا حاجة به في معرفته، إلى أن يكون عالماً بدقائق المنطق والطب والهندسة والحساب . بل كونه عالماً بها. مستغلاً باستنباط دقائقها مما يضره في كونه مستغرقاً في معرفة الله تعالى . وعند هذا تزول جملة الشبهات المذكورة في باب نفي النبوات . فإنه دلت المشاهدة على أن محمدا - عليه السلام كان طبيبا حاذقا في علاج هذه الأمراض كما بيناه ، بل كان روحه قدرت على قلب طبائع أهل الدنيا ، فنقلهم من الباطل إلى الحق ، ومن الكذب إلى الصدق ، ومن الأديان الفاسدة إلى العقائد الصحيحة بقدر الإمكان.
Chapter Five: On explaining that establishing prophethood by this way is stronger and more complete than establishing it by miracles. Know: that clinging to the way of miracles is of the type of demonstration of the present (burhan al-aan). And it is inferring the effect from the cause by way of probability. For we know by the appearance of the miracle upon him - peace be upon him - that he is honored with God in summary fashion, without our knowing the modality of that honor. As for this second way, it is of the type of demonstration of knowledge (burhan al-ilm). And that is because we have explained: that spiritual diseases prevail on most souls, so they must have a physician; and we observe that this man treats and his treatment has effect and produces health to the extent possible. So this indicates that he is a skilled physician in this field. And then it becomes clear: that this man has no need, in his knowledge, to be knowledgeable of the subtleties of logic, medicine, geometry, and arithmetic. Rather, being knowledgeable of them and engaged in extracting their subtleties harms him in being engrossed in knowledge of God Most High. And at this point the totality of the doubts mentioned in the chapter on denying prophethood are removed. For observation has indicated that Muhammad - peace be upon him - was a skilled physician in treating these diseases as we have explained; rather, his spirit was able to overturn the natures of the people of the world, and transfer them from falsehood to truth, from lying to honesty, and from corrupt religions to sound creeds to the extent possible.
Source pages p. 124

Commentary
This passage opens Chapter Five, in which the author declares that the method of proving prophethood based on the analogy of a spiritual physician is stronger and more complete than the method based on miracles. He then explains why. The method of miracles is classified as 'burhan al-aan' (demonstration of the present), which infers the effect from the cause probabilistically. It only tells us that the miracle-worker is honored by God in a general sense, without specifying the nature of that honor. The second method, in contrast, is 'burhan al-ilm' (demonstration of knowledge). The author establishes that spiritual diseases are prevalent in most souls, so they require a physician. Observing that this man (the Prophet) treats and his treatment produces health shows that he is a skilled physician in this field. Moreover, the Prophet does not need to be an expert in logic, medicine, geometry, etc.; in fact, such expertise would distract him from being engrossed in knowledge of God. This approach removes all the doubts raised against prophethood. The observation confirms that Muhammad (saw) was such a physician, transforming people from falsehood to truth, from lies to honesty, and from corrupt religions to sound creeds. Thus, this second proof is more complete.

برهان الآن
برهان العلم
المعجزات
الأمراض الروحانية
طبيب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Miracles are the only valid proof of prophethood.
Correction: The passage shows that while miracles are a proof, a stronger proof is the prophet's role as a spiritual physician, as it is based on knowledge rather than probability.

Checkpoint

B) One is based on demonstration of the present (burhan al-aan), the other on demonstration of knowledge (burhan al-ilm).
C) One is for the elite, the other for the masses.

Key Takeaway
The second proof of prophethood—based on the prophet's effective treatment of spiritual diseases—is stronger than the miracle proof because it provides certain knowledge rather than probabilistic inference.

Defending Harsh Measures: The Prophet's Therapeutic Method
Defends the Prophet's use of plunder and killing as part of his healing method.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author defends the Prophet's use of plunder and killing as part of a consistent therapeutic method that requires established authority.

Core Idea
Even harsh measures serve the greater good when authority is established.

This passage addresses a common objection to prophethood: that the Prophet engaged in plunder and killing. The author responds by framing these actions as part of a unified 'medicine' that requires acceptance of the Prophet's authority. Only then can minor particulars uphold major principles.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation. Notice the objection-and-answer structure.
The passage is a single sentence, but it contains a complete argument.
2
Identify the objection and response
Find the objection: 'He prescribed plunder and killing to establish this objective.' Then find the author's response: 'His medicine... only works if he is accepted.'
3
Understand the key terms
Focus on 'al-usul al-muhimma' (important principles), 'al-juz'iyyat al-saghira' (minor particulars), and 'al-kulliyyat al-qawiyya' (major universals). How do they relate?
These terms form the core of the argument.
4
Reflect on the logic
The author argues that the Prophet's treatment only works if people accept his authority. So he had to apply his method consistently even in small matters. This shows a coherent system, not arbitrary violence.
5
Test your understanding
Take the checkpoint quiz to see if you grasped the main idea.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقوله : « شرع النهب والقتل لتقرير هذا المقصود ، فيقال له : إن طبه وعلاجه في الأصول المهمة : إنما يؤثر فيهم ، لو كان مقبول القول فوجب عليه تقرير هذا الطريق في الجزئيات الصغيرة ، ليبقى النفع في الكليات القوية .

Source pages p. 125

Commentary
This passage answers the objection that the Prophet's method involved plunder and killing. The critic argues that such violence undermines his claim to prophethood. The author replies that the Prophet's medicine and treatment in important principles (al-usul al-muhimma) only have effect if he is accepted as authoritative (maqbul al-qawl). Therefore, it was necessary for him to establish his way even in minor details (al-juz'iyyat al-saghira), so that the benefit would be preserved in major principles (al-kulliyyat al-qawiyya). In other words, the harsh measures were part of a consistent method that ensured the efficacy of the greater objectives.

Refuting the Objection to Abrogation: Two Types of Divine Laws
The author refutes the objection that abrogation is useless by dividing law into rational and positive types.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why abrogation in divine law is beneficial, especially for positive laws, and how it renews sincere worship.

Core Idea
Abrogation breaks religious habits, restoring sincerity over routine.

The objection that abrogation is pointless misses the deeper wisdom behind changing certain divine commands. This passage from al-Matalib al-Aliyah explains that while rational laws are eternal, positive laws are subject to change for a profound spiritual reason: they prevent worship from becoming mere custom. By the end, you'll see how abrogation actually enhances devotion.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and response
First, read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how the author begins with the objection ('Indeed abrogation is speech with no benefit') and then responds with a division of laws.
The objection is a common one; the response is careful and systematic.
2
Distinguish the two types of laws
Identify the two categories: rational laws (ʿaqliyya) which are eternal and cannot be abrogated, and positive laws (waḍʿiyya) which are temporal and can change. The author gives an example of rational law from a hadith and Quran verse.
Rational laws include principles like worshiping God alone and compassion.
3
Understand the benefit of abrogation
Focus on the benefit: abrogation removes the state of habit and familiarity, so that acts are done with sincerity (ikhlāṣ) and worship (ʿibāda) rather than mere routine. This is why changing positive laws is good.
This directly answers the objection about uselessness.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما قولهم : « إن النسخ كلام لا فائدة منه ، فنقول : قد ذكرنا أن الشرائع على قسمين : عقلية لا تقبل النسخ . وحاصلها يرجع إلى ما ذكرناه في قوله عليه السلام التعظيم لأمر الله ، والشفقة على خلق الله ، ولما كان طريان النسخ عليهما محالا . لا جرم قال : يا أهل الكتاب تعالوا إلى كلمة سواء بيننا وبينكم : ألا نعبد إلا الله ، ولا نشرك به شيئا ) (١) . وأما القسم الثاني . وهو الشرائع الوضعية . وهي الأحوال القابلة للنسخ - فالفائدة في النسخ : أن الإنسان إذا واظب على أمر من الأمور مدة مديدة ، صار ذلك كالمألوف المعتاد ، فيأتي بتلك الأعمال للإلف والعادة ، لا للإخلاص والعبادة . فيحسن إبدالها بغيرها ، إزالة لهذه الحالة التي ذكرناها
And as for their saying, 'Indeed abrogation is speech with no benefit,' we say: We have already mentioned that the divine laws are of two types: rational, which do not admit abrogation. Their summary returns to what we mentioned in his statement, peace be upon him: 'Veneration for the command of God, and compassion for the creation of God.' Since the occurrence of abrogation upon them is impossible, inevitably he said: 'O People of the Book, come to a common word between us and you: that we worship none but God, and associate nothing with Him' (1). As for the second type, which are the positive laws, and they are the conditions that admit abrogation – the benefit in abrogation is that when a person persists in a matter for a long period, it becomes like the familiar and habitual; so he performs those deeds out of familiarity and habit, not out of sincerity and worship. So it is good to replace them with others, removing this state that we have mentioned.
Source pages p. 125

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer pair. The objection claims that abrogation (naskh) in divine law is pointless speech. The author responds by dividing divine laws into two categories: rational (ʿaqliyya) and positive (waḍʿiyya). Rational laws, such as the principles of venerating God's command and showing compassion to His creation, cannot be abrogated because they are eternal and unchanging; the author cites the Quranic verse calling to common worship as an example. The second category, positive laws, are temporal rulings that can change over time. The benefit of abrogation in this category is that prolonged adherence to a particular law can lead to mechanical habit rather than sincere devotion; therefore, changing the law renews the worshiper's intention and breaks the cycle of routine. The author ends by affirming that replacing such laws is praiseworthy to eliminate that undesired state.

Key Terms
نسخ (naskh)
شرائع عقلية (rational laws)
شرائع وضعية (positive laws)
إلف (familiarity)
عادة (habit)
إخلاص (sincerity)
عبادة (worship)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think abrogation implies change in God's knowledge or will, or that it is arbitrary and pointless.
Correction: The passage clarifies that abrogation is a practical measure for positive laws only, aimed at renewing human sincerity, not altering divine knowledge.

Checkpoint
According to the response to the objection, what is the benefit of abrogation in positive divine laws?
A) To test people's obedience
B) To replace laws that have become habitual with new ones to renew sincerity
C) To abrogate all previous laws permanently
D) To make worship easier for the weak

Key Takeaway
Abrogation of positive divine laws renews worship from habit to sincere devotion.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Two Human Powers and Two Types of Miracles
Classification of human powers and corresponding miracles.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers' distinction between theoretical and practical powers in humans and how each gives rise to a different type of miracle.

Core Idea
Miracles flow from the soul's two powers: knowledge and action.

The philosophers analyze miracles by first examining human faculties. They identify two fundamental powers—theoretical and practical—and link each to a specific miracle type. This lays the foundation for their explanation of prophetic foreknowledge.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on two powers
Read the Arabic passage once to grasp the distinction between the theoretical and practical powers. Note their definitions and functions.
The theoretical power receives intelligibles from the separate world; the practical power enables action in the physical world.
2
Identify the two miracle types
The passage explicitly connects each power to a miracle: the theoretical power yields miracles of foreknowledge, the practical power yields extraordinary actions. Write down both.
3
Reflect on why this distinction matters
Consider how this classification prepares the ground for the philosophers' specific explanation of how prophets know the unseen. It shows that miracles are not only physical but also cognitive.
This connects to the lesson's objective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل السادس عشر في تغير طريقة الفلاسفة في كيفية ظهور المعجزات على الأنبياء عليهم السلام. اعلم (١): أنا بينا: أن الإنسان له قوتان: قوة نظرية ترتسم فيها صور المعقولات من عالم المفارقات. وقوة عملية، يقدر بها على التصرف في عالم الجسمانيات. فالمعجزة الصادرة عن القوة العاقلة الشاعرة: كونه آتياً بالإخبار عن المغيبات. والمعجزة الصادرة عن القوة العملية: كونه آتياً بالأفعال الغريبة الخارقة للعادة. أما النوع الأول: فطريق الفلاسفة في تقريره: [أن قالوا: قد عرفنا بأن الحس المشترك على وجهين: أحدهما: أن الحواس الظاهرة (٢)] إذا أخذت صور المحسوسات الموجودة في الخارج. وأدتها إلى الحس المشترك، فحينئذ تنطبع تلك الصور في الحس المشترك، وتصير مشاهدة له. والثاني: إن القوة المتخيلة التي من شأنها تركيب الصور بعضها بالبعض. إذا ركبت صورة، فإن تلك الصورة قد تنطبع في الحس المشترك، ومتى حصل الانطباع، وجب أن تصير مشاهدة، وذلك لأن في القسم الأول إنما صارت تلك الصورة مشاهدة، لأجل أن تلك الصورة انطبعت في الحس المشترك لا لأجل أنها وردت عليها من الخارج، وإذا كان كذلك، وجب أيضاً في الصور المنحدرة عليه من جانب المتخيلة أن تصير مشاهدة. ومثال الحس المشترك: المرآة. فإن كل صورة تنطبع فيها من أي جانب كان: صارت مشاهدة، فكذلك الصور المنطبعة في الحس المشترك، إذا انطبعت فيه من أي جانب كان، وجب أن تصير محسوسة. إذا عرفت هذا، فنقول: الصور التي يشاهدها الأبرار، والكهنة، والنائمون، والممرورون، ليست موجودة في الخارج، فإنها لو كانت موجودة في الخارج، لوجب أن يراها كل من كان سليم الحس، بناء على أنه متى كانت الحاسة سليمة وكان الشيء الحاضر بحيث تصح رؤيته، ولم يحصل القرب القريب، والبعد البعيد، واللطافة، والصغر، وحصلت المقابلة. فعند حصول هذه الشرائط يكون الإدراك والإبصار واجباً. إذ لو جاز أن لا يحصل الإدراك عند حصول هذه الشرائط، لجاز أن يصير عندنا جبال عظيمة، وأصوات هائلة، ولا نراها ولا نسمعها. ومعلوم أن تجويزه يوجب الجهالات العظيمة. فثبت بهذا: أن تلك الصور غير موجودة في الخارج، فيجب الجزم بأن ورودها على الحس المشترك، إنما كان من الداخل، وهو أن القوة المتخيلة ركبت تلك الصور، فانحدرت إلى الحس المشترك، فصارت مرئية، وقد كان الواجب أن تحصل هذه الحالة أبداً، إلا أن العائق عنه أمران: الأول: إن الحس المشترك إذا حصلت فيه الصور المأخوذة من الخارج، ثم يتسع للصور التي تركبها المتخيلة، فحينئذ تصير الصور، التي تركبها المتخيلة، بحيث لا يمكن انطباعها في الحس المشترك. والثاني: إن القوة العاقلة تكون مسلطة على القوة المتخيلة، فتمنعها عن تركيب تلك الصور.
The sixteenth chapter on the change of the philosophers' method concerning how miracles appear upon the prophets, peace be upon them. Know (1) that we have explained: that man has two powers: a theoretical power in which images of intelligibles from the world of separate entities are imprinted; and a practical power by which he is able to act in the world of bodies. So the miracle issuing from the rational, conscious power is his coming with reports of unseen things. And the miracle issuing from the practical power is his coming with strange, extraordinary actions. As for the first type: the philosophers' method in establishing it is [that they said: we have known that the common sense is in two aspects: one of them (2): that the external senses when they take the images of sensed objects existing externally and convey them to the common sense, then those images imprint in the common sense and become observable to it. The second: that the imaginative faculty whose function is to combine images with one another, when it composes an image, then that image may imprint in the common sense, and whenever the imprint occurs, it must become observable. This is because in the first division, that image became observable only because that image imprinted in the common sense, not because it came to it from outside. If that is so, then it is also necessary for images descending upon it from the side of the imagination to become observable. The example of the common sense is the mirror: for every image that imprints in it from whatever side becomes observable. So likewise, the images imprinted in the common sense, when they imprint in it from whatever side, must become sensed. If you have understood this, we say: the images that the pious, the soothsayers, the sleepers, and the melancholic see are not existing externally. For if they existed externally, it would be necessary that everyone with sound sense would see them, based on the fact that whenever the sense is sound and the present thing is such that its vision is valid, and there is no extreme proximity, extreme distance, subtlety, smallness, and there is confrontation, then upon the presence of these conditions, perception and sight are necessary. Because if it were possible that perception does not occur when these conditions are present, it would be possible that there exist before us great mountains and immense sounds while we neither see nor hear them. And it is known that allowing this leads to great absurdities. So it is established by this: that those images do not exist externally, so it must be determined that their arrival at the common sense is only from the inside, which is that the imaginative faculty composed those images, then they descended to the common sense and became visible. And it would have been necessary that this state occur always, except that the obstacle to it is two things: the first: that if the common sense has the images taken from the external, then it becomes occupied with the images that the imagination composes, then the images that the imagination composes become such that they cannot imprint in the common sense. The second: that the rational faculty is dominant over the imaginative faculty and prevents it from composing those images.
Source pages p. 128

Commentary
This passage presents the philosophers' method for explaining a specific type of miracle: miracles of foreknowledge (informing of unseen things). The author first distinguishes between two human powers—theoretical and practical—and correlates each with a type of miracle. The theoretical power receives intelligibles from the separate world, and its miracle is prophetic knowledge of the unseen. The practical power enables action in the physical world, and its miracle is extraordinary acts. Then, focusing on the first type (foreknowledge), the passage explains the philosophers' account. They argue that the common sense (al-ḥiss al-mushtarak) can receive images from two sources: (1) external senses bringing images from outside, and (2) the imaginative faculty (al-quwwa al-mutakhayyila) composing images internally. These internal images, when imprinted in the common sense, are perceived just as if they came from outside, analogous to a mirror reflecting any image placed before it. The philosophers then use this to explain visions experienced by the pious, soothsayers, sleepers, and the melancholic: these images are not external but produced by the imagination. They prove this by arguing that if such images were external, they would be visible to everyone with sound senses under normal conditions—but they are not. Therefore, they must originate internally. Finally, they note that normally such images do not constantly appear because of two obstacles: (1) the common sense is occupied with external images, leaving no room for imaginative images; (2) the rational faculty controls the imagination and restrains it from composing images. The passage is a sustained philosophical argument that grounds prophetic foreknowledge in the soul's own imaginative power, not in external perception.

Key Terms
قوة نظرية (quwwa naẓariyya, theoretical power)
قوة عملية (quwwa ʿamaliyya, practical power)
معجزة (muʿjiza, miracle)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that all miracles are purely physical events like levitation or healing.
Correction: This passage shows that philosophers recognize a second type of miracle: miracles of knowledge, where prophets foretell the unseen, stemming from the theoretical power.

Checkpoint
According to the philosophers in this passage, what kind of miracle issues from the theoretical power?
A) Miracles of extraordinary actions
B) Miracles of foreknowledge (informing of unseen things)
C) Miracles of healing
D) Miracles of controlling nature

Key Takeaway
The philosophers divide miracles into two types based on the human power they originate from: cognitive miracles from the theoretical power and physical miracles from the practical power.

?

The Common Sense and Imagination in Prophetic Visions
Explanation of how internal images become perceptible via common sense and imagination.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers' account of how the common sense and imaginative faculty produce internal images that are perceived as real, and how this explains prophetic knowledge of the unseen.

Core Idea
The common sense is like a mirror: images from both outside and inside become visible.

Having distinguished two miracle types, the philosophers now focus on explaining miracles of foreknowledge. They argue that the common sense can receive images from two sources: the external senses and the imaginative faculty. This psychological mechanism accounts for visions seen by prophets, soothsayers, sleepers, and melancholic individuals, without requiring external objects.

Guided Path
1
Understand the two sources of images for common sense
The passage states that common sense receives images either from the external senses (external objects) or from the imaginative faculty (internally composed images). Read carefully to see how each works.
External images come via the five senses; internal images are composed by the imagination.
2
Grasp the mirror analogy
The common sense is compared to a mirror that reflects any image placed before it, regardless of origin. This illustrates why internal images can appear as real as external ones.
3
Examine the proof that these images are internal
The philosophers argue that if such images (seen by pious, soothsayers, etc.) were external, everyone with sound senses would see them under normal conditions. Since they are not universally seen, they must be internal, produced by the imagination.
This proof uses the principle of uniformity of perception.
4
Note the two obstacles to constant vision
The passage concludes with two obstacles that normally prevent such internal images from being constantly perceived: occupation of common sense with external images and control by the rational faculty.
These obstacles explain why such visions are rare or intermittent.

عرفت هذا، فنقول: الصور التي يشاهدها الأبرار، والكهنة، والنائمون، والممرورون، ليست موجودة في الخارج، فإنها لو كانت موجودة في الخارج، لوجب أن يراها كل من كان سليم الحس، بناء على أنه متى كانت الحاسة سليمة وكان الشيء الحاضر بحيث تصح رؤيته، ولم يحصل القرب القريب، والبعد البعيد، واللطافة، والصغر، وحصلت المقابلة. فعند حصول هذه الشرائط يكون الإدراك والإبصار واجباً. إذ لو جاز أن لا يحصل الإدراك عند حصول هذه الشرائط، لجاز أن يصير عندنا جبال عظيمة، وأصوات هائلة، ولا نراها ولا نسمعها. ومعلوم أن تجويزه يوجب الجهالات العظيمة. فثبت بهذا: أن تلك الصور غير موجودة في الخارج، فيجب الجزم بأن ورودها على The sixteenth chapter on the change of the philosophers' method concerning how miracles appear upon the prophets, peace be upon them. Know (1) that we have explained: that man has two powers: a theoretical power in which images of intelligibles from the world of separate entities are imprinted; and a practical power by which he is able to act in the world of bodies. So the miracle issuing from the rational, conscious power is his coming with reports of unseen things. And the miracle issuing from the practical power is his coming with strange, extraordinary actions. As for the first type: the philosophers' method in establishing it is [that they said: we have known that the common sense is in two aspects: one of them (2): that the external senses when they take the images of sensed objects existing externally and convey them to the common sense, then those images imprint in the common sense and become observable to it. The second: that the imaginative faculty whose function is to combine images with one another, when it composes an image, then that image may imprint in the common sense, and whenever the imprint occurs, it must become observable. This is because in the first division, that image became observable only because that image imprinted in the common sense, not because it came to it from outside. If that is so, then it is also necessary for images descending upon it from the side of the imagination to become observable. The example of the common sense is the mirror: for every image that imprints in it from whatever side becomes observable. So likewise, the images imprinted in the common sense, when they imprint in it from whatever side, must become sensed. If you have understood this, we say: the images that the pious, the soothsayers, the sleepers, and the melancholic see are not existing externally. For if they existed externally, it would be necessary that everyone with sound sense would see them, based on the fact that whenever the sense is sound and the present thing is such that its vision is valid, and there is no extreme proximity, extreme distance, subtlety, smallness, and there is confrontation, then upon the presence of these conditions, perception and sight are necessary. Because if it were possible that perception does not occur when these conditions are present, it would be possible that there exist before us great mountains and immense sounds while we neither see nor hear them. And it is known that allowing this leads to great absurdities. So it is established by this: that those images do not exist externally, so it must be determined that their arrival at the common sense is only from the inside, which is that the imaginative faculty composed those images, then they descended to the common sense and became visible. And it would have been necessary that this state occur always, except that the obstacle to it is two things: the first: that if the common sense has the images taken from the external, then it becomes occupied with the images that the imagination composes, then the images that the imagination composes become such that they cannot imprint in the common sense. The second: that the rational faculty is dominant over the imaginative faculty and prevents it from composing those images.

Commentary
This passage presents the philosophers' method for explaining a specific type of miracle: miracles of foreknowledge (informing of unseen things). The author first distinguishes between two human powers—theoretical and practical—and correlates each with a type of miracle. The theoretical power receives intelligibles from the separate world, and its miracle is prophetic knowledge of the unseen. The practical power enables action in the physical world, and its miracle is extraordinary acts. Then, focusing on the first type (foreknowledge), the passage explains the philosophers' account. They argue that the common sense (al-ḥiss al-mushtarak) can receive images from two sources: (1) external senses bringing images from outside, and (2) the imaginative faculty (al-quwwa al-mutakhayyila) composing images internally. These internal images, when imprinted in the common sense, are perceived just as if they came from outside, analogous to a mirror reflecting any image placed before it. The philosophers then use this to explain visions experienced by the pious, soothsayers, sleepers, and the melancholic: these images are not external but produced by the imagination. They prove this by arguing that if such images were external, they would be visible to everyone with sound senses under normal conditions—but they are not. Therefore, they must originate internally. Finally, they note that normally such images do not constantly appear because of two obstacles: (1) the common sense is occupied with external images, leaving no room for imaginative images; (2) the rational faculty controls the imagination and restrains it from composing images. The passage is a sustained philosophical argument that grounds prophetic foreknowledge in the soul's own imaginative power, not in external perception.

The Three Causes of Confused Dreams (Adghath al-Ahlam)
The author enumerates three types of false dreams and their causes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three causes of meaningless false dreams (adghath al-ahlam) and why they should be disregarded.

Core Idea
Not all dreams are meaningful; three types arise from imagination, thought, or temperament.

In the study of dreams, it is crucial to distinguish between true dreams and false ones. The author explains that some dreams are mere 'confused dreams' (adghath al-ahlam) resulting from natural processes during sleep. This passage enumerates three distinct causes for such dreams, emphasizing that they carry no spiritual significance.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Start by reading the Arabic passage silently, then refer to the literal translation. Familiarize yourself with the flow and key terms.
Pay attention to the enumeration markers: 'al-thānī' and 'al-thālith'.
2
Identify the three types
The passage clearly lists three causes. As you read, note each one and its description.
3
Understand each cause
For each type, understand the mechanism: (1) an image from the imagination imprints on common sense, (2) a composed thought transfers to common sense, (3) a change in temperament alters the intellectual faculty's actions.
The examples given for the third type help illustrate how temperament influences dream content.
4
Grasp the conclusion
The author concludes that all three types are completely disregarded (lā ʿibra bihā al-battah). Reflect on why that is important for the study of dreams.
This guards against the misconception that every dream must be interpreted.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
خزانة الخيال ، فعند النوم ، ترتسم تلك الصورة في الحس المشترك ، فتصير مشاهدة محسوسة والثاني : إن القوة الفكرية إذا ألفت صورة ، ارتسمت تلك الصورة في الخيال ، ثم في وقت النوم تنتقل تلك الصورة إلى الحس المشترك ، فتصير محبوسة ، كما أن الإنسان إذا تفكر في الانتقال من بلد إلى بلد ، أو حصل في خاطره رجاء شيء ، أو خوف من شيء ، فإنه يرى تلك الأحوال في النوم. والثالث : إن مزاج الروح الحامل للقوة المفكرة إذا تغير ، فإنه تتغير أفعال القوة المفكرة . ولهذا السبب ، فإن الذي يميل مزاجه إلى الحرارة يرى في النوم : النيران والحريق والدخان ، ومن مال مزاجه إلى الرطوبة يرى الثلوج والأمطار . ومن مال مزاجه إلى اليبوسة يرى التراب والألوان المظلمة. فهذه الأنواع الثلاثة ، لا عبرة بها البتة ، بل هي من قبيل أضغاث الأحلام.
The treasury of imagination: when asleep, that image is imprinted on the common sense (al-ḥiss al-mushtarak), and it becomes an observed sensation. The second: if the intellectual faculty composes an image, that image is imprinted in the imagination, then at the time of sleep that image transfers to the common sense, and it becomes perceived — just as when a person reflects on traveling from one country to another, or the hope of something or fear of something occurs to his mind, he sees those states in sleep. The third: if the temperament of the spirit bearing the intellectual faculty changes, then the actions of the intellectual faculty change. For this reason, one whose temperament inclines to heat sees in sleep fires, conflagration, and smoke; one whose temperament inclines to moisture sees snow and rain; one whose temperament inclines to dryness sees dust and dark colors. These three types are completely disregarded; they are nothing but confused dreams (aḍghāth al-aḥlām).
Source pages p. 131

Commentary
This passage classifies false dreams (aḍghāth al-aḥlām) into three types based on their causes, after which the author asserts that such dreams carry no significance. The explanation proceeds by enumerating each cause in order. First, the author describes a scenario where an image stored in the treasury of the imagination becomes imprinted on the common sense (al-ḥiss al-mushtarak) during sleep, producing an apparent sensory experience. Second, if the intellectual faculty (al-quwwah al-fikriyyah) has composed an image, that image is first stored in imagination and then transfers to the common sense during sleep, resulting in a perception — for example, if one has been thinking about traveling or experiencing hope or fear, those states appear in the dream. Third, a change in the temperament (mizāj) of the spirit that carries the intellectual faculty causes the actions of that faculty to alter; consequently, a person with a hot temperament sees heat-related images like fire and smoke, a person with a moist temperament sees snow and rain, and a person with a dry temperament sees dust and dark colors. After listing all three, the author concludes emphatically: these three types are of no account whatsoever; they belong to the category of confused dreams (aḍghāth al-aḥlām), which are meaningless and should not be interpreted.

الحس المشترك
القوة المفكرة
أضغاث الأحلام

Correction: The author explains that confused dreams arising from physical or psychological causes are meaningless and should be disregarded.

Key Takeaway

The Two Premises for True Dreams
The author presents two premises explaining the cause of true dreams: divine knowledge and the soul's capacity to connect.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two premises that explain the cause of true dreams, and why the soul often fails to achieve universal connection.

Core Idea
True dreams occur when the soul lifts its gaze from the body to the knowledge above.

Mulla Sadrā now turns from the false dream to the true dream. He builds his explanation on two foundational premises: one about the scope of divine knowledge, the other about the soul's capacity for connection. This lesson unpacks those premises and addresses why the connection is not constant.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the English translation. Let the author's argument unfold naturally.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the two premises
Find the two muqaddimatān (premises). Notice how the first premise is about knowledge and the second about the soul's nature.
The author uses a numbered structure in the Arabic.
3
Consider the objection and response
The author preempts a possible objection: why isn't the connection constant? He gives a three-part response: not stinginess, not incapacity, but bodily preoccupation.
4
Reflect on the significance
Think about how this account explains the phenomenon of true dreams and why they are rare. It preserves both the soul's potential and the barrier of worldly engagement.
This connects to broader discussions in prophecy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الرؤيا الصادقة . فالكلام في ذكر سببها ، يتفرع على مقدمتين : إحداهما : إن جميع الأمور الكائنة في هذا العالم الأسفل مما كان ، ومما سيكون ، ومما هو كائن موجود في علم الباري تعالى. وعلم الملائكة العقلية ، والنفوس السماوية والثانية : إن النفس الناطقة من شأنها أن تتصل بتلك المبادىء ، وتنتقش فيها الصور المنتقشة في تلك المبادىء . وعدم حصول هذا المعنى ليس لأجل البخل من تلك المبادىء ، أو لأجل أن النفس الناطقة غير قابلة لتلك الصور ، بل لأجل أن استغراق النفس في تدبير البدن ، صار مانعاً لها من ذلك الاتصال العام .
As for the true dream (al-ruʾyā al-ṣādiqah), the discussion of its cause branches into two premises. The first: all things occurring in this lower world — past, future, and present — exist in the knowledge of God Most High, and in the knowledge of the intellectual angels and the celestial souls. The second: the rational soul (al-nafs al-nāṭiqah) is such that it can connect to those principles and become imprinted with the images that are imprinted in those principles. The failure of this connection is not due to stinginess on the part of those principles, nor because the rational soul is incapable of receiving those images, but rather because the soul’s total absorption in managing the body prevents it from that universal connection.
Source pages p. 131

Commentary
This passage shifts from false dreams to true dreams (al-ruʾyā al-ṣādiqah). The author states that explaining the cause of true dreams depends on two premises (muqaddimatān). He then presents them in order. The first premise is epistemic: all events, past, present, future, and all things that occur in this sublunar world are present in the knowledge of God and also in the knowledge of the intellectual angels and the celestial souls. The second premise concerns the nature of the rational soul (al-nafs al-nāṭiqah): it is capable of connecting to those higher principles and becoming imprinted with the images they contain. The author then addresses a possible objection: why does this connection not happen always? He clarifies that the failure is not due to any deficiency on the part of the higher principles (they are not stingy) nor due to the soul's inability. Rather, the cause is that the soul is so engrossed in governing the body that it becomes preoccupied, and this preoccupation prevents the universal connection (al-ittiṣāl al-ʿāmm).

Key Terms

الاتصال العام
علم الباري

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The soul is inherently incapable of connecting to higher principles, or the higher principles are stingy with knowledge.
Correction: The author explicitly denies both: the soul is capable, and the principles are not stingy. The real barrier is the soul's preoccupation with managing the body.

C) The soul is preoccupied with managing the body

Key Takeaway
True dreams are possible because the soul can connect to the universal knowledge of higher principles, but worldly preoccupation typically prevents this connection.

How the Soul Receives True Dreams
The author details the steps from soul's connection to dream formation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the mechanism by which the soul receives images from higher principles during sleep and how the imaginative faculty transforms them into sensory dream images.

Core Idea
Dreams arise when the freed soul connects to higher principles and the imagination translates those images.

The author now describes the precise process of true dreams. When the soul is momentarily free from managing the body, it naturally connects to higher principles and receives images suited to its own concerns. The imaginative faculty then translates these abstract meanings into sensory images that we experience as dreams.

Guided Path
1
Read the mechanism passage
Read the Arabic passage describing the soul's connection and the role of the imaginative faculty. Notice the sequence from freedom to dream formation.
The passage is dense; focus on the steps.
2
Identify the three faculties
The passage mentions the rational soul, the imaginative faculty (al-mutakhayyila), and the common sense (al-ḥiss al-mushtarak). Understand their roles in dream production.
The imaginative faculty imitates; the common sense makes the image observed.
3
Reflect on personal influence
Note that the content of the dream depends on the person's inclinations—whether toward rational sciences or worldly concerns. This shows the dream is tailored to the dreamer.
