إذا عرفت هذا فنقول : النفس إذا حصل لها أدنى فراغ من تدبير البدن ، اتصلت بطباعها بتلك المبادىء ، فتنطبع فيها بعض تلك الصور الحاضرة عند تلك المبادىء ، وهي الصورة التي هي أليق بتلك النفس . ومعلوم أن أليق الأحوال بها ، ما يتعلق بأحوال ذلك الإنسان وبأصحابه وأهل بلده وإقليمه . وأما إن كان ذلك الإنسان منجذب الهمة إلى تحصيل علوم المعقولات ، لاحت له منها أشياء . ومن كانت همته مصالح الناس رآها ، ثم إذا انطبعت تلك الصور في جوهر النفس الناطقة أخذت المتخيلة التي من طباعها محاكاة الأمور ، في حكاية تلك الصور المنطبعة في النفس ، بصور جزئية تناسبها ، ثم إن تلك الصور تنطبع في الحس المشترك فتصير مشاهدة. فهذا هو سبب الرؤيا في المنام . ثم إن تلك الصور التي ركبتها القوة المتخيلة ، لأجل تلك المعاني قد تكون شديدة المناسبة لتلك المعاني ، فتكون هذه الرؤيا غنية عن التعبير وقد لا تكون كذلك ، إلا أنها أيضاً مناسبة لتلك المعاني من بعض الوجوه ، وههنا تحتاج هذه المنامات إلى التعبير . وفائدة التعبير : التحليل بالعكس ، يعني : أن المعبر عن هذه الصور الحاضرة في الخيال إلى تلك المعاني وأما القسم الثالث أن لا تكون هذه الصور مناسبة لتلك المعاني البتة . وذلك يكون لأحد وجهين : أحدهما : أن يكون حدوث هذا الخيال الغريب ، إنما كان لوجه من الوجوه الثلاثة المذكورة في أسباب أضغاث الأحلام . والثاني : أن يكون ذلك الأجل أن القوة المتخيلة ركبت لأجل ذلك
Once you know this, we say: when the soul obtains even a slight respite from managing the body, it naturally connects to those principles, and some of the images present with them become imprinted in it — specifically the image most suitable for that soul. It is known that the states most suitable for it are those related to the conditions of that person, his companions, his country's people, and his region. If that person is drawn to the pursuit of rational sciences, things from them become apparent to him. One whose concern is the welfare of people sees that. Then, when those images become imprinted in the substance of the rational soul, the imaginative faculty (al-mutakhayyila) — whose nature is to imitate things — takes them and retells those imprinted images in partial images appropriate to them. Then those images become imprinted in the common sense and become observed. This is the cause of the dream in sleep. Then those images composed by the imaginative faculty for the sake of those meanings may be highly appropriate to those meanings, so this dream is free from need for interpretation. Or they may not be so, but they are still appropriate in some respects — here these dreams need interpretation. The benefit of interpretation is analysis in reverse, meaning that the interpreter [translates] these images present in the imagination back to those meanings. As for the third division: these images are not at all appropriate to those meanings. That occurs for one of two reasons. First: the occurrence of this strange imagination is due to one of the three previously mentioned causes of confused dreams. Second: that is because the imaginative faculty composed for that...
Source pages p. 132

Commentary
This passage provides the detailed mechanism of how true dreams occur and then classifies dreams based on the degree of appropriateness between the composed images and the underlying meanings. The author begins by stating that when the soul obtains even a small amount of freedom from managing the body, it naturally connects to the higher principles. During this connection, the most suitable image from the principles imprints itself on the soul. Which images are most suitable? Those related to the person's own circumstances — his state, his companions, his country and region. If the person is inclined towards intellectual sciences, he sees images from that domain; if his concern is public welfare, he sees that. Once these images are imprinted in the rational soul's substance, the imaginative faculty (al-mutakhayyila), whose nature is imitation, takes them and translates them into particular sensory images that are appropriate. These images then imprint on the common sense and become observed — that is the dream. Then the author turns to the relationship between the composed images and the original meanings. The images composed by the imaginative faculty may be highly appropriate, so the dream is clear and needs no interpretation. Alternatively, they may be less appropriate but still related in some way — these dreams require interpretation. The benefit of interpretation is 'analysis in reverse' (al-taḥlīl bil-ʿaks), i.e., the interpreter works back from the sensory images to the original meanings. Finally, the author introduces a third division: images that are not at all appropriate to the meanings. This happens for one of two reasons. The first reason is that the strange image is due to one of the three causes of confused dreams (mentioned in lesson 1). The second reason is that the imaginative faculty composed the images for that purpose (the sentence cuts off). This third division essentially recategorizes such dreams as meaningless.

Key Terms
النفس الناطقة
القوة المتخيلة
الحس المشترك

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think all dreams are purely psychological or random.
Correction: The author shows that true dreams originate from the soul's connection to higher principles, not just the brain.

Checkpoint
What is the role of the imaginative faculty (al-mutakhayyila) in dream formation according to this passage?
A) It directly receives images from higher principles.
B) It translates abstract meanings into partial sensory images.
C) It stores memories for later recall.
D) It controls the body during sleep.

Key Takeaway
True dreams originate from the soul's connection to higher principles, with the imaginative faculty forming sensory images.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
At night, when your soul is freed from managing the body, what kind of images does it carry to you? Do your dreams reflect the pursuits of this world or a genuine longing for higher principles—and what does that say about where your heart truly resides?
?
The imaginative faculty translates abstract meanings into sensory images based on your waking concerns. If you could see the ‘replay’ of your last few dreams, would they show a mind preoccupied with eternal truths or with fleeting distractions—and are you ready to let that judgment call you to change?
?
You claim to seek knowledge of the divine, but the passage says the soul receives images that are 'most suitable' to it. What if your dreams are a mirror of your actual spiritual state—would you be willing to examine them honestly and adjust your life's focus accordingly?

Classifying Dreams: Clear, Interpretable, and Meaningless
The author categorizes dreams by degree of appropriateness and defines interpretation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three categories of dreams based on the appropriateness of their images to their meanings, and the purpose of dream interpretation.

Core Idea
Dreams vary from self-explanatory to requiring interpretation to being meaningless.

Having explained the origin of true dreams, the author now classifies dreams according to how closely the dream images match the underlying meanings. Some dreams are so clear they need no interpretation; others require analysis in reverse; and a third category has no real meaning at all.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three divisions
The passage presents three types: (1) images highly appropriate, (2) images somewhat appropriate, (3) images not at all appropriate. Note the different outcomes for each.
The third division is cut off but gives two causes.
2
Understand interpretation as reverse analysis
The author defines the benefit of interpretation as 'analysis in reverse' (al-taḥlīl bil-ʿaks). The interpreter works back from the sensory images to the original meanings.
This is a key concept for understanding dream interpretation.
3
Note the causes of meaningless dreams
The third division (no appropriateness) occurs due to either one of the three causes of confused dreams or because the imaginative faculty composed for that purpose (incomplete).

How a Perfect Soul Receives Inspiration While Awake
Razi explains the mechanism of inspiration from the unseen.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the process Razi describes for how a perfect rational soul can receive revelation while awake, including the roles of the rational soul, imaginative faculty, and common sense.

Core Idea
Revelation is a natural process of a perfected soul connecting to the unseen.

Razi provides a detailed psychological mechanism for how revelation occurs to prophets while awake. He builds on the idea that the rational soul can detach from the body and connect to separate principles, similar to dreams but during wakefulness. This passage explains the steps from imprinting forms to sensible images, and distinguishes between clear revelation and revelation needing interpretation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and translation. Notice the conditional structure and the detailed psychological description.
The passage is long, take your time.
2
Identify the conditions for inspiration
List the prerequisites for the rational soul to receive inspiration while awake: perfection of power, unimpeded bodily management, and strong intellectual power to detach common sense.
3
Trace the process from imprint to sensible image
Follow how forms from separate principles become imprinted in the rational soul, then the imaginative faculty constructs a sensible image, which descends to the common sense and becomes observable.
The person hears speech or sees a majestic scene.
4
Understand the distinction between clear and interpreted revelation
Compare the two outcomes: if the sensible image matches the rational meaning perfectly, it is clear revelation (wahy sareeh); if it differs in some respects, it needs interpretation (wahy muhtaj ila ta'wil).
The preventive factors that ensure clarity are only partially given here.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفرع الثاني : في كيفية الإضمار عن الغيب . اعلم أن النفس الناطقة إذا كانت كاملة القوة ، وافية بالوصول إلى الجوانب العالية والسافلة ، وتكون في القوة بحيث لا يصير اشتغالها بتدبير البدن عائقاً لها عن الاتصال بالمباديء المفارقة ، ثم اتفق أيضاً أن كانت قوتها الفكرية قوية الفكر قادرة على انتزاع لوح الحس المشترك عن الحواس الظاهرة، فحينئذ لا يبعد أن يقع لهذه النفس في حال اليقظة مثل ما يقع للنائمين من الاتصال بالمبادىء المفارقة فحينئذ ترتسم من بعض تلك المفارقات صور تدل على وقائع هذا العالم في جوهر النفس الناطقة . ثم إن القوة المتخيلة لأجل قوتها تركب صورة مناسبة لها ، ثم تنحدر تلك الصورة إلى لوح الحس المشترك فتصير مشاهدة ، وعند هذه الحالة يسمع ذلك الإنسان كلاماً منظوماً من هاتف ، وقد يشاهد منظراً في أكمل أبهة وأجل صورة وتخاطبه تلك الصورة بما يهمه من أحواله ، وأحوال من يتصل به ، ثم إن كانت هذه الصورة المحسوسة منطبقة على تلك المعاني التي أدركتها النفس الناطقة ، كان ذلك وحياً صريحاً ، وإن كانت الصورة الخيالية مخالفة لذلك المعنى العقلي من بعض الوجوه ، كان ذلك وحياً محتاجاً إلى التأويل . والصارف للقوة المتخيلة عن هذا التغيير والتبديل أمران : الأول : إن الصورة المنطبعة في النفس الناطقة الفائضة من جانب المبادىء العالية ، إذا فاضت على نعت الجلاء والوضوح صارت تلك القوة مانعة
The second branch: On the manner of inspiration from the unseen. Know that if the rational soul is perfect in power, sufficient in reaching the high and low aspects, and is such that its occupation with managing the body does not prevent it from connecting to the separate principles, and moreover it also happens that its intellectual power is strong in thought, able to withdraw the tablet of the common sense from the external senses, then it is not unlikely that this soul in the waking state experiences what occurs to sleepers in terms of connection to the separate principles. Then from some of those separate entities, forms indicating events of this world become imprinted in the substance of the rational soul. Then the imaginative faculty, due to its strength, constructs a form appropriate to it, then that form descends to the tablet of the common sense and becomes observable. At this state, that person hears organized speech from a caller, and may witness a scene in the most complete splendor and most majestic form, and that form addresses him concerning his important affairs and those of his connections. Then if this sensible form corresponds to those meanings that the rational soul apprehended, it is clear revelation; if the imaginative form differs from that rational meaning in some respects, it is revelation needing interpretation. That which prevents the imaginative faculty from this alteration and change are two things: The first: that the form imprinted in the rational soul flowing from the side of the high principles, when it flows in the manner of clarity and brightness, that power becomes a preventer
Source pages p. 133

Commentary
This passage describes the second branch on how inspiration occurs from the unseen. It explains that if the rational soul (an-nafs an-natiqa) is perfect and not hindered by bodily management, and its intellectual power is strong enough to detach the common sense from external senses, then it can connect to separate principles even while awake, similar to what happens during sleep. From these principles, forms indicating worldly events are imprinted in the soul. The imaginative faculty then constructs an appropriate sensible image, which appears in the common sense, making it visible and audible. The person hears speech or sees a majestic form. If the sensible image matches the original meanings, it is clear revelation (wahy sareeh); if it differs in some respects, it is revelation needing interpretation (wahy muhtaj ila ta'wil). The passage then begins listing two factors that prevent the imaginative power from altering the image: the first is when the form from the high principles flows with clarity and brightness, making the imaginative power a preventer (the sentence ends here, likely continued on the next page).

القوة المتخيلة
الحس المشترك
الوحي
التأويل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think revelation only comes through an angel in a fixed form without any cognitive process.
Correction: Razi explains that revelation can involve a psychological process where the soul receives forms from higher principles and the imaginative faculty constructs sensible images that may need interpretation.

Checkpoint
According to Razi, what determines whether the inspiration is clear revelation (wahy sareeh) or revelation needing interpretation?
A) The strength of the soul's power
B) Whether the sensible image perfectly matches the rational meanings or differs in some respects
C) The presence of a caller
D) The time of day

Key Takeaway
Razi describes inspiration as a natural cognitive process of the perfected soul, where forms from the unseen become sensible images that either match exactly (clear revelation) or require interpretation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Razi says the soul must be perfect and not hindered by managing the body to receive inspiration. What in your daily life prevents your soul from connecting to higher realities? Are you willing to detach even temporarily from worldly distractions to seek that connection?
?
The imaginative faculty constructs a form appropriate to the meaning from the unseen. When you read the Qur'an, do you allow the meanings to shape your imagination, or do you impose your own pre-existing images? How can you purify your imagination to be a faithful vessel for divine truth?
?
Revelation may need interpretation if the image does not match the meaning. In your own life, when you sense a prompting or a sign from God, do you rush to interpret it literally, or do you humbly seek the deeper meaning? What would it require for you to be patient and consult the scholars?

How the Rational Soul Receives Revelation in Wakefulness
Explains how the perfect rational soul receives images from higher principles in wakefulness, distinguishing explicit and interpretable revelation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the process by which the perfect rational soul receives revelation in wakefulness, and the distinction between explicit revelation and revelation that requires interpretation.

Core Idea
Revelation in wakefulness mirrors the prophetic dream, but with conscious awareness.

This passage from al-Matalib al-Aliyah explores how the rational soul, when perfected, can receive direct knowledge from the higher principles even while awake. It describes a process similar to dreaming but occurring in a state of full consciousness. The passage also clarifies that not all revelation is literal; some comes in symbolic forms that require interpretation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the prerequisites
Read the passage and note the conditions required for the rational soul to receive revelation in wakefulness: perfection in power, ability to reach high and low aspects, and strong intellectual power that can disengage the common sense.
The soul must be so powerful that bodily management does not hinder spiritual connection.
2
Follow the descent of images
Trace how images from the separate principles imprint in the rational soul, then the imaginative faculty composes an appropriate image, which then descends to the common sense and becomes visible.
Notice that the process involves both intellectual and imaginative components.
3
Compare explicit and interpretable revelation
Focus on the final distinction: if the sensory image matches the intellectual meaning exactly, it is explicit revelation; if there is a discrepancy, it requires interpretation.
This distinction is crucial for understanding how prophetic revelation can be both literal and symbolic.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفرع الثاني: في كيفية الإضمار عن الغيب. اعلم أن النفس الناطقة إذا كانت كاملة القوة، وافية بالوصول إلى الجوانب العالية والسافلة، وتكون في القوة بحيث لا يصير اشتغالها بتدبير البدن عائقاً لها عن الاتصال بالمباديء المفارقة، ثم اتفق أيضاً أن كانت [قوتها الفكرية (۳)] قوية الفكر قادرة على انتزاع لوح الحس المشترك عن الحواس الظاهرة، فحينئذ لا يبعد أن يقع هذه النفس في حال اليقظة مثل ما يقع للنائمين من الاتصال بالمبادىء المفارقة، فحينئذ ترتسم من بعض تلك المفارقات صور تدل على وقائع هذا العالم في جوهر النفس الناطقة. ثم إن القوة المتخيلة لأجل قوتها تركب صورة مناسبة لها، ثم تنحدر تلك الصورة إلى لوح الحس المشترك فتصير مشاهدة، وعند هذه الحالة يسمع ذلك الإنسان كلاماً منظوماً من هاتف، وقد يشاهد منظراً في أكمل أبهة وأجل صورة وتخاطبه تلك الصورة بما يهمه من أحواله، وأحوال من يتصل به، ثم إن كانت هذه الصورة المحسوسة منطبقة على تلك المعاني التي أدركتها النفس الناطقة، كان ذلك وحياً صريحاً، وإن كانت الصورة الخيالية مخالفة لذلك المعنى العقلي من بعض الوجوه، كان ذلك وحياً محتاجاً إلى التأويل. فقد
Second branch: On the manner of informing about the unseen. Know that the rational soul, when it is perfect in power, reaching the high and low aspects, and is so powerful that its engagement in managing the body does not prevent it from connection with the separate principles, and it also happens that its [intellectual power] is strong in thought, able to withdraw the tablet of common sense from the external senses, then it is not unlikely that this soul experiences in wakefulness similar to what happens to sleepers in terms of connection with the separate principles. Then some of those separate [principles] imprint images indicating the events of this world in the substance of the rational soul. Then the imaginative faculty, due to its strength, composes an appropriate image, then that image descends to the tablet of common sense and becomes visible. At that state, the person hears organized speech from a caller, or sees a scene in the most complete splendor and most sublime form, and that image addresses him about his concerns and the concerns of those connected to him. Then if this sensory image corresponds to those meanings that the rational soul perceived, it is explicit revelation; but if the imaginal image contradicts that intellectual meaning in some respects, it is revelation that requires interpretation.
Source pages p. 133

Commentary
This passage explains the process of receiving revelation in wakefulness, specifically how the rational soul attains knowledge of hidden events. It begins by describing the conditions for such reception: the rational soul must be perfect in power, able to reach both higher and lower realms, and its involvement with the body must not hinder its connection to the separate principles (the active intellect or angels). Additionally, the intellectual power must be strong enough to disengage the common sense from external senses. When these conditions are met, the soul can connect to the separate principles in wakefulness, similar to how sleepers connect in dreams. From these principles, images representing worldly events are imprinted in the soul. Then the imaginative faculty, due to its strength, composes a suitable image, which then descends to the common sense and becomes visible. The person hears speech or sees a scene that communicates relevant matters. The distinction is then made: if the sensory image exactly matches the meanings perceived by the rational soul, it is explicit revelation (waḥy ṣarīḥ); if the imaginal image differs in some aspects from the intellectual meaning, it is revelation that requires interpretation (waḥy muḥtāj ilā al-taʾwīl). This sets the stage for understanding how prophetic revelation is preserved from error while allowing for symbolic expressions.

Key Terms
النفس الناطقة
المبادىء المفارقة
القوة المتخيلة
لوح الحس المشترك
وحي صريح
وحي محتاج إلى التأويل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All revelation is literal and does not require interpretation.
Correction: The passage shows that when the sensory image does not exactly match the intellectual meaning, the revelation needs interpretation, indicating that not all revelation is literal.

Checkpoint
What distinguishes explicit revelation (وحي صريح) from revelation requiring interpretation (وحي محتاج إلى التأويل)?
A) The strength of the imaginative faculty.
B) Whether the sensory image corresponds exactly to the meanings perceived by the rational soul.
C) Whether the revelation occurs in wakefulness or sleep.
D) The presence of a visible scene or speech.

Key Takeaway
Wakeful revelation occurs when the perfect soul connects to higher principles, and its truth may come in explicit or symbolic forms.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read a verse of the Qur'an that seems symbolic or difficult, do you immediately assume it must be taken literally, or do you pause to consider that it might be revelation requiring interpretation? How does this lesson challenge your default approach to understanding revelation?
?
The lesson says the rational soul must be so perfect that managing the body does not hinder connection to higher principles. In your own life, what bodily or worldly concerns most frequently block your receptivity to divine guidance? What specific steps can you take this week to detach from those hindrances?
?
Imagine you experience a strong spiritual insight or dream. How would you test whether it corresponds to an explicit truth or needs interpretation? Are you willing to subject your experiences to the standard of correspondence with intellectual meaning, even if it means admitting your understanding might need adjustment? What might you be afraid to discover?

Two Factors Preserving Revealed Images
Lists two safeguards for revealed images.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two factors that safeguard the revealed image from alteration by the imaginative faculty.

Core Idea
Revelation is preserved by the clarity of the image and the strength of the soul.

When a prophet receives revelation, an image from the higher principles is imprinted in the rational soul. But what prevents the imagination from distorting it? Sanusi identifies two factors: one relating to the image itself, and one relating to the soul. This passage explains how these work together to ensure the integrity of revelation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once and then its translation. Let the author's structure come through before you analyze.
Notice the two 'أمران' (two things) that structure the entire argument.
2
Identify the two factors
The passage lists two preventers. Find each one and note how Sanusi explains it. The first relates to the image's clarity, the second to the soul's strength.
The author uses 'الأول' and 'الثاني' to mark them.
3
Understand the analogy for the first factor
Sanusi compares the clear revealed image to extremely clear sensory images. Why does extreme clarity prevent the imagination from altering them? Reflect on this.
This shows the principle applies to both internal and external images.
4
Examine the second factor
The second factor is the strength of the rational soul. How does a powerful soul prevent alteration? Consider the active role of the soul in preserving the image.
This highlights the soul's strength as a guardian of revelation.
5
Reflect on the combined lesson
Think about how both factors together ensure the revealed image remains unchanged. This is important for understanding how revelation is preserved without error.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والصارف للقوة المتخيلة عن هذا التغيير والتبديل أمران: الأول: إن الصورة المنطبعة في النفس الناطقة الفائضة من جانب المبادىء العالية، إذا فاضت على نعت الجلاء والوضوح صارت تلك القوة مانعة للخيال عن التصرف فيها، كما أن الصور المحسوسة المأخوفة من الخارج إذا كانت في غاية الجلاء والظهور فحينئذ تعجز القوة المتخيلة عن التصرف فيها. والثاني: إن جوهر النفس الناطقة، إذا كان في غاية القوة، فحينئذ يقوى على منع القوة المتخيلة من التصرف في تلك الصور بالتغيير والتبديل.
The preventer of the imaginative faculty from this alteration and change are two things: First: When the image imprinted in the rational soul, flowing from the side of the high principles, flows in the state of clarity and obviousness, that power becomes a preventer for the imagination from acting upon it, just as sensory images taken from outside, when they are in extreme clarity and appearance, then the imaginative faculty is unable to act upon them. Second: When the substance of the rational soul is in extreme strength, then it is able to prevent the imaginative faculty from acting upon those images with alteration and change.
Source pages p. 133

Commentary
This passage enumerates two safeguards that ensure the revealed image remains unchanged. The first factor is intrinsic to the image itself: when the image from the higher principles is extremely clear and distinct, the imaginative faculty is prevented from altering it. The author draws an analogy with external sensory images: if an external image is extremely clear, the imagination cannot modify it. The second factor is the strength of the rational soul itself: if the soul is extremely powerful, it can actively prevent the imagination from distorting the images. Together, these two factors guarantee the integrity of revelation, whether it is explicit or requires interpretation.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think revelation is only preserved by divine intervention, with no role for the nature of the image or the soul.
Correction: This passage shows that the clarity of the image and the strength of the rational soul are also active factors in preserving revelation from alteration.

Checkpoint

A) The clarity of the image and the strength of the rational soul.
B) The strength of the imaginative faculty and the weakness of the soul.
C) The presence of a caller and the visibility of the scene.
D) The connection to the separate principles and the withdrawal of common sense.

Key Takeaway
Revelation is preserved from alteration by both the clarity of the image and the strength of the receiving soul.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?
Think of a time when a divine truth felt extremely clear to you—like an undeniable sign. Did you let your imagination 'add' or 'subtract' from it to make it more convenient? How can you ensure you hold it with both clarity and inner strength?

Weak Souls and Sensory Aids to Unseen Knowledge
Describes how weak souls use sensory distraction to receive hidden knowledge, often leading to illusions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how weak souls attempt to connect to the unseen through sensory distraction and why this is distinct from genuine prophetic revelation.

Core Idea
Weak souls seek artificial shortcuts to the unseen, but true revelation requires spiritual strength.

This passage from al-Matalib al-Aliyah explores an alternative path to unseen knowledge used by those lacking strong spiritual faculties. The author explains how sensory distraction can create a temporary disconnect from the body, allowing a glimpse into the unseen, but he warns that this is only accessible to the weak-minded and prone to illusion. Let's examine the details.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice how the author describes the method and conditions.
The passage is a continuous explanation by Ibn Sina.
2
Identify the methods used
Look for the two techniques mentioned: intense running and staring at transparent or shiny objects. Understand how they daze the senses.
These are artificial aids, not natural spiritual gifts.
3
Note the target audience
The passage specifies that only weak-minded individuals—like children, women, and the stupid—are prone to this phenomenon. Consider why this is significant.
This contrasts with the strong souls of prophets and saints.
4
Understand the outcome
During the swoon, the person may hear speech or see images they attribute to jinn. Others may use these utterances for guidance. Recognize the unreliability of such experiences.
5
Reflect on the distinction
Compare this with genuine revelation. Why does the author emphasize the weakness of intellect here?
This ties back to the learning objective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفرع الثالث: إن النفوس التي ليس لها من القوة ما تقوى على الاتصال بعالم الغيب في حال اليقظة فربما استعانت في حال اليقظة بما يدهش الحس، ويحير الخيال، كما يستعين بعضهم بشد حثيث، وبعضهم بتأمل شيء شفاف، أو براق لامع، يورث البصر ارتعاشاً. فإن كل ذلك مما يدهش الخيال، فتبتعد النفس بسبب حيرتها وانقطاعها في تلك اللحظة عن تدبير البدن، لانتهاز فرصة إدراك الغيب. والشرط في هذا: أن يكون الإنسان ضعيف العقل، مصدقاً لكل ما يحكى له عن مسيس الجن. مثل الصبيان والنسوان والبله. فهؤلاء إذا ضعفت حواسهم، وكانت أوهامهم شديدة الانجذاب إلى مطلوب معين، فحينئذ يقع لنفوسهم التفات في تلك اللحظة إلى عالم الغيب، وتتلقى ذلك المطلوب. فتارة يسمع خطاباً، ويظن أنه من جني، وتارة يترائى له صورة مشـاهدة، فيظن أنها من أعوان الجن، فيلقى إليه من الغيب ما ينطق به في أثناء ذلك الغشى، فيأخذه السامعون ويبنون عليه تدابيرهم في مهماتهم. هذا منتهى ما قرره الشيخ الرئيس في هذا الباب.
Third branch: Souls that do not have the strength to connect to the world of the unseen in wakefulness perhaps seek aid in wakefulness with what dazes the sense and confuses the imagination, as some use intense running, and some use contemplation of something transparent or shiny and lustrous that causes trembling of the sight. For all of that dazes the imagination, so the soul, due to its confusion and its detachment at that moment from managing the body, removes itself to seize an opportunity to perceive the unseen. The condition for this is that the person be weak-minded, believing everything told to him about the touch of jinn, such as children, women, and the stupid. These, when their senses are weak and their fancies are strongly attracted to a specific sought thing, then there occurs to their souls a turning at that moment to the world of the unseen, and they receive that sought thing. Sometimes he hears a speech and thinks it is from a jinn, and sometimes a visible image appears to him, and he thinks it is from the helpers of the jinn, and there is cast to him from the unseen what he utters during that swoon, and the listeners take it and build their plans upon it in their important matters. This is the end of what the Chief Master (al-Shaykh al-Ra'īs) established in this chapter.
Source pages p. 134

Commentary
This passage discusses a different category of souls: those lacking sufficient power to connect to the unseen in wakefulness naturally. Instead, they may use artificial means to induce a state of sensory distraction or confusion, such as intense running or staring at a transparent or shiny object. These techniques daze the imagination and cause the soul to momentarily disengage from the body, allowing a brief connection to the unseen. However, the passage emphasizes that this is only effective for people who are weak-minded, credulous, and prone to believe stories about jinn—specifically mentioning children, women, and the stupid. When their senses are weak and their imaginations are strongly focused on a desired object, they may experience a turning of the soul toward the unseen. During such episodes, they may hear speech or see images that they attribute to jinn, and they utter words that listeners use to guide their actions. The passage notes that this is the end of what Ibn Sīnā (al-Shaykh al-Ra'īs) established on this topic. The tone suggests that these experiences are not reliable and are associated with weakness of intellect, contrasting with the authentic revelation received by prophets and saints.

Correction: This passage distinguishes genuine revelation by strong souls from the unreliable experiences of weak souls who use artificial aids and are credulous.

Key Takeaway
Weak souls may attempt to access the unseen through sensory distraction, but such experiences are unreliable and only occur in those with weak intellects, unlike the authentic revelation received by strong souls.

The Third Branch: Distraction and Connection to the Unseen
Ibn Sina explains how weak souls use distraction to access the unseen.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ibn Sina explains that weak souls can perceive the unseen through sensory distraction and the specific conditions required for such experiences.

Core Idea
Weak souls connect to the unseen by confusing the senses and imagination.

Ibn Sina presents a fascinating case where individuals unable to perceive the unseen while awake may still access it through techniques that overwhelm their senses and imagination. This lesson explores the third branch of his account, focusing on the role of distraction, the condition of weak intellect, and the resulting phenomena. Read the passage carefully to see how he distinguishes this path from the stronger souls' direct connection.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation once to grasp the overall narrative of the third branch.
Notice the distinction between strong souls (earlier branches) and the weak souls described here.
2
Identify the method
Look for the specific techniques mentioned: tight binding, contemplating transparent or shiny objects. Understand how these 'astound the sense and confuse the imagination.'
3
Understand the condition
Focus on the condition Ibn Sina sets: the person must be weak in intellect and believe in jinn contact. Note the examples given: children, women, the mentally deficient.
Consider the social context of the time.
4
Explore the outcome
Trace what happens: the soul withdraws from the body, becomes attentive to the unseen, and receives utterances or images. The listeners then act on these.
This is not prophecy but a lower form of connection.
5
Reflect on the significance
Ask yourself why Ibn Sina includes this branch. It shows he acknowledges non-prophetic access to the unseen under specific psychological conditions.
This balances his overall theory of prophethood.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفرع الثالث : إن النفوس التي ليس لها من القوة ما تقوى على الاتصال بعالم الغيب في حال اليقظة [ فربما استعانت في حال اليقظة ] بما يدهش الحس ، ويحير الخيال ، كما يستعين بعضهم بشد حثيث ، وبعضهم بتأمل شيء شفاف ، أو براق لامع ، يورث البصر ارتعاشاً . فإن كل ذلك مما يدهش الخيال ، فتبتعد النفس بسبب حيرتها وانقطاعها في تلك اللحظة عن تدبير البدن ، لانتهاز فرصة إدراك الغيب. والشرط في هذا : أن يكون الإنسان ضعيف العقل، مصدقاً لكل ما يحكى له عن مسيس الجن . مثل الصبيان والنسوان والبله . فهؤلاء إذا ضعفت حواسهم ، وكانت أوهامهم شديدة الانجذاب إلى مطلوب معين ، فحينئذ يقع لنفوسهم التفات في تلك اللحظة إلى عالم الغيب ، وتتلقى ذلك المطلوب . فتارة يسمع خطاباً ، ويظن أنه من جني وتارة يترائي له صورة مشاهدة ، فيظن أنها من أعوان الجن ، فيلقى إليه من الغيب ما ينطق به في أثناء ذلك الغشى ، فيأخذه السامعون ويبنون عليه تدابيرهم في مهماتهم هذا منتهى ما قرره الشيخ الرئيس في هذا الباب
The third branch: Verily, souls that do not have sufficient power to connect with the unseen world while awake [perhaps they seek help while awake] by what astounds the sense and confuses the imagination, just as some of them use a tight binding, and some by contemplating a transparent thing, or a bright shiny thing that causes the vision to tremble. For all of that astounds the imagination, so the soul, due to its bewilderment and disconnection at that moment, withdraws from managing the body, to seize an opportunity to perceive the unseen. The condition in this is that the person be weak in intellect, believing everything told to him about the touch of the jinn, like children, women, and the mentally deficient. These, when their senses weaken and their fancies are strongly drawn to a particular sought thing, then their souls become attentive at that moment to the unseen world and receive that sought thing. Sometimes he hears a speech and thinks it is from a jinn, and sometimes a visible image appears to him, so he thinks it is from the helpers of the jinn, and information from the unseen is cast to him that he utters during that swoon, and the listeners take it and build their plans upon it in their important matters. This is the end of what the Chief Master (al-Shaykh al-Ra'is) established in this chapter.
Source pages p. 134

Commentary
This passage presents the third branch of Ibn Sina's (the Chief Master) account of how souls can perceive the unseen. It describes a specific case: souls that are weak in power and cannot connect to the unseen while awake. To compensate, they seek help by using means that astound the senses and confuse the imagination, such as tight binding or focusing on a transparent or shiny object that makes the eyes tremble. This astonishment causes the imagination to be overwhelmed, and the soul withdraws from managing the body, seizing the opportunity to perceive the unseen. The condition for this is that the person be weak-minded, believing everything told about contact with jinn, like children, women, and the mentally deficient. When their senses weaken and their fancies are strongly focused on a specific object, their souls become attentive to the unseen and receive that object. Sometimes they hear a speech and think it is from a jinn; sometimes they see an image and think it is from the jinn's helpers. They then utter something from the unseen during the swoon, and listeners take it as guidance. This ends Ibn Sina's discussion.

Key Terms
النفس الناطقة
القوة المتخيلة
عالم الغيب
الخيال
الإدهاش

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that anyone can perceive the unseen through distraction without any special condition.
Correction: Ibn Sina restricts this to individuals with weak intellect and a predisposition to believe in jinn; it is not a universal method.

Second Objection: Conditions for Perceiving the Unseen
Objection that perception is conditioned by sleep, weak mind, or perfect soul; answer begins.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that perception of unseen images is conditioned by specific states (sleep, weak intellect, or perfect soul) and the beginning of the reply that questions this path.

Core Idea
Perception of the unseen is conditional, but conditions themselves can be questioned.

The objector attempts to prove that we can be certain of external existence when no unusual conditions are present. This lesson presents those conditions and the initial response that the objector's own path undermines their claim. The reply, though incomplete here, begins to engage with the logic.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Identify the three conditions listed: sleep, weak intellect (as with the insane), and perfect soul/strong intellect (as with prophets and saints).
The objector uses these to argue that if none apply, certainty is possible.
2
Understand the claim
The objector claims that if none of these conditions apply and the person has a balanced temperament, then certainty about external existence is achieved.
This is the core of their argument: normal perception is reliable.
3
Examine the beginning of the reply
The reply begins by saying that according to the objector's own path, it appears that the person does not sense... (the text cuts off). Consider how this might challenge the objector's conclusion.
The reply likely argues that the objector's conditions actually lead to doubt rather than certainty.

Source Text

فإن قالوا : إن حصول هذه الحالة مشروطة بحصول أحوال منها : أن يكون الإنسان نائماً . ومنها : أن يكون ضعيف العقل ، كما في حق المجانين . ومنها : أن يكون كامل النفس ، قوي العقل . كما في حق الأنبياء والأولياء ، فإذا لم [ يحصل شيء من هذه الأحوال . وكان الإنسان باقياً على مقتضى المزاج المعتدل ، لم يحصل شيء من هذه الأحوال ، فحينئذ ] يحصل القطع بوجود هذه الأشياء في الخارج فنقول في الجواب : إن بالطريق الذي ذكرتم ، ظهر أنه لا يحس الإنسان
For if they say: 'Verily, the occurrence of this state is conditioned by the occurrence of conditions: among them that the person be asleep; and among them that he be weak in intellect, as is the case with the insane; and among them that he be perfect in soul, strong in intellect, as is the case with prophets and saints. So if none of these conditions occur, and the person remains upon the requirement of the balanced temperament, none of these conditions occur, then at that time certainty occurs about the existence of these things externally.' Then we say in reply: 'Verily, by the path you have mentioned, it appears that the person does not sense...'
Source pages p. 135

Commentary
This passage presents a second objection and the beginning of its reply. The objector argues that the perception of unseen images is conditional upon specific states: sleep, weak intellect (as with the insane), or perfect soul and strong intellect (as with prophets and saints). If none of these conditions are present and the person has a balanced temperament, then one can be certain that the things perceived are externally existent. Thus, they claim that ordinary waking perception under normal conditions is reliable. The reply begins by stating that according to the path the objector has mentioned, it appears that the person does not sense... and is cut off. The argument likely continues to show that even with these conditions, the possibility of illusion remains, or that the conditions themselves are problematic. The lesson is incomplete as the full reply is not available in this window.

Checkpoint
According to the objection, under what condition can we be certain that perceived images are externally existent?
A) When the person is dreaming
B) When the person is insane
C) When the person is a prophet
D) When none of the special conditions apply and the person has a balanced temperament

Key Takeaway
The objection asserts that absence of special conditions ensures external reality, but the reply begins to challenge that very path.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever assumed your ordinary waking perception is always accurate? What does this passage warn you about trusting your senses when it comes to unseen realities? Are you willing to reconsider moments when you dismissed spiritual experiences as mere imagination?
?
The objector says only prophets and saints can perceive the unseen under certain conditions. Do you truly believe that, or have you in your heart denied the possibility of divine communication today? What would it mean for your life if you accepted that angels might speak to ordinary believers?
?
This passage challenges the assumption that a balanced temperament leads to certainty. When you feel most 'balanced' and 'reasonable', are you more or less open to the unseen? Could your intellectual pride be blinding you to realities that only a humble soul perceives?

Philosophers on Miracles: Psychic Power or Arbitrary Choice?
Author narrates and critiques the philosophers' explanation of miracles and karamat.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers' explanation of miracles through the estimative faculty and why the author considers their exclusive attribution to psychic power as an arbitrary preference among multiple possible explanations.

Core Idea
Explaining miracles by psychic power alone is just one possibility, not a proven fact.

The author presents the philosophers' claim that miracles and karamat are produced by a powerful estimative faculty in the soul. He then critiques this view by pointing out that other causes—angels, jinn, celestial connections, or stars—are equally possible. Thus, singling out the psychic explanation is an arbitrary preference without sufficient grounds.

Guided Path
1
Read the Philosophers' Claim
Read the passage from the beginning through 'How he wishes and desires.' Identify the philosophers' core argument: that the estimative faculty can affect bodies, and a perfect soul can dispose of the world's matter.
Note the reference to their book 'The Soul' and the concept of 'estimative faculty' (القوة الوهمية).
2
Examine the Supporting Analogy
Read the next part where they support their claim by noting that weak souls, when gathered in large numbers (e.g., prayer for rain), can produce effects. Understand this as an analogy: if weak souls can have some effect, a strong soul can perform wonders.
The analogy is from collective intention to individual psychic power.
3
Understand the Critique
Read the author's response: while the psychic explanation is possible, so are others (angels, jinn, celestial connections, etc.). Therefore, the philosophers' exclusive attribution is a preference without a preferrer (ترجيح من غير مرجح).
The author does not deny the possibility, only the exclusivity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل السابع في حكاية قول الفلاسفة في السبب الذي لأجله يقدر الأنبياء والأولياء على الاتيان بالمعجزات والكرامات . حاصل كلامهم فيه : أنا بينا في كتاب ( النفس ) : أن القوة الوهمية التي للإنسان ، قد تكون قادرة على التأثير في الأجسام. وذكرنا الوجوه الكثيرة في تقرير هذا الباب . وعند هذا قالوا : لا يمتنع وجود إنسان تكون نفسه كاملة في هذه القوة ، فلا جرم يقدر على التصرف في هيولى هذا العالم كيف شاء وأراد . ولما يقوي ذلك ؛ أن النفوس الضعيفة إذا اجتمعت فقد يحصل لها نوع من القوة المؤثرة . مثل : الجمع العظيم إذا اجتمعوا على توجيه الفكر إلى شيء معين ، ومثل ما يشاهد في صلاة الاستسقاء وغيرها . وإذا كان هذا محسوسا لم يمتنع كون النفس القوية قادرة على الإتيان بهذه الغرائب والبدائع . واعلم أن حاصل هذا الكلام : أن تلك النفس موصوفة بخاصية ، لأجلها قدرت على الإتيان بهذه المعجزات والكرامات. وكما أن هذا محتمل ، فكذلك سائر الوجوه محتملة . مثل هذه الأحوال إلى الملائكة أو الجن ، أو الاتصالات الفلكية ، أو أفعال الكواكب ، التي هي أحياء ناطقة أو العقول والنفوس . وإذا كان الكل محتملاً ، كان جزمهم بإسناد هذه المعجزات إلى القوة النفسانية فقط . ترجيحاً من غير مرجح . وهذا آخر الكلام في هذا الباب . ويتلوه الكلام في السحر . ليحصل الفرق بين المعجز ، والسحر ، والنبي ، والساحر [ والله اعلم ]
Chapter Seven: Narrating the statement of the philosophers concerning the reason why prophets and saints are able to bring miracles and karamat. The summary of their speech in it: We have explained in the book 'The Soul' that the estimative faculty that a human has may be capable of affecting bodies. And we mentioned many aspects in establishing this chapter. And upon this they said: It is not impossible that there exists a human whose soul is perfect in this power, so without doubt he is able to dispose of the matter of this world however he wishes and desires. And what strengthens that is that weak souls when they gather, they may acquire some type of effective power. Such as a great multitude when they gather to direct thought toward a specific thing, and like what is witnessed in the prayer for rain and other things. And if this is sensible, it is not impossible that the strong soul is capable of producing these wonders and marvels. And know that the gist of this speech is that that soul is described by a property, because of which it is able to produce these miracles and karamat. And just as this is possible, so other aspects are possible. Such as attributing these states to angels or jinn, or celestial connections, or the actions of stars which are rational living beings, or intellects and souls. And if all are possible, then their certainty in attributing these miracles only to the psychic power is a preference without a preferrer. And this is the end of the speech in this chapter. And next follows the speech on sorcery, so that the difference between the miracle, sorcery, the prophet, and the sorcerer may be attained. And God knows best.
Source pages p. 138

Commentary
This is a new chapter where the author recounts the philosophers' explanation for miracles and karamat of prophets and saints. He summarizes their view: based on their book on the soul, they argue that the human estimative faculty (قوة وهمية) can affect bodies. They posit that a person with a perfect soul in this power can manipulate the matter of the world. To support this, they note that even weak souls, when gathered in large numbers (e.g., in prayer for rain), can produce an effect. Therefore, a strong soul can produce extraordinary wonders. The author then critiques this position: while this psychic explanation is possible, other explanations are equally possible—such as attributing miracles to angels, jinn, celestial connections, stars (as rational beings), or intellects and souls. Since all are possible, the philosophers' exclusive attribution of miracles to psychic power is an arbitrary preference without grounds. The author closes by noting that the next topic will be sorcery, to distinguish miracle from sorcery and prophet from sorcerer.

Key Terms
القوة الوهمية
المعجزات
الكرامات
الفلاسفة
السحر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The philosophers' view that miracles come only from psychic power is definitively false.
Correction: The author only calls it arbitrary, not false; he acknowledges it as one possible explanation among many.

Checkpoint
Why does the author consider the philosophers' attribution of miracles solely to psychic power as arbitrary?
A) Because it contradicts revelation
B) Because other explanations (angels, jinn, etc.) are equally possible
C) Because the philosophers admitted their theory was weak
D) Because miracles are impossible

Key Takeaway
The philosophers' exclusive psychic explanation of miracles is arbitrary because many other causes are equally possible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The author shows that the philosophers leaped to an exclusive conclusion without sufficient reason. Think of a belief you hold as absolute truth—can you honestly say you have ruled out all alternative explanations? If not, are you guilty of the same 'preference without a preferrer'? What would it take for you to genuinely examine those alternatives?
?
If miracles could come from angels, jinn, psychic power, or celestial forces, why do you assume your understanding of how God works is the only valid one? Does this lesson challenge your certainty? How will you respond when you encounter an interpretation that contradicts your own?
?
The philosophers' error was not in their explanation being false, but in claiming it exclusively. Where in your daily spiritual life do you treat one permissible practice or belief as the only correct way? Are you willing to accept that other paths may be equally valid in God's sight? What would change if you did?
